Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
5 min
The Cooperative Purpose of Munazara
This passage defines the purpose and cooperative nature of jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the purpose of munazara as cooperative truth-seeking and distinguish it from combative debate.

Core Idea
The debater is a partner, not an enemy.

The passage then emphasizes that the dialectical process can only succeed if both parties share the motive of seeking truth. This lesson explores that cooperative aim, quoting a definition of munazara as mutual enlightenment and al-Ghazali's description of jadal as 'cooperation in investigation'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the essential condition
Find the condition for jadal to achieve its goal: the motive must be the pursuit of truth, and this motive must be present in both parties simultaneously. Without that, the process fails.
This condition distinguishes Islamic jadal from secular debates aimed at victory.
2
Analyze the definition of munazara
The passage defines munazara as 'cooperation of the two debating parties to know the truth and arrive at it by each making his companion see the dark spots.' This implies mutual enlightenment, not one-sided instruction.
Even if one party knows the truth, the aim is still to enlighten the other, using gentle guidance, not force.
3
Reflect on al-Ghazali's metaphor
Al-Ghazali calls jadal 'a cooperation in investigation and a fencing with the weapons of thoughts and intellect.' Consider how fencing involves a partner who helps you improve; it is not a fight to the death.
The metaphor emphasizes skill, mutual benefit, and intellectual rigor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو ذاته التحذير الذي صدر عن الإمام الزركشي حين قال : إياك أن تستصغر خصمًا، فإن استصغرته فالوجه ألا تكلمه، فلربما هجم من استصغاره الانقطاع لقلة التحفظ منه والاهتمام به. فالعلاقة التي ينبغي أن تحكم الحوار بين المتجادلين هي علاقة الأدب الإسلامي المتكامل، الذي يقتضي أن يسود الاحترام والتسامح والتجاوز عن الهفوات غير المضرة بالحق. قال الطوفي: «وليلن كل منهما لخصمه الكلام ولا يغلظ عليه، وليتلق ما يصدر عنه بقبول و لطف ،وتحسين مثل أن يقول : ما ذكرته حسن متجه، لكن يرد عليه كذا أو يعارضه كذا. ويحلم ويتطلب هذا ؛ أنه إذا أخطأ أحدهما في حق الأخر، وجب عليه أن يصبر عليه معه ولا يعامله بالمثل، ويتحرى البعد عن المشاغبات والمماحكات التي لا تنفع سير عملية الجدل ولا تضيف إلى البحث العلمي أي نتيجة علمية جديدة، قال ابن عقيل: من إذا كان الصبر على شعب السائل في الجدل فضيلة، والحلم عن بادرة إن كانت منه ،رفعة فينبغي لمن أحب اكتساب الفضائل أن يستعمل ذلك بحسب علمه بما له فيه من الحفظ الجزيل والمحل الجليل وليس ينقصه الحلم إلا عند جاهل ولا يضع منه الصبر على شنب السائل إلا عند غبي يعتقد أن ذلك الذل والركاكة وانخساس النفس وقصور اللسان في الشغب هو الفضل، فإن من خاض فيه تعوده، ومن تعوده حرم الإصابة واستروح إليه ومن عرف بذلك سقط سقوط الذرة، ومن صبر على ذلك وحلم عنه ارتفع في نفوس العلماء، ونبل عند أهل الجدل، وبانت منه القوة على نفسه حيث منعها المقابلة على الجفاء بمثله، والقوة على خصمه حيث أحوجه إلى الشغب، لا سيما إذا ظهر منه أنه فعل ذلك حرصا على الإرشاد إلى الحق ومحبة الاستنقاذ من الباطل الذي أثارته الشبهة من الضلال المؤدي بصاحبه إلى العطب والهلاك فله بهذه النية الجميلة الثواب من ربه والمدحة من كل منصف حضره أو سمع به 2 - الرغبة المشتركة في تبادل الرأي والتعاون لإظهار الحق: لا يمكن أن تؤدي العملية الجدلية مهمتها أو تحقق هدفها إلا إذا كان الدافع إليها هو طلب الحق والرغبة في الوصول إليه، وهذا الدافع ينبغي أن يتوفر لدى طرفي الجدل في آن واحد بحيث ينطلق كل منهما من شعور بالتعاون مع الآخر لأجل الوصول إلى الحق في المسألة محل النزاع والجدل فلا بد من وجود رغبة لدى المتحاورين في تبادل الآراء من أجل الاقتناع والإقناع ولا توجد هذه الرغبة إلا عند وجود الحافز إليها، الذي لا بد أن يكون حافزا مطلقا يدفع إلى طلب المعرفة من أجل المعرفة. فإن «هدف المناظرة هو في الأصل تعاون الفريقين المتناظرين على معرفة الحقيقة والتوصل إليها بتبصير كل منهما صاحبه بالأماكن المظلمة عليه، والتي خفيت عنه حينما أخذ ينظر باحثًا عن الحقيقة، وذلك حينما لا يكون أحدهما واقفًا على الحقيقة المبينة وقوفًا قطعيًا غير قابل للنقض، أما في هذه الحالة فإن هدف المناظرة إنما هو تبصير الواقف على الحقيقة أخاه المناظر له بها، والأخذ بيده في طرق الاستدلال الصحيح لإبلاغه وجه الحق المشرق، وذلك باستخدام الحوار البريء من التعصب الخالي من العنف والانفعال المتمشي وفق الأصول العامة للحوار الذي يهدف فيه كل من الفريقين المتحاورين أن يصل إلى الحقيقة، كأنه جاهل بها» قال الغزالي رحمه الله : الجدال معاونة على النظر، ومصاولة بأسلحة الخواطر والفكر
It is the same warning issued by Imam al-Zarkashi when he said: Beware of belittling an opponent, for if you belittle him, the proper course is not to speak to him, because perhaps from belittling him comes defeat due to lack of caution and concern for him. The relationship that should govern the dialogue between the disputants is that of complete Islamic etiquette, which requires that respect, tolerance, and overlooking of slips that do not harm the truth prevail. Al-Tufi said: 'Let each of them soften his speech to his opponent and not be harsh with him, and let him receive what comes from him with acceptance and gentleness, and improvement, such as saying: What you mentioned is good and valid, but it is refuted by such-and-such or opposed by such-and-such.' And let him forbear. And this requires that if one of them errs against the other's right, he must be patient with him and not retaliate in kind, and avoid quarrels and squabbles that do not benefit the conduct of the debate and do not add any new scientific result to academic research. Ibn 'Aqil said: 'If patience with the opponent's acrimony in debate is a virtue, and forbearance if an outburst comes from him is an elevation, then whoever loves to acquire virtues should practice that according to his knowledge of the great reward and sublime station he has in it. Forbearance only diminishes him in the eyes of an ignorant person, and patience with the opponent's acrimony only lowers him in the eyes of a stupid person who thinks that such humility, weakness, lowness of soul, and shortcoming of tongue in wrangling is merit. He who engages in it becomes accustomed to it, and he who becomes accustomed to it is deprived of correctness and delights in it, and he who is known for that falls like a mote, and he who practices patience and forbearance rises in the eyes of scholars and is noble among the people of debate. His strength over his soul becomes apparent as he prevents it from responding to harshness with its like, and his strength over his opponent becomes apparent as he compels him to resort to wrangling, especially if it appears that he did that out of eagerness to guide to the truth and love of rescuing from falsehood that doubt has stirred from error leading its possessor to ruin and destruction. So by this beautiful intention he has reward from his Lord and praise from every just person who attended or heard of him.' 2 - The shared desire for exchange of opinion and cooperation to manifest the truth: The dialectical process cannot fulfill its task or achieve its goal unless the motive for it is the pursuit of truth and the desire to attain it. This motive should be present in both parties of the debate simultaneously, so that each proceeds from a feeling of cooperation with the other in order to reach the truth in the matter under dispute and debate. Therefore, there must be a desire among the interlocutors to exchange views for the sake of conviction and persuasion. This desire only exists when there is an incentive for it, which must be an absolute incentive that drives the seeking of knowledge for its own sake. Indeed, 'the aim of munazara is originally the cooperation of the two debating parties to know the truth and arrive at it by each making his companion see the dark spots that were hidden from him when he began to look searching for the truth, when neither of them has definitive, irrefutable knowledge of the apparent truth. In this case, the aim of munazara is to make the one who knows the truth enlighten his debating brother about it, and to take him by the hand in the paths of correct inference to convey to him the bright face of truth, using dialogue free from fanaticism, devoid of violence and emotion, proceeding according to the general principles of dialogue in which each of the two dialoguing parties aims to reach the truth as if he were ignorant of it.' Al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Dialectical debate (jadal) is a cooperation in investigation and a fencing with the weapons of thoughts and intellect.'
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the ethics and purpose of dialectical debate (jadal) from an Islamic perspective. It begins by quoting Imam al-Zarkashi's warning against belittling an opponent, because underestimating them can lead to defeat due to negligence. The relationship between disputants should be governed by complete Islamic etiquette, emphasizing respect, tolerance, and overlooking minor mistakes. The passage then cites al-Tufi, advising that each debater should speak gently, accept the opponent's words with kindness, and respond gracefully, e.g., praising the valid aspect while pointing out what refutes or opposes it. The debater should be forbearing. Then Ibn 'Aqil elaborates: patience with the opponent's acrimony is a virtue, and forbearance raises one's station. Forbearance only lowers one in the eyes of the ignorant; patience only seems like weakness to the foolish. Those who engage in harsh wrangling become accustomed to it, lose correctness, and fall. Those who are patient and forbearing rise in the eyes of scholars and are noble among the people of debate. They demonstrate strength over themselves and over their opponent, especially when their motive is sincere desire to guide to truth and rescue from falsehood. Such a person attains reward and praise. The second section (numbered 2) discusses the shared desire for exchange of opinion and cooperation to manifest truth. The dialectical process cannot achieve its goal unless the motive is the pursuit of truth, and this motive must be shared by both parties. Each must feel they are cooperating to reach the truth. The incentive must be absolute — seeking knowledge for its own sake. The passage then quotes a definition of munazara: it is originally a cooperation to know the truth, with each helping the other see what is hidden. If one party already has definitive knowledge, the goal is still to enlighten the other, using dialogue free from fanaticism and emotion. Both act as if ignorant, seeking truth together. Finally, al-Ghazali is quoted: 'Dialectical debate is a cooperation in investigation and a fencing with the weapons of thoughts and intellect.' The entire passage emphasizes that jadal is not about winning but about mutual discovery of truth, requiring humility, patience, and sincere cooperation.

المناظرة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must be the shared motive of both parties in jadal?
A) To win the debate and gain prestige
B) To seek truth and arrive at it cooperatively
C) To defend one's school of thought at all costs
D) To impress the audience with rhetorical skill

Key Takeaway
The purpose of munazara is cooperative truth-seeking, where each party helps the other uncover obscured aspects, using dialogue free from fanaticism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a hypothetical debate between two scholars on the issue of free will (qadr), write a dialogue that exemplifies the cooperative purpose of munazara, ensuring both parties seek truth together.
?
Identify a contemporary Islamic discussion (e.g., the ruling on cryptocurrency) and explain how using the cooperative purpose of munazara could lead to a more productive scholarly exchange.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
10 min
Prerequisites for Fruitful Jadal: Intention and Cooperation
Presents ethical prerequisites for fruitful dialectic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two essential prerequisites for productive jadal and evaluate an intention's sincerity using the criteria from the passage.

Core Idea
Jadal is cooperative truth-seeking, not competitive debate.

The author of this passage lays the ethical foundation for dialectic. Before any logical moves, the intention of the participants must be pure. The passage insists that without a shared desire for truth, jadal becomes a hollow exercise. This lesson unpacks the two prerequisites: sincere intention (niyya) and mutual cooperation for truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two prerequisites
Read the first two sections of the passage. Notice that the author first emphasizes intention, then the common desire for exchange of opinions. These are the two conditions for productive jadal.
2
Analyze the role of intention
The first paragraph states that even a misguided argument, if accompanied by sincere intention, brings reward. Consider how intention transforms the moral value of the dialectical act.
Intention (niyya) in jadal is parallel to intention in worship; both require sincerity towards Allah.
3
Examine the requirement of mutual desire for truth
The author argues that both parties must be motivated by the pursuit of truth, not personal gain. The goal is cooperation to reveal what is obscure, as al-Ghazali describes.
This is contrasted with a scenario where one already has definitive knowledge; in that case, the goal becomes teaching.
4
Compare the three intentions of Ibn Aqeel
Ibn Aqeel lists three intentions: supporting truth, training for ijtihad, and dominance. Compare them and note which are praiseworthy and which are condemned.
The third intention (dominance) is to be avoided; it corrupts the purpose of jadal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضلال المؤدي بصاحبه إلى العطب والهلاك فله بهذه النية الجميلة الثواب من ربه والمدحة من كل منصف حضره أو سمع به

2 - الرغبة المشتركة في تبادل الرأي والتعاون لإظهار الحق:

لا يمكن أن تؤدي العملية الجدلية مهمتها أو تحقق هدفها إلا إذا كان الدافع إليها هو طلب الحق والرغبة في الوصول إليه، وهذا الدافع ينبغي أن يتوفر لدى طرفي الجدل في آن واحد بحيث ينطلق كل منهما من شعور بالتعاون مع الآخر لأجل الوصول إلى الحق في المسألة محل النزاع والجدل فلا بد من وجود رغبة لدى المتحاورين في تبادل الآراء من أجل الاقتناع والإقناع ولا توجد هذه الرغبة إلا عند وجود الحافز إليها، الذي لا بد أن يكون حافزا مطلقا يدفع إلى طلب المعرفة من أجل المعرفة .

فإن «هدف المناظرة هو في الأصل تعاون الفريقين المتناظرين على معرفة الحقيقة والتوصل إليها بتبصير كل منهما صاحبه بالأماكن المظلمة عليه، والتي خفيت عنه حينما أخذ ينظر باحثًا عن الحقيقة، وذلك حينما لا يكون أحدهما واقفًا على الحقيقة المبينة وقوفًا قطعيًا غير قابل للنقض، أما في هذه الحالة فإن هدف المناظرة إنما هو تبصير الواقف على الحقيقة أخاه المناظر له بها، والأخذ بيده في طرق الاستدلال الصحيح لإبلاغه وجه الحق المشرق، وذلك باستخدام الحوار البريء من التعصب الخالي من العنف والانفعال المتمشي وفق الأصول العامة للحوار الذي يهدف فيه كل من الفريقين المتحاورين أن يصل إلى الحقيقة، كأنه جاهل بها» قال الغزالي رحمه الله :

الجدال معاونة على النظر، ومصاولة بأسلحة الخواطر والفكر

وقال أيضا : الجدل» موضوع لتنقيح الخواطر وامتحانها بالتدوار على درجات الفكر، ولإفحام الخصم وقطعه بالإلزامات، ولذلك أجمعوا على قبول التعلق بمناقضات الخضم وتعلق فريق بالتركيبات - وهم الأكثرون - ولم يجوزوا للمعترض أن يمنع النقض ويدل عليه إلى غير ذلك من أمور لا تخفى، فوجب - على الضرورة - رعاية مصلحة الجدال» .

ويؤكد ابن عقيل أنه على المتجادلين أن يجعلا قصدهما أحد أمرين، ويجتهدا في اجتناب الثالث .

فأعلى الثلاثة من المقاصد نصرة الحق ببيان الحجة ودحض الباطل بإبطال : الشبهة، لتكون كلمة الله هي العليا والثاني : الإدمان للتقوي على الاجتهاد، والاجتهاد من مراتب الدين المحمودة، وهي رتبة الفتيا. فالأولى كالجهاد والثانية كالمناضلة التي يُقصد بها التقوي على الجهاد. الثالث ، وهو المغالبة وبيان الفراهة على الخصم والترجح عليه ونعوذ بالله من الطريقة .
The misguidance that leads its companion to destruction and perdition, so for him with this beautiful intention there is reward from his Lord and praise from every just person who witnessed or heard about it.

2 - The common desire for exchange of opinions and cooperation to manifest the truth:

The dialectical process cannot fulfill its task or achieve its goal unless the motive behind it is the pursuit of truth and the desire to reach it. This motive should be present in both parties of the dialectic at the same time, so that each proceeds from a feeling of cooperation with the other to reach the truth in the matter under dispute and dialectic. There must be a desire among the interlocutors to exchange opinions for the sake of conviction and persuasion, and this desire does not exist except when there is an incentive towards it, which must be an absolute incentive that drives the pursuit of knowledge for its own sake.

Indeed, 'the goal of the discussion (munazara) is originally the cooperation of the two debating parties in knowing the truth and arriving at it by each making clear to his companion the obscure places that were hidden from him when he began to investigate seeking the truth, when neither of them has a definitive, irrefutable knowledge of the manifest truth. In this case, the goal of the discussion is only to enlighten the one who has the truth to his debating brother, and to take him by the hand in the paths of correct reasoning to convey to him the bright face of truth, using innocent dialogue free from fanaticism, devoid of violence and emotion, proceeding according to the general principles of dialogue in which each of the two debating parties aims to reach the truth as if he were ignorant of it.' Al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on him, said:

Dialectic is assistance in reflection and a duel with the weapons of thoughts and intellect.

He also said: 'Dialectic is a subject for refining thoughts and testing them by circulating through the degrees of thought, and for silencing the opponent and cutting him off with obligations. Therefore, they unanimously accepted the adherence to the contradictions of the opponent, and one party adhered to the combinations—and they are the majority—and they did not allow the objector to prevent the criticism and indicate otherwise, among other matters that are not hidden. So it is necessary—by necessity—to observe the interest of dialectic.'

And Ibn Aqeel emphasizes that the two debaters should make their intention one of two things, and strive to avoid the third.

The highest of the three intentions is supporting the truth by presenting the proof and refuting falsehood by invalidating the doubt, so that the word of Allah is supreme. The second is practice to strengthen oneself for ijtihad, and ijtihad is one of the praised ranks of the religion, and it is the rank of fatwa. The first is like jihad, and the second is like target practice that aims to strengthen for jihad. The third is overcoming, showing superiority over the opponent, and gaining precedence over him in the method—and we seek refuge in Allah from this method.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage describes the necessary conditions for proper jadal (dialectic). It begins by emphasizing the importance of intention (niyya): even if a person errs in dialectic, if his intention is sincere in seeking truth, he is rewarded. The author then proceeds to the second condition: a common desire for exchange of opinions and cooperation between the two parties. He argues that the dialectical process cannot achieve its goal unless both parties are motivated purely by the pursuit of truth, not by personal gain. He quotes al-Ghazali to define the goal of munazara (discussion) as mutual cooperation to reveal truth, where each party helps the other see what was hidden. Al-Ghazali further describes jadal as a cooperative aid to reflection, not a hostile altercation. Then the author cites Ibn Aqeel, who classifies the intentions of debaters into three: first, supporting truth; second, training for ijtihad; third, gaining dominance—the third being condemned. The passage underscores that the highest aim is to elevate the word of Allah, while the lowest is mere competition.

Key Terms
نية
جدل
مناظرة

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Aqeel, what is the highest intention for engaging in jadal?
A) To train for ijtihad
B) To support the truth and refute falsehood
C) To gain superiority over the opponent
D) To practice rhetorical skill

Key Takeaway
The success of jadal depends on the purity of intention and a shared goal of truth, not personal victory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a recent disagreement you had about a religious issue. Using Ibn Aqeel's three intentions, classify the intention you and the other person had. Could you identify any elements of the third intention (dominance)? Write a brief analysis.
?
Imagine you are to debate a fellow student on the ruling of riba in contemporary banking. Draft a short statement of your intention before the debate, ensuring it aligns with the cooperative truth-seeking model described in the passage.

Intellectual Freedom as a Condition for Valid Jadal
Author argues that intellectual freedom is essential for both parties in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition of intellectual freedom in jadal and explain why it is necessary for reaching truth.

Core Idea
Intellectual freedom is essential; without it, dialectic collapses into intimidation and cannot yield conviction.

This passage presents the third requirement for valid dialectic: both parties must enjoy intellectual freedom. The author explains that jadal is a reciprocal expression of beliefs, and for it to achieve its goal of truth, each party must be able to express their views freely and transparently, without personal attacks. Fear and intimidation undermine the search for truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being proposed
Find the Arabic phrase 'توفر الحرية الفكرية لكلا الطرفين' and note that it is presented as a requirement for valid jadal.
The phrase 'الحرية الفكرية' is a modern term, but the concept is rooted in adab al-bahth.
2
Understand the nature of jadal
The passage describes jadal as a dialogue of ideas (حوار أفكار) and reciprocal expression of inner beliefs. Why does this require freedom?
Jadal is not about imposing views but exchanging concepts.
3
Analyze the consequence of absence
The passage describes intellectual terrorism (إرهاب فكري) and loss of self-confidence. What happens to the dialectic when one party feels crushed by the other's perceived greatness?
The result is that the intimidated party cannot genuinely engage.
4
Apply the condition to a real dialectical scenario
Think of a theological debate (e.g., on divine attributes) where one party feels intellectually inferior. Does the condition hold? How would the debate be affected?
Consider the role of the munazara tradition in ensuring fairness.

3 - توفر الحرية الفكرية لكلا الطرفين الجدل أساسا هو حوار أفكار وتداول مفاهيم وهو تعبير متقابل عما يعتقده كل طرف في ضميره في شأن المسألة موضع الجدل، ولذلك لا يمكن لهذا الجدل أن يحقق هدفه إلا إذا أتيح لكل من الطرفين أن يقول ما يريد وما يعتقد بكل

الحاسمة

حرية وشفافية ما دام يعبر عن رأيه ولا يقدح في شخص أو هيئة غيره . فإنه من الطبيعي للأي حوار يدور بين اثنين، لينتهي – في هدفه – إلى النتيجة من الإيمان العميق المنفتح بنتائج الحوار أن يحقق شرطا أساسيا ؛ أن يملك كل من الطرفين حرية الحركة الفكرية التي يملك معها الثقة بشخصيته الفكرية المستقلة، فلا يكون واقعا تحت رحمة الإرهاب الفكري والنفسي الذي بالانسحاق أمام شخصية الآخر لما يحس فيه بأعماقه بالعظمة الكبيرة والمطلقة التي يملكها الآخر، فتتضاءل - إزاء ذلك – ثقته بنفسه، وبالتالي
3 - The provision of intellectual freedom for both parties. Dialectic is fundamentally a dialogue of ideas and an exchange of concepts, and it is a reciprocal expression of what each party believes in his conscience regarding the matter under dispute. Therefore, this dialectic cannot achieve its goal unless each of the two parties is enabled to say what he wants and what he believes with complete freedom and transparency, as long as he expresses his opinion and does not attack another person or entity. For it is natural for any dialogue between two people that aims—in its goal—to reach a result of deep, open conviction in the results of the dialogue, to fulfill a basic condition: that each of the two parties possesses intellectual freedom of movement, with which he possesses confidence in his independent intellectual personality, so that he is not under the mercy of intellectual and psychological terrorism that causes him to be crushed before the other's personality, due to what he deeply feels of the great and absolute greatness that the other possesses. Thus his self-confidence diminishes in response, and consequently...
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents the third requirement for valid dialectic: intellectual freedom for both parties. The author states that jadal is fundamentally a dialogue of ideas and exchange of concepts, a reciprocal expression of each party's inner beliefs. For this dialogue to achieve its goal of reaching truth, each party must be allowed to say what they want and believe with complete freedom and transparency, provided they do not attack persons or institutions. The author explains that any genuine dialogue aiming at sincere conviction must ensure that both parties have intellectual freedom of movement, enabling them to maintain confidence in their independent intellectual identity. Without such freedom, a party may fall under intellectual and psychological terrorism, feeling crushed by the perceived absolute greatness of the other, leading to loss of self-confidence. The passage implies that fear and intimidation undermine the search for truth.

Key Terms
حرية فكرية
إرهاب فكري

A) That one party dominates

C) That the dialogue is conducted in private
D) That the parties agree beforehand

Key Takeaway
For jadal to be a genuine search for truth, both parties must be free from fear and intimidation, enabling them to express their beliefs transparently.

?

The Aims of Jadal and the Need for Intellectual Freedom
Author discusses the purposes of jadal and the necessity of intellectual freedom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three aims of jadal as classified by Ibn 'Aqil and explain why intellectual freedom is a necessary condition for productive dialectic.

Core Idea
Jadal's highest purpose is truth, not victory; intellectual freedom is its lifeblood.

This lesson explores the deeper purposes of dialectic (jadal) in the Islamic tradition. While jadal can be used for victory, classical scholars like Ibn 'Aqil elevate the pursuit of truth and the training for ijtihad. The lesson also highlights the indispensable condition of intellectual freedom, without which dialectic degenerates into oppression.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three aims of jadal according to Ibn 'Aqil
Locate the passage where Ibn 'Aqil is quoted. He lists three purposes: the highest is supporting truth by presenting proof and refuting falsehood; the second is practice to strengthen ijtihad; the third is mere victory and showing superiority. Note the ranking.
Ibn 'Aqil urges disputants to choose one of the first two and avoid the third.
2
Understand the analogies used
Ibn 'Aqil compares the first aim to jihad (struggle) and the second to marksmanship practice that strengthens for jihad. This analogy indicates that truth-seeking is the primary goal, while practice is preparatory and commendable.
Victory-seeking is not analogized to anything praiseworthy.
3
Analyze the condition of intellectual freedom
The author argues that jadal cannot achieve its goal unless each party can express beliefs freely and without fear. The absence of freedom leads to psychological intimidation, loss of confidence, and the researcher becoming a mere echo of the opponent.
The passage quotes Ibn Hazm on prizing truth over praise.
4
Reflect on the scholar's warning
Ibn 'Aqil warns that oppressing the opponent in debate corrupts the dialectic, breaks the spirit, and kills thought. He advises guarding against such oppression and ensuring parity between disputants.
The author notes that usul scholars historically maintained parity with opponents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أيضا : الجدل موضوع لتنقيح الخواطر وامتحانها بالتدوار على درجات الفكر، ولإفحام الخصم وقطعه بالإلزامات، ولذلك أجمعوا على قبول التعلق بمناقضات الخضم وتعلق فريق بالتركيبات - وهم الأكثرون - ولم يجوزوا للمعترض أن يمنع النقض ويدل عليه إلى غير ذلك من أمور لا تخفى، فوجب - على الضرورة - رعاية مصلحة الجدال. ويؤكد ابن عقيل أنه على المتجادلين أن يجعلا قصدهما أحد أمرين، ويجتهدا في اجتناب الثالث. فأعلى الثلاثة من المقاصد نصرة الحق ببيان الحجة ودحض الباطل بإبطال الشبهة، لتكون كلمة الله هي العليا. والثاني : الإدمان للتقوي على الاجتهاد، والاجتهاد من مراتب الدين المحمودة، وهي رتبة الفتيا. فالأولى كالجهاد والثانية كالمناضلة التي يُقصد بها التقوي على الجهاد. الثالث، وهو المغالبة وبيان الفراهة على الخصم والترجح عليه، ونعوذ بالله من هذه الطريقة. 3 - توفر الحرية الفكرية لكلا الطرفين. الجدل أساسا هو حوار أفكار وتداول مفاهيم وهو تعبير متقابل عما يعتقده كل طرف في ضميره في شأن المسألة موضع الجدل، ولذلك لا يمكن لهذا الجدل أن يحقق هدفه إلا إذا أتيح لكل من الطرفين أن يقول ما يريد وما يعتقد بكل حرية وشفافية ما دام يعبر عن رأيه ولا يقدح في شخص أو هيئة غيره. فإنه من الطبيعي لأي حوار يدور بين اثنين، لينتهي – في هدفه – إلى النتيجة من الإيمان العميق المنفتح بنتائج الحوار أن يحقق شرطا أساسيا؛ أن يملك كل من الطرفين حرية الحركة الفكرية التي يملك معها الثقة بشخصيته الفكرية المستقلة، فلا يكون واقعا تحت رحمة الإرهاب الفكري والنفسي الذي بالانسحاق أمام شخصية الآخر لما يحس فيه بأعماقه بالعظمة الكبيرة والمطلقة التي يملكها الآخر، فتتضاءل - إزاء ذلك – ثقته بنفسه، وبالتالي ثقته بفكره وبقابليته لأن يكون طرفا للحوار، فيتجمد عند ذلك ويفقد قدرته على الحركة الفكرية، فيتحول إلى صدى للأفكار التي يتلقاها من الآخر. ويقول المثل الشائع : الحقيقة بنت البحث، ولا يكون البحث النافع إلا على قاعدة حرية التفكير والتعبير، ولله در الإمام ابن حزم حيث يقول : من حقق النظر وراض نفسه على الحقائق وإن آلمتها في أول صدمة كان اغتباطه بذم الناس إياه أشد وأعظم من اغتباطه بمدحهم إياه، لذلك كان للمجادل أن يقول كل ما يجول بخاطره في شأن ما يبحث، وتعين على مناظره أن يصغي بتفهم كل ما يُسرد أمامه على بساط البحث من غير تأفف أو ضجر، ولو كان ما يقال مخالفا لرأيه و اعتقاده، إذ طالما سمعنا كلاما خلناه في أول الأمر خطأ أو وهما أو سهوا، ولكننا بعد التريث والبحث والاستقصاء ألفيناه الصواب بعينه وأننا المخطئون. فغياب الحرية الفكرية وتعدي طرف على آخر أو حجره عليه، من شأنه أن يحرم موضوع البحث من الثراء والتوسع ويدفع الباحث إلى التخلي عن الموضوع والبعد عن التأمل فيه. لذلك يحذر الإمام ابن عقيل قائلا : كل جدل يقع فيه ظلم الخصم فإنه يختل بحسب قوة ذلك وضعفه وذلك أن ظلم الخصم يكسر من نفسه، أو يزيله عن طريقته، فينبغي أن يحترز منه، إذ كان انكسار النفس يميت الخاطر، ويقطع عن بلوغ الآخر، والزوال عن الطريق يخرج عن الاعتماد، ومن عرف من عادته ظلم الخصم فليس ينبغي أن يتهكم إلا أن يرجع إلى الإنصاف، أو يدفع إلى ذلك حال، فيحترز منه غاية الاحتراز. وقد عمل الأصوليون فيما أداروه من محاورات جدلية مع مخالفيهم أن يهيئوا الندية بينهم وبين مخالفيهم، فلم نقف في استقرائنا لمختلف المباحث الجدلية الأصولية على ما يمكن أن نسميه تطاولا على الطرف الآخر من هذا الطرف أو ذاك، وإنما وقفنا على نماذج كثيرة من التكافؤ في الحوار، والحرص على النظر.
He also said: Dialectic is intended for refining thoughts and testing them by rotating through degrees of thought, and for silencing the opponent and cutting him off with compelling arguments. Therefore they agreed on accepting attachment to the contradictions of the opponent, and a group attached to the constructions—and they are the majority—and they did not permit the objector to prevent the refutation or point to it, and other matters that are not hidden. So it is necessary—by necessity—to observe the benefit of dialectic. And Ibn 'Aqil emphasizes that the two disputants must make their intention one of two things, and strive to avoid the third. The highest of the three purposes is supporting the truth by presenting proof and refuting falsehood by invalidating the doubt, so that the word of Allah is the highest. The second: practice to gain strength for ijtihad, and ijtihad is among the praiseworthy ranks of religion, and it is the rank of fatwa. The first is like jihad, and the second is like marksmanship intended to gain strength for jihad. The third is overcoming and showing superiority over the opponent and prevailing over him, and we seek refuge in Allah from this way. 3 - Provision of intellectual freedom for both parties. Dialectic is essentially a dialogue of ideas and an exchange of concepts, and it is a mutual expression of what each party believes in their conscience regarding the issue under debate. Therefore this dialectic cannot achieve its goal unless each party is enabled to say what they want and what they believe with all freedom and transparency as long as they express their opinion and do not insult a person or entity other than them. For it is natural for any dialogue between two, to end—in its goal—to the result of deep and open faith in the results of the dialogue, to achieve a basic condition: that each party possesses the freedom of intellectual movement with which they have confidence in their independent intellectual personality, so that they are not under the mercy of intellectual and psychological terrorism that crushes them before the personality of the other because they feel deep within them the great and absolute greatness that the other possesses, so that—in the face of that—their self-confidence diminishes, and consequently their confidence in their thought and their ability to be a party to dialogue, so they become frozen and lose their ability for intellectual movement, turning into an echo of the ideas they receive from the other. And the common saying goes: Truth is the daughter of research, and beneficial research is only on the basis of freedom of thought and expression. And how excellent is the saying of Imam Ibn Hazm: Whoever truly examines and disciplines himself to accept truths even if they hurt him at first shock, his delight in people's blame of him is more intense and greater than his delight in their praise of him. Therefore the disputant may say everything that crosses his mind regarding what he researches, and it is incumbent upon his opponent to listen with understanding to everything presented before him on the research table without irritation or annoyance, even if what is said contradicts his opinion and belief, for often we have heard speech that we thought at first was wrong or mistaken or forgetful, but after deliberation, research, and investigation we found it to be the very truth and that we were the mistaken ones. So the absence of intellectual freedom and one party's transgression against another or restricting him would deprive the subject of research of richness and expansion and push the researcher to abandon the subject and distance themselves from contemplation. Therefore Imam Ibn 'Aqil warns saying: Every dialectic in which oppression of the opponent occurs is corrupted according to the strength or weakness of that oppression, because oppression of the opponent breaks his spirit or removes him from his way. So one should guard against it, since the breaking of the spirit kills the thought and cuts off from reaching the other, and deviating from the way removes one from reliance. And whoever knows that it is his habit to oppress the opponent should not mock unless he returns to fairness, or a situation forces that, and he guards against it with utmost caution. And the usul scholars, in the dialectical dialogues they conducted with their opponents, worked to establish parity between themselves and their opponents. We have not found, in our survey of various usuli dialectical discussions, what we could call arrogance towards the other party from this side or that. Rather, we have found many examples of equality in dialogue and concern for reflection.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage expands on the nature and prerequisites of dialectic (jadal) in the Islamic intellectual tradition. It begins by quoting al-Ghazali's definition of jadal as a tool for refining thoughts through repeated examination and for silencing opponents via binding arguments. The author then turns to Ibn 'Aqil, who classifies the possible intentions of disputants into three, urging them to avoid the third. The highest aim is the vindication of truth by presenting proof and demolishing falsehood, so that Allah's word prevails. The second aim is practice to strengthen one's capacity for ijtihad, which is a praiseworthy religious rank akin to issuing fatwas. Ibn 'Aqil analogizes the first to jihad and the second to marksmanship training for jihad. The third aim, which must be shunned, is mere victory and showing superiority. The passage then introduces a third point: the necessity of intellectual freedom for both parties. It explains that true dialectic requires each party to express their beliefs freely and transparently without personal attacks, for only then can the dialogue lead to deep conviction. Quoting a modern scholar, it stresses that each side must feel confident in their independent intellectual personality, free from intellectual or psychological intimidation. The author cites Ibn Hazm to illustrate the virtue of prizing truth over praise, and concludes with Ibn 'Aqil's warning that oppression in debate corrupts the discussion, breaks the spirit, and must be avoided. The passage ends by noting that the usul scholars historically maintained parity with their opponents, avoiding arrogance.

المقاصد
الحرية الفكرية

Rule 4: Adapting Speech to the Listener's Level
Author explains the rule of adapting speech to the listener's intellectual level in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rule of adapting speech to the listener's level and apply it to a dialectical exchange in the Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Effective jadal requires bridging the knowledge gap, not showcasing expertise.

In dialectical debate (jadal), the success of the process depends heavily on mutual understanding. This lesson unpacks the fourth rule from the adab al-bahth tradition: the arguer must adapt his speech to the listener's intellectual level. The author explains why this is necessary and gives a concrete example from the encounter of Moses and al-Khidr. By understanding this rule, you will be able to diagnose why many debates fail and how to structure your own arguments for maximum clarity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core requirement
Read the first sentence: 'Intellectual convergence between the two parties of debate is required for the success of the dialectical process.' The rule is that each party must try to adapt to the other's level of knowledge on the subject.
2
Recognize the consequence of ignorance
The author states that the first thing that spoils discussion is 'lack of understanding' (adam al-fahm). The arguer must consider the listener's position, including what background knowledge the listener lacks.
3
Understand the asymmetry of knowledge
The speaker often wrongly assumes the listener shares his mental picture. But the listener only accesses what is spoken; the speaker's integrated world of knowledge remains partly hidden. Persuasion fails if the means of persuasion do not match the listener's means of conviction.
The key concept is takyif: adaptation of discourse level.
4
Apply the rule to a real example
The passage ends with al-Tufi's citation of Moses and al-Khidr. Each acknowledged the other's special knowledge and did not debate beyond their depth. This models the ideal of mutual adaptation.
Al-Tufi: 'One should not debate with another in a science he does not understand or in which he is weak.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
4 - تكيف المتحدث مع مستوى المستمع :
التقارب الفكري بين طرفي الجدل أمر مطلوب لنجاح العملية الجدلية، وفي حال تباعد رؤيتيهما حول المسألة، فعلى كل منهما أن يحاول التكيف مع مستوى معرفة الآخر في المسألة موضع الجدل والنقاش وإلا فإن عملية الجدل ستدور في غير موضعها الصحيح، ولن تقود إلى نتيجة ذات قيمة علمية . ذلك أن أول ما يفسد النقاش هو عدم الفهم، ولهذا يتعين على المستدل أن يأخذ بعين الاعتبار وضعية المستمع من الأمر الذي يريد محاورته فيه، ومهما تكن درجة إحاطة المستدل بالموضوع الذي يريد إثباته، شاملة، ومهما تكن درجة ثقته في صحة ما يقدم كاملة، فإن وقع ذلك على المستمع سيكون بحسب موقع هذا المستمع من موضوع النقاش ومن معارف المستدل، إذ كثيرًا ما يظن المتحدث خطأ أن المستمع يتصور الأمور كما يتصورها هو، وإذا كان المستمع لا يستطيع أن يطلع من عالم معارف المتحدث إلا على ما يرد على لسان هذا الأخير، فإن قسمًا كبيرًا من عالم المعارف هذا يبقى مجهولاً لدى المستمع، وبالتالي غير فعال على تفكيره، بينما هو لدى المتحدث جزء من كل يندرج فيه مع سائر الأجزاء الأخرى ينسجم معها عند جريان التفكير لديه ؛ ولهذا ستكون قدرة المستدل على الإقناع متناسبة مع قدرته على إطلاع المستمع على جميع ما يستدرجه إلى الاقتناع، بل إن وسائل الإقناع لدى المستدل ينبغي أن تكون متناسبة مع وسائل الاقتناع لدى المستمع، فلا تكون دونها أبدًا» (1) .
وكما ينبغي لكل من المتجادلين أن يتكيف مع المستوى الفكري للآخر، ينبغي كذلك لكل منهما أن لا يفتح باب الجدل مع غيره إذا رأى أن هذا الغير ليس متخصصا في الموضوع وليس له معرفة سابقة به، لأن جهله بالمسألة سيحمله على التعنت والتكلف لما لا يعلم وربما قاده ذلك إلى إهانة الطرف المتخصص والاستهانة به قصد إظهار ضعفه وحمله على التسليم. قال الطوفي: «ولا يناظر أحدهما الآخر في علم لا يفهمه أو هو فيه ضعيف، إذ المتعرض لذلك مهین لنفسه، والداعي إليه مع علمه بقصور خصمه جائر عليه، وقد اجتمع موسى بالخضر فسلم كل منهما للآخر حاله واعترف له بما خصه الله عز وجل به من العلم»(1).
4 - The speaker's adaptation to the listener's level: Intellectual convergence between the two parties of debate is required for the success of the dialectical process. If their views on the issue diverge, each must try to adapt to the level of knowledge of the other regarding the subject of debate and discussion; otherwise, the debate will revolve in the wrong place and will not lead to a result of scientific value. That is because the first thing that spoils discussion is lack of understanding. Hence the arguer must take into consideration the position of the listener regarding the matter he wants to discuss with him. However comprehensive the arguer's knowledge of the subject he wants to prove, and however complete his confidence in the soundness of what he presents, when it reaches the listener it will be according to the listener's position vis-à-vis the subject of discussion and the arguer's knowledge. The speaker often mistakenly thinks that the listener conceives things as he conceives them. If the listener cannot access the speaker's world of knowledge except through what the latter utters, then a large part of this world of knowledge remains unknown to the listener, and thus ineffective on his thinking, while for the speaker it is part of a whole integrated with other parts, harmonious with them when his thinking flows. Hence the arguer's ability to persuade will be proportionate to his ability to inform the listener of all that leads him to conviction. Indeed, the arguer's means of persuasion should be proportionate to the listener's means of conviction, never falling short of them. (1) And just as each of the disputants should adapt to the intellectual level of the other, so too each should not open the door of debate with someone if he sees that the other is not specialized in the subject and has no prior knowledge of it, because his ignorance of the issue will lead him to obstinacy and affectation about what he does not know, and perhaps lead him to insult the specialized party and disparage him in order to show his weakness and force him to submit. Al-Tufi said: “One should not debate with another in a science he does not understand or in which he is weak, for the one who exposes himself to that demeans himself, and the one who calls him to it while knowing his opponent's deficiency is unjust towards him. Moses and al-Khidr met, and each submitted to the other's state and acknowledged what Allah had specially given him of knowledge.” (1)
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth rule of jadal: the speaker must adapt his speech to the listener's level of understanding. The author begins by stating that intellectual convergence between the two parties is necessary for the debate to succeed. If their views diverge, each must try to adapt to the other's level of knowledge on the subject; otherwise the debate will not achieve a meaningful result. The author explains that the primary cause of spoiled discussion is lack of understanding. Therefore, the arguer must consider the listener's position regarding the topic. Even if the arguer has comprehensive knowledge and complete confidence in his proof, its effect on the listener depends on the listener's location relative to the subject and the arguer's knowledge. The author warns that the speaker often wrongly assumes the listener conceives things as he does. Since the listener can only access the speaker's knowledge through what is spoken, a large part of that knowledge remains hidden and ineffective for the listener, whereas for the speaker it is an integrated whole. Consequently, the arguer's persuasive power is proportional to his ability to inform the listener of everything that leads to conviction; the means of persuasion must match the listener's means of conviction, never falling short. The passage then adds that each disputant should also avoid starting a debate with someone who is not specialized in the subject, because ignorance leads to obstinacy and may result in insulting the expert. This is supported by a quote from al-Tufi, who cites the example of Moses and al-Khidr as an ideal of mutual recognition of each other's knowledge.

Key Terms
الجدل
المستدل
المستمع
التكيف

C) Lack of understanding between the parties
D) The arguer's overconfidence

Key Takeaway
The arguer must adapt his means of persuasion to match the listener's means of conviction, otherwise the debate fails to produce understanding.

?
Imagine you are a specialist in usul al-fiqh arguing with a layperson about the validity of qiyas (analogical reasoning). Write a brief dialogue (4-6 exchanges) showing how you would adapt your speech to ensure the layperson understands the key points. Use the concepts from this lesson.
?
Identify a real historical debate from the Islamic tradition (e.g., between al-Ash'ari and a Mu'tazili) and explain how the participants did or did not follow the rule of adapting speech to the listener's level.

Rule 5: Not Reproaching Unintentional Slips in Debate
Author explains the rule of not reproaching an opponent for accidental slips.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of not reproaching an opponent for unintentional slips, distinguishing between deliberate error and slip of the tongue in a jadal context.

Core Idea
Forgive slips; hold only deliberate errors.

In extended debates, slips of the tongue are inevitable. The fifth rule of jadal teaches us to recognize unintentional errors and not use them to vilify the opponent. This lesson explains the rule through the words of Imam al-Juwayni and an example from al-Tufi on a verbal disagreement about the mubah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rationale behind the rule
Debate involves lengthy speech, which naturally leads to slips. Both parties must be aware of this and not exploit slips for attack.
2
Identify the two types of slips according to al-Juwayni
Al-Juwayni distinguishes between slips where the opponent's intent is unclear (alert him) and those where he admits it was unintentional (overlook). Only if you know he meant it should you hold him accountable.
3
Analyze the example from al-Tufi and al-Ka'bi
Al-Ka'bi says the mubah is commanded. When challenged, he clarifies he means it is obligatory for something else. Al-Tufi responds that the disagreement becomes verbal: no actual contradiction. This illustrates how understanding intent resolves apparent contradictions.
The example shows the rule in action: instead of reproaching, al-Tufi interprets al-Ka'bi's statement charitably.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
5 - عدم مؤاخذة المخالف فيما يزل به ولا يقصده : الجدل أساسًا تبادل كلام وربما طال الكلام من كلا الطرفين إلى ما لا حد له، وهذا بطبيعة الحال قد يجر أيا منهما إلى الزلل فيما يقول فيصدر عنه من الكلام ما لا يقصد معناه أو يحمله التعب على أن يقول كلامًا يفهم منه عكس مراده. لذلك كان مطلوبًا من كلا طرفي الجدل أن يعرف هذه الحقيقة، فلا يتحين فرص مثل هذا الزلل للتشنيع على المخالف وإظهار تناقضه، بل عليه أن يقدر هذه الحالة التي تعترضه فيكون منصفا في الحكم عليه فلا يؤاخذه بما يصدر عنه ويعلم أنه لا يقصده، قال إمام الحرمين الجويني : لا تؤاخذ الخصم بما تعلم أنه لا يقصده من أنواع الزلل، بل تعلم أنه يسبق اللسان ولما لا يخلو المتكلم منه . فإن أشكل عليك قصده في ذلك نبهته، فإن قال : هو لسبق اللسان ولم أقصده، تجاوزته، وإن علمته مقصودًا منه أحذته به (2) . من ذلك ما علق به الطوفي على قول الكعبي بأن المباح مأمور به : فإن قال: أنا لا أدعي وجوب المباح لذاته بل لغيره، وهو استلزامه الواجب ، وكونه لا ينفك عنه ولا يتم إلا به، فيصير من باب ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به وهو واجب. قلنا : رجع الخلاف لفظيا، لأنا لا ننازعك في وجوبه بهذا التفسير، فأنت تقول : المباح واجب لغيره، ونحن نقول ليس واجبًا لذاته، ولا تنافي بينهما (3).
5 - Not reproaching the opponent for slips he makes unintentionally: Debate is essentially an exchange of speech, and speech may become lengthy from both sides without limit. This naturally may lead either of them to slip in what he says, uttering speech whose meaning he did not intend, or fatigue may cause him to say something understood as the opposite of his intent. Therefore it is required from both parties of the debate to know this reality, and not to seize opportunities of such slips to vilify the opponent and show his contradiction. Rather, he should appreciate this condition that befalls him, be fair in judging him, and not reproach him for what he utters knowing that he did not intend it. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said: “Do not reproach the opponent for slips that you know he did not intend, rather know that it is a slip of the tongue from which no speaker is free. If his intent is unclear to you, alert him. If he says it was a slip of the tongue and he did not intend it, overlook it. If you know he intended it, hold him to it.” (2) An example is what al-Tufi commented on al-Ka'bi's statement that the permissible (mubah) is commanded: If he says: “I do not claim obligation of the mubah for itself, but for something else – namely, its entailing the obligatory and that it is inseparable from it and only completed through it – so it falls under 'what is necessary for the obligatory is obligatory'.” We say: The disagreement becomes verbal, for we do not dispute its obligation according to this interpretation. You say: the mubah is obligatory for something else; we say it is not obligatory in itself. There is no contradiction between them. (3)
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth rule of jadal: one should not reproach the opponent for unintentional slips of the tongue. The author begins by noting that debate is an exchange of speech that can become lengthy, which naturally may cause either party to slip and say something they did not intend, or fatigue may cause a statement that is the opposite of their intent. Therefore, both parties must be aware of this and should not seize upon such slips to vilify the opponent or show contradiction. Rather, they should be fair and not reproach the opponent for what they know was not intended. The author then quotes Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, who instructs: do not reproach the opponent for slips you know are unintentional; recognize it as a slip of the tongue from which no speaker is free. If the opponent's intent is unclear, alert him. If he explains it was a slip, overlook it. Only if you know he meant it should you hold him accountable. The passage then provides an example from al-Tufi regarding al-Ka'bi's statement that the permissible (mubah) is commanded. Al-Ka'bi clarifies that he does not mean the permissible is obligatory in itself, but because it entails the obligatory. al-Tufi comments that the disagreement becomes verbal: they agree on the meaning but differ in wording. This illustrates how understanding context and intent can resolve apparent contradictions.

Key Terms
الزلل
المخالف
المؤاخذة
سبق اللسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Juwayni, what should you do if an opponent makes a slip and then claims it was unintentional?
A) Immediately point out the contradiction publicly
B) Overlook it if he says it was a slip of the tongue
C) Demand proof that it was unintentional
D) End the debate immediately

Key Takeaway
In jadal, one must distinguish between deliberate error and unintentional slip, and only reproach the former.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Task: In a debate, your opponent says: 'The Quran is created.' You know this contradicts Sunni doctrine. Apply al-Juwayni's procedure: first, consider if it might be a slip. If he clarifies it was a slip, how should you respond? If he insists it was deliberate, what should you do?

Rule Five: Not Holding Opponent Accountable for Slips
Author states and explains the fifth rule of jadal regarding slips.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the fifth rule of jadal by distinguishing unintended slips from deliberate errors in a debate and responding appropriately.

Core Idea
In jadal, do not exploit your opponent's slip; overlook it if unintended.

Dialectical debates involve long exchanges where slips of the tongue are natural. The fifth rule teaches fairness: do not hold the opponent accountable for what he does not intend. This lesson explains the rule and its practical application as outlined by Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni.

Guided Path
1
Identify the slip
Recognize that the opponent may have said something unintended due to the length or fatigue of debate.
A slip is an error in speech, not a deliberate misstatement.
2
Determine intent
If the opponent's intention is unclear, caution him. If he admits it was a slip, overlook it. If you know it was intended, then take him to task.
Juwayni's guideline: 'Do not hold the opponent accountable for what you know he does not intend.'
3
Act accordingly
Respond with fairness: if unintended, do not use it to vilify him. If intended, address it as a genuine error.
This preserves the integrity of the debate and focuses on truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
5 - عدم مؤاخذة المخالف فيما يزل به ولا يقصده : الجدل أساسًا تبادل كلام وربما طال الكلام من كلا الطرفين إلى ما لا حد له، وهذا بطبيعة الحال قد يجر أيا منهما إلى الزلل فيما يقول فيصدر عنه من الكلام ما لا يقصد معناه أو يحمله التعب على أن يقول كلامًا يفهم منه عكس مراده. لذلك كان مطلوبًا من كلا طرفي الجدل أن يعرف هذه الحقيقة، فلا يتحين فرص مثل هذا الزلل للتشنيع على المخالف وإظهار تناقضه، بل عليه أن يقدر هذه الحالة التي تعترضه فيكون منصفا في الحكم عليه فلا يؤاخذه بما يصدر عنه ويعلم أنه لا يقصده، قال إمام الحرمين الجويني : لا تؤاخذ الخصم بما تعلم أنه لا يقصده من أنواع الزلل، بل تعلم أنه يسبق اللسان ولما لا يخلو المتكلم منه . فإن أشكل عليك قصده في ذلك نبهته، فإن قال : هو لسبق اللسان ولم أقصده، تجاوزته، وإن علمته مقصودًا منه أحذته به (2) .
من ذلك ما علق به الطوفي على قول الكعبي بأن المباح مأمور به : فإن قال: أنا لا أدعي وجوب المباح لذاته بل لغيره، وهو استلزامه الواجب ، وكونه لا ينفك عنه ولا يتم إلا به، فيصير من باب ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به وهو واجب.
قلنا :
رجع الخلاف لفظيا، لأنا لا ننازعك في وجوبه بهذا التفسير، فأنت تقول : المباح واجب لغيره، ونحن نقول ليس واجبًا لذاته، ولا تنافي بينهما (3)

ومن ذلك؛ ما نراه من احترام إمام الحرمين الجويني للقاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني رغم مخالفته له .
ففي مسألة الصيغة المطلقة للأمر، هل تقتضي فورية الامتثال أم لا؟ رأى الباقلاني حمل الصيغة على إيقاع الامتثال من غير نظر إلى وقت مقدم أو مؤخر، مع الاستمساك بالوقف وتجهيل من لا يراه.
فبعد أن عرض الجويني هذا الرأي ودليله، قال في التعليق عليه : وهذا خروج عظيم عن مسلك التحقيق، وفيه أولا التزام أمر اقتحاما عليه من غير أن يشعر اللفظ به ؛ وقد صار هذا الحَبْرُ - أي الباقلاني – إلى الوقف في أصل الصيغة . . . » . واحترام الجويني للباقلاني لم يتوقف عند حد وصفه له بكونه حبرًا، بل تجاوز ذلك إلى محاولة إيجاد تبريرات يعتذر بها عنه، فقال: والذي أراه في طريقة القاضي رحمه الله، أنه إنما يوجب العزم في الوقت الأول، ولا يوجب تجديده، ثم يحكم بأن ذلك العزم ينسحب حكمه على جميع الأوقات المستقبلة، وهذا كانبساط النية على العبادة الطويلة، مع عزوب النية ؛ ولا ينبغي أن يظن بهذا الرجل العظيم غير هذا على أنا لا نرى ذلك رأيا (1) .
5 - Not holding the opponent accountable for what he slips into and does not intend: Dialectic is fundamentally an exchange of speech, and perhaps speech may become prolonged from both sides indefinitely, and this naturally may lead either of them to slip in what he says, so there issues from him speech whose meaning he does not intend, or fatigue may cause him to say speech from which the opposite of his intention is understood. Therefore it is required of both sides of the dialectic to know this reality, so that they do not seek opportunities for such a slip to vilify the opponent and show his contradiction, but rather they should appreciate this condition that befalls him, being fair in judgment upon him, not holding him accountable for what issues from him while knowing that he does not intend it. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said: Do not hold the opponent accountable for what you know he does not intend among the types of slips, but know that it is a slip of the tongue, and indeed the speaker is not free from it. If his intention in that is unclear to you, then caution him. If he says: it is due to a slip of the tongue and I did not intend it, then overlook it. And if you know it is intended from him, then seize him with it (2). Among that is what al-Tufi commented on al-Ka'bi's statement that permissible actions are commanded: For if he says: I do not claim obligation of the permissible in itself but for something else, namely its entailing the obligatory, and its not being separable from it nor completed except by it, so it becomes of the category of 'that without which the obligatory is not completed' which is obligatory. We say: The disagreement returns to being verbal, for we do not dispute you in its obligation according to this interpretation. You say: The permissible is obligatory for something else, and we say it is not obligatory in itself, and there is no contradiction between them (3). And among that is what we see of the respect of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni for al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani despite his disagreement with him. For in the issue of the absolute form of the command, whether it necessitates immediacy of compliance or not, al-Baqillani held that the form indicates initiating compliance without regard to a preceding or succeeding time, with holding to suspension and declaring ignorant whoever does not hold that. After al-Juwayni presented this opinion and its evidence, he said in commentary on it: This is a great departure from the path of verification, and in it is first an undertaking of a matter that is an imposition upon it without the wording implying it; and this great scholar – i.e., al-Baqillani – has come to suspension in the original form ... And al-Juwayni's respect for al-Baqillani did not stop at describing him as a scholar, but exceeded that to attempting to find excuses to apologize for him. He said: What I see in the method of the Qadi, may God have mercy on him, is that he only necessitates resolve at the first time, and does not necessitate its renewal, then he judges that that resolve extends its ruling to all future times, and this is like the extension of intention over a long act of worship, despite the absence of intention; and it is not proper to think of this great man other than this, although we do not see that as an opinion (1).
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage explains the fifth rule of dialectical debate: not holding an opponent accountable for slips he does not intend. The author begins by stating that debate is essentially an exchange of speech that can become lengthy, which naturally leads to slips. Therefore, both parties must recognize this and not exploit such slips to vilify the opponent or show his contradiction. Instead, they should be fair and not hold him accountable for what he does not intend. The author then quotes Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, who provides a practical guideline: do not hold the opponent accountable for slips you know he did not intend; if his intention is unclear, caution him; if he admits it was a slip, overlook it; if you know it was intended, then take him to task. Two examples follow. First, al-Tufi's commentary on al-Ka'bi's claim that permissible actions are commanded: the opponent clarifies that he means obligatory by something else (i.e., by entailment), so al-Tufi notes the disagreement is merely verbal—they do not dispute the obligation under that interpretation. This shows not seizing a verbal slip. Second, al-Juwayni's respectful treatment of al-Baqillani despite strong disagreement on the issue of command immediacy. Al-Juwayni calls him a 'great scholar' and even attempts to reinterpret his position in a more favorable light, showing respect and avoiding harsh accountability for what he sees as an erroneous view.

Key Terms
مؤاخذة
زلل
خصم
جدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Juwayni, if an opponent says something unintentionally and admits it was a slip, what should you do?
A) Warn him
B) Overlook it
C) Take him to task
D) Demand proof

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the fifth rule demands fairness: hold the opponent accountable only for intended errors, not unintended slips.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a mock debate, one student says: 'The Quran is created because it is composed of letters.' Another student catches the slip (the Quran is uncreated in Sunni theology). Apply the fifth rule: is this a slip? How would you respond?

Applying Rule Five: Two Case Studies
Author provides two examples illustrating the fifth rule of jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the fifth rule of jadal applies to real debates by analyzing the examples of al-Ka'bi and al-Baqillani.

Core Idea
The fifth rule turns potential disagreements into verbal disputes or redirects to respectful reinterpretation.

The fifth rule is not just theoretical. Two examples from Islamic intellectual history show it in action: al-Tufi's response to al-Ka'bi on the permissibility of permissible actions, and al-Juwayni's respectful treatment of al-Baqillani on the issue of command immediacy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the potential slip or disagreement
In the first example, al-Ka'bi says 'permissible actions are commanded' – a statement that seems to contradict the definition of permissible as not commanded.
In the second, al-Baqillani holds that the absolute command does not imply immediacy, which al-Juwayni sees as a departure.
2
Check if the opponent intends the literal meaning
Al-Ka'bi clarifies that he means 'obligatory for something else' (entailing the obligatory), not obligatory in itself.
Al-Juwayni similarly tries to reinterpret al-Baqillani's view as meaning only initial resolve, not a claim that command is completely indifferent.
3
Determine if the issue is verbal or substantive
Al-Tufi concludes the disagreement is merely verbal: both agree on the content but use different terminology.
Al-Juwayni, despite his criticism, offers a charitable interpretation, showing respect.
4
Apply the fifth rule
Neither al-Tufi nor al-Juwayni seizes on the statement as a deliberate error; they seek clarification or reinterpretation.
This illustrates the rule: do not hold the opponent accountable for unintended implications.

And if you know it is intended from him, then seize him with it (2). After al-Juwayni presented this opinion and its evidence, he said in commentary on it: This is a great departure from the path of verification, and in it is first an undertaking of a matter that is an imposition upon it without the wording implying it; and this great scholar – i.e., al-Baqillani – has come to suspension in the original form ... And al-Juwayni's respect for al-Baqillani did not stop at describing him as a scholar, but exceeded that to attempting to find excuses to apologize for him. He said: What I see in the method of the Qadi, may God have mercy on him, is that he only necessitates resolve at the first time, and does not necessitate its renewal, then he judges that that resolve extends its ruling to all future times, and this is like the extension of intention over a long act of worship, despite the absence of intention; and it is not proper to think of this great man other than this, although we do not see that as an opinion (1).
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage explains the fifth rule of dialectical debate: not holding an opponent accountable for slips he does not intend. The author begins by stating that debate is essentially an exchange of speech that can become lengthy, which naturally leads to slips. Therefore, both parties must recognize this and not exploit such slips to vilify the opponent or show his contradiction. Instead, they should be fair and not hold him accountable for what he does not intend. The author then quotes Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, who provides a practical guideline: do not hold the opponent accountable for slips you know he did not intend; if his intention is unclear, caution him; if he admits it was a slip, overlook it; if you know it was intended, then take him to task. Two examples follow. First, al-Tufi's commentary on al-Ka'bi's claim that permissible actions are commanded: the opponent clarifies that he means obligatory by something else (i.e., by entailment), so al-Tufi notes the disagreement is merely verbal—they do not dispute the obligation under that interpretation. This shows not seizing a verbal slip. Second, al-Juwayni's respectful treatment of al-Baqillani despite strong disagreement on the issue of command immediacy. Al-Juwayni calls him a 'great scholar' and even attempts to reinterpret his position in a more favorable light, showing respect and avoiding harsh accountability for what he sees as an erroneous view.

Key Terms
مؤاخذة
زلل
خصم
جدل
وجوب لغيره
صيغة الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the al-Ka'bi example, after clarification, what did al-Tufi conclude?
A) They had a real disagreement
B) The disagreement was verbal
C) Al-Ka'bi was wrong
D) Al-Ka'bi was right

Key Takeaway
The fifth rule allows disagreements to be resolved by clarifying intent, often revealing mere verbal disputes.

Rule Six: Alerting the Opponent to Contradictions in Debate
Author states and explains the sixth rule of jadal about mutual notification of contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the sixth rule of jadal and apply it to identify when and how to alert an opponent to contradictions in his speech.

Core Idea
A dialectician must actively point out contradictions to keep the debate focused and reveal truth, not to nitpick slips.

The sixth rule of jadal complements the fifth: while you must not hold the opponent accountable for unintentional slips, you must not fall short in alerting him to clear contradictions in his speech. This keeps the debate anchored on the main issue and fulfills the purpose of dialectic: making the truth evident. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni underscores this duty, warning that neglecting it abandons the debate's purpose.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish slips from contradictions
Rule five forbids holding the opponent to account for slips (accidental errors). Rule six requires you to alert him to contradictions (mutanāqiḍāt) that conflict with his own stated positions. These are different: slips are minor mistakes; contradictions undermine logical coherence.
Slips are not the same as contradictions.
2
Understand the purpose of alerting
The author states that alerting the opponent to contradictions 'gathers the dialectic around a specific issue' and prevents the discussion from scattering into subsidiary branches. It keeps the debate focused.
Focus is a key goal of jadal.
3
Apply Juwayni's advice
Juwayni says: do not fall short in alerting the opponent to what you see of contradictions in his speech. Also, if he retracts something he conceded, alert him to that as well.
Retractions are also contradictions to prior concessions.
4
Recognize the ethical imperative
Juwayni warns that neglecting this duty leaves 'most of the purpose of the dialectic' and then 'your support of truth does not become clear'. Thus, alerting is not optional; it is required for the truth to be made evident.
The goal of jadal is to clarify truth, not to win at all costs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
6 - تنبيه كل منهما الآخر إلى ما يقع في كلامه من متناقضات : كما ينبغي على كل مجادل أن لا يؤاخذ خصمه في ما يزل به، عليه أيضًا أن لا يقصر في تنبيهه إلى ما قد يقع في كلامه من متناقضات أثناء الجدل، فإن هذا التنبيه شأنه أن يجمع الجدل حول مسألة محددة ولا يسمح بتشتت فروعها ومسائلها الجزئية.
قال إمام الحرمين الجويني :
لا تقصر في تنبيه الخصم وإعلامه ما ترى من مناقضاته في كلامه، وهكذا إذا رجع فيما سلم نبهته عليه، لأنك إذا لم تعامله بذلك، تركت معظم المقصود من الجدل، وعند ذلك لا تبين نصرتك للحق (2).
6 - Alerting each other to contradictions that occur in his speech: Just as it is incumbent upon every dialectician not to hold his opponent accountable for what he slips into, so also he should not fall short in alerting him to contradictions that may occur in his speech during the dialectic. For such alerting has the property of gathering the dialectic around a specific issue and does not allow its subsidiary branches and partial issues to scatter. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said: Do not fall short in alerting the opponent and informing him of what you see of contradictions in his speech, and likewise if he retracts something he conceded, you alert him to it. Because if you do not treat him thus, you have left most of the purpose of the dialectic, and then your support of truth does not become clear (2).
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth rule of dialectical debate, which complements the fifth. While rule five forbids holding the opponent accountable for unintentional slips, rule six requires the dialectician to actively alert the opponent to contradictions in his speech. The author explains that such alerting helps keep the debate focused on the main issue, preventing the discussion from scattering into subsidiary points. He then quotes Juwayni, who emphasizes not 'falling short' in pointing out contradictions and also in noting if the opponent retracts a previous concession. Juwayni warns that neglecting this duty abandons the main purpose of dialectic, which is to make the truth evident. Thus, the dialectician must strike a balance: not nitpicking slips but clearly flagging contradictions that affect the argument's coherence.

Key Terms
تنبيه
متناقضات
مناقضات

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The dialectician wins the debate by default.

Key Takeaway
The dialectician has a duty to alert the opponent to contradictions in his speech to keep the debate focused and to manifest the truth, as neglecting this abandons the essential purpose of jadal.

Adab al-Ikhtilaf: Al-Juwayni's Respectful Critique of al-Baqillani
Example of Juwayni respectfully critiquing Baqillani's view on command form while disagreeing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the adab of respectful disagreement in a jadal exchange and reconstruct al-Juwayni's charitable interpretation strategy.

Core Idea
True adab means disagreeing firmly but charitably interpreting the opponent's position.

This passage from the usul tradition presents al-Juwayni's interaction with al-Baqillani on whether an absolute command demands immediate compliance. The lesson is not about the command debate itself but about how to disagree with respect. Al-Juwayni first states his strong objection, then immediately seeks a charitable reading of al-Baqillani's intention, distinguishing between the man and his view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the point of disagreement
Recognize that this is a jadal between two major usuli scholars: al-Juwayni and al-Baqillani, on whether an absolute command (al-sighat al-mutlaqa) implies immediate compliance (fawri) or allows delay (tarakhi). Note al-Baqillani's view: he holds that the command indicates compliance without specifying time, and he suspends judgment while considering those who disagree as ignorant.
The debate is part of the larger usuli discussion about command (amr) and the implications of its form.
2
Extract al-Juwayni's criticism of al-Baqillani's view
Locate the phrase 'This is a great departure from the path of verification' and understand his objection: al-Baqillani imposes a meaning not indicated by the wording. This shows that rigorous critique is permissible in jadal.
Al-Juwayni uses strong language like 'kharajun 'adhim' (great departure), but immediately follows with respect.
3
Identify the charitable interpretation al-Juwayni offers
Al-Juwayni suggests that al-Baqillani might have meant that only an initial resolve (azm) is required, which extends automatically to all future times, like the extension of intention (niyya) over a long act of worship. This is a 'takhrij' or excuse.
Even though al-Juwayni does not agree with this interpretation, he insists it is the only fair way to view a great scholar.
4
Reflect on the adab principle: separating the person from the argument
Note that al-Juwayni calls al-Baqillani a 'great man' and says 'it should not be thought of this great man anything other than this'. This exemplifies the ethical rule in jadal: respect the opponent even while refuting his argument.
This principle is foundational in Islamic dialectic (adab al-bahth) to prevent debate from becoming personal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك؛ ما نراه من احترام إمام الحرمين الجويني للقاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني رغم مخالفته له . ففي مسألة الصيغة المطلقة للأمر، هل تقتضي فورية الامتثال أم لا؟ رأى الباقلاني حمل الصيغة على إيقاع الامتثال من غير نظر إلى وقت مقدم أو مؤخر، مع الاستمساك بالوقف وتجهيل من لا يراه. فبعد أن عرض الجويني هذا الرأي ودليله، قال في التعليق عليه : وهذا خروج عظيم عن مسلك التحقيق، وفيه أولا التزام أمر اقتحاما عليه من غير أن يشعر اللفظ به ؛ وقد صار هذا الحَبْرُ - أي الباقلاني – إلى الوقف في أصل الصيغة . . . » . واحترام الجويني للباقلاني لم يتوقف عند حد وصفه له بكونه حبرًا، بل تجاوز ذلك إلى محاولة إيجاد تبريرات يعتذر بها عنه، فقال: والذي أراه في طريقة القاضي رحمه الله، أنه إنما يوجب العزم في الوقت الأول، ولا يوجب تجديده، ثم يحكم بأن ذلك العزم ينسحب حكمه على جميع الأوقات المستقبلة، وهذا كانبساط النية على العبادة الطويلة، مع عزوب النية ؛ ولا ينبغي أن يظن بهذا الرجل العظيم غير هذا على أنا لا نرى ذلك رأيا» (١).
And among that is what we see of the respect of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni for the judge Abu Bakr al-Baqillani despite his disagreement with him. In the issue of the absolute command form, does it require immediate compliance or not? Al-Baqillani held that the form is to be applied to performing compliance without consideration of an earlier or later time, while adhering to suspension and considering ignorant one who does not hold that. After al-Juwayni presented this view and its evidence, he said in commentary on it: 'This is a great departure from the path of verification, and in it first is undertaking a matter that is an intrusion into it without the wording indicating it; and this scholar—meaning al-Baqillani—has adopted suspension regarding the original form...' And al-Juwayni's respect for al-Baqillani did not stop at describing him as a scholar; it went beyond that to an attempt to find justifications to apologize for him, saying: 'What I see in the method of the judge, may God have mercy on him, is that he only requires resolve in the first moment, and does not require its renewal; then he judges that that resolve extends its ruling to all future times, and this is like the extension of intention over a long act of worship, despite the absence of intention; and it should not be thought of this great man anything other than this, although we do not see that as a view.'
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage provides an example of respectful disagreement in dialectic (jadal). The author discusses the disagreement between al-Juwayni and al-Baqillani regarding whether an absolute command implies immediate compliance (fawri) or permissibility of delay (tarakhi). Al-Baqillani's view is described: he holds that the command form indicates compliance without specifying time, but he suspends judgment and considers those who disagree as ignorant. Al-Juwayni first critiques this view strongly, calling it a departure from verification and noting that it imposes a meaning not indicated by the wording. However, al-Juwayni then shows respect by attempting to find a charitable interpretation: perhaps al-Baqillani meant that an initial resolve (azm) suffices and covers all future times, like extending intention over a lengthy worship. Al-Juwayni does not agree with this interpretation but insists that such a great man should not be thought of otherwise. This illustrates the adab of debate: even when criticizing, one should seek excuses for the opponent and maintain respect.

Key Terms
الوقت الأول
العزم
الحبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) He provides a charitable reinterpretation to justify al-Baqillani's position.
C) He declares al-Baqillani an innovator.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the duty to argue against an opponent's view is paired with the duty to think well of the opponent's intention.
