Resolving Apparent Contradiction: Wajib Mukhayyar
Tufi and Qarafi resolve the seeming contradiction in the Mu'tazilite view on mukhayyar obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a verbal disagreement (khilaf lafzi) in a juridical debate and reconstruct the reconciliation offered by later scholars.

Core Idea
Not every contradiction is real — often it's just a verbal dispute hiding agreement.

In usul al-fiqh, scholars often debate issues that appear contradictory on the surface. The case of the mukhayyar duty (wajib mukhayyar) — where multiple actions are obligatory but any one suffices — is a classic example. Al-Tufi and al-Qarafi show how the Mu'tazilite statement 'all is obligatory by way of choice' is not self-contradictory when properly understood. This lesson will train you to detect when a disagreement is merely verbal (lafzi) rather than substantive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Look for the two incompatible implications: 'obligation of all' seems to require doing every act, while 'choice' allows doing any one. Recognize why the majority thought this was a real contradiction.
The contradiction is only apparent; it arises from a surface reading.
2
Extract the actual meaning of the Mu'tazila
Al-Tufi explains that by 'obligation of all' they mean it is not permissible to leave all, not that each individual act is obligatory. Note that obligation attaches to the set, not to each item.
The key distinction: obligation to do at least one vs. obligation to do each.
3
See the reconciliation by al-Qarafi
Al-Qarafi states that obligation attaches to all actions in a way that liability is discharged by doing some. This preserves both the idea of obligation (you must do something) and choice (you can choose which).
The reconciliation shows that the positions are substantively the same.
4
Determine if the disagreement is verbal or real
Since both sides agree that one must do at least one of the acts, and that doing any one suffices, the disagreement is merely about expression. Al-Tufi calls this khilaf lafzi.
Many debates in usul al-fiqh resolve into verbal disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من ذلك ما ساقه الطوفي في مسألة الواجب المخير، من رأي للمعتزلة، إن قولهم : يجب الجميع على التخيير، كلام متناقض في نفسه، إذ مقتضى وجوب الجميع : أن لا يبرأ إلا بفعل الجميع ومقتضى التخيير ؛ أنه يبرأ بفعل أيها شاء، وهما لا يجتمعان، وإنما مرادهم بوجوب الجميع أنه لا يجوز ترك الجميع، وهو صحيح، لكن لا يلزم منه وجوب فعل الجميع، أو وجوب الجميع على البدل، لا على الجمع بمعنى إن لم تفعل هذا افعل هذا، وهو مذهب الجمهور. وقال القرافي في معناه : إن الوجوب تعلق بالجميع على وجه تبرأ الذمة بفعل البعض، وهو معنى ما قلناه، وتبين بذلك أن الخلاف لفظي، وأقول : إن الغلط في المسألة، إما من المعتزلة، حيث ظنوا أن الوجوب مع التخيير لا يجتمعان، أو من الجمهور على المعتزلة بأن رأوا لهم عبارة موهمة أو بعيدة الغور، فظنوا أنهم أرادوا وجوب الجميع، كما وهموا عليهم في تلخيص مسألة تحسين العقل وتقبيحه، وغيرها من المسائل التي توجد في كتب المعتزلة، على خلاف المنقول عنهم فيها (١).
Among that is what al-Tufi presented in the issue of the mukhayyar duty, concerning a view of the Mu'tazila: their statement 'all is obligatory by way of choice' is self-contradictory, because the implication of obligating all is that one only becomes free of liability by doing all, while the implication of choice is that one becomes free by doing any of them; they are incompatible. But what they mean by 'obligation of all' is that it is not permissible to leave all, which is correct, but it does not necessitate obligation to do all, or obligation of all on substitution, not on conjunction, meaning if you do not do this, do that, which is the view of the majority. And al-Qarafi said in its meaning: obligation attached to all in such a way that liability is discharged by doing some, which is the meaning of what we said, and it becomes clear from that that the disagreement is verbal. I say: the error in the issue is either from the Mu'tazila, where they thought that obligation with choice cannot coexist, or from the majority against the Mu'tazila, where they saw an ambiguous or deep expression and thought they meant obligation of all, just as they misunderstood them in summarizing the issue of rational goodness and badness and other issues found in Mu'tazilite books, contrary to what is transmitted from them.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage uses a specific case from usul al-fiqh to illustrate how apparent contradictions in debate can be resolved. The issue is the 'mukhayyar duty' (wajib mukhayyar), where multiple acts are obligatory but the fulfillment of any one suffices. The Mu'tazila's statement 'all is obligatory by way of choice' seems contradictory: obligation of all implies doing all, while choice implies doing one. Al-Tufi explains that the Mu'tazila's actual meaning is that it is not permissible to leave all, not that each individual act is obligatory. The majority view is that it is obligation with substitution (badal). Al-Qarafi further clarifies that the obligation attaches to the set in a way that discharge occurs by doing some, making the disagreement verbal. The author then suggests that the error lies either with the Mu'tazila for thinking obligation and choice cannot coexist, or with the majority for misunderstanding the Mu'tazila's expression. This shows that careful analysis can turn a seeming contradiction into a verbal dispute.

Key Terms
الواجب المخير (al-wajib al-mukhayyar): A duty where multiple actions are obligatory but performance of any one suffices to fulfill the obligation; e.g., kaffara options.
الخلاف لفظي (al-khilaf lafzi): Verbal disagreement; when the substantive positions are the same but expressed differently, causing apparent contradiction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the wajib mukhayyar example, how does al-Qarafi resolve the apparent contradiction in the Mu'tazilite view?
A) He says the Mu'tazila are simply wrong.
B) He argues that obligation attaches to all actions but discharge occurs by doing some.
C) He says the disagreement is real and substantial.
D) He says the Mu'tazila meant that all actions are equally optional.

Key Takeaway
The reconciliation of the wajib mukhayyar debate shows that differences in expression do not always indicate substantive opposition — identifying verbal disputes is a key skill in usul al-fiqh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Patience: Allowing Opponent to Complete Speech
The requirement for debaters to patiently let each other finish speaking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the requirement of patience in debate and its Quranic basis, and apply it to a hypothetical jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Patience in debate is a sign of respect and a condition for understanding the truth.

In a structured debate (jadal), the goal is to arrive at the truth through disciplined exchange. This requires mutual respect, and one of its key expressions is allowing your opponent to fully express his argument without interruption. This lesson explores that principle and its foundation in Islamic adab, as derived from the Quranic command to listen attentively when the Quran is recited.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main rule
Find the sentence in the source that states the requirement. It says: each debater must wait for the other until he fully expresses his opinion on the disputed issue, finishing by himself.
The rule is about completing the expression without being cut off.
2
Extract the Quranic evidence
The author cites Surah al-A'raf (7:204): 'And when the Quran is recited, listen to it and be silent that you may receive mercy.' Note how this verse is used analogically: just as one must listen to Allah's speech, so must one listen to the opponent's speech.
The analogy is between listening to divine revelation and listening to human argument.
3
Understand the underlying rationale
The purpose of patience is to understand the opponent's argument fully, and through that understanding, the truth becomes clearer. Interrupting prevents this and shows disrespect.
The passage ties patience to the higher goal of reaching truth and attaining mercy.
4
Apply the rule to a hypothetical scenario
Consider a debate where one speaker keeps interrupting the other. Determine which rule is violated and why this harms the dialectical process according to the author.
This step reinforces the practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من مقتضيات احترام كل من طرفي الجدل للطرف الآخر؛ أن يصبر كل منهما على الآخر حتى يعبر عن رأيه في المسألة المتنازع فيها تعبيرا كاملا، بحيث ينهي كلامه بنفسه وليس بأن يوقفه الآخر. وهذا من الأدب الرفيع الذي أدب الإسلام به المسلمين، فأمرهم بالإنصات للقرآن حتى يفهموا مراد الله من كلامه ومن ثم تنزل عليهم رحمة الله التي أودعها الإيمان والصلاح والهدى والرشاد والشفاء الذي تضمنها القرآن الكريم، قال تعالى : وَإِذَا قُرِئَ الْقُرْوَانُ فَاسْتَمِعُوا لَهُمْ وَأَنصِتُوا لَعَلَّكُمْ تُرْحَمُونَ (٢).
One of the requirements of respect between the two parties of debate is that each should be patient with the other until he fully expresses his opinion on the disputed issue, so that he finishes his speech by himself and not by being stopped by the other. This is from the noble etiquette with which Islam taught Muslims, for He commanded them to listen attentively to the Quran so that they understand God's intent from His speech, and then God's mercy that He placed in faith, righteousness, guidance, right conduct, and healing contained in the noble Quran descends upon them. He said, the Most High: 'And when the Quran is recited, listen to it and be silent that you may receive mercy.'
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage emphasizes the etiquette of patience in debate: each debater must allow the other to fully express his view without interruption. The author grounds this principle in Islamic adab, citing the Quranic command to listen attentively when the Quran is recited. The analogy is that just as one must listen to Allah's speech to understand His intent, in debate one must listen to the opponent's speech to understand his argument fully. This patience is a sign of respect and is necessary for the truth to become clear. The Quranic verse (al-A'raf 7:204) is quoted as evidence, showing that even in a sacred context, silence and listening are commanded to attain mercy.

Key Terms
الصبر
الإنصات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What Quranic evidence does the author give to support the duty of patiently listening to the opponent?
A) 'And speak to people kindly.'
B) 'And when the Quran is recited, listen to it and be silent that you may receive mercy.'
C) 'And argue with them in the best way.'
D) 'And do not be like those who divided and differed.'

Key Takeaway
Patience in debate is a manifestation of Islamic adab, grounded in the Quranic command to listen attentively, and it is essential for a productive and respectful dialectical exchange.

Dialectic -- Jadal

The Etiquette of Listening in Jadal: Full Attention as a Dialectical Obligation
Authors cite classical scholars on the necessity of full listening for effective debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate and apply the rules of full listening in jadal, citing the Qur'anic principle, Prophetic practice, and the rulings of al-Juwayni, al-Baji, Ibn 'Aqil, and al-Khatib al-Baghdadi.

Core Idea
In jadal, listening is not passive—it is an active duty that safeguards fairness and reveals truth.

This passage from a classical work on jadal (debate) compiles authoritative statements on the necessity of listening fully to one's opponent. It begins with the Qur'anic command to listen to speech and follow the best, and the Prophet's practice of waiting until a speaker finishes. The scholars then derive practical rules: alternation of turns, patience even when the opponent's speech seems confused, and the prohibition of interruption. Understanding these rules is essential for anyone engaging in scholarly debate within the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational texts
Locate the Quranic verse and the Prophetic sunna cited at the beginning. Why are they placed here? They establish that listening is a divinely mandated and Prophetic practice.
2
Extract the principle of munawaba
From al-Juwayni's quote, identify the rule of turn-taking. What does he say about equality in the right to speak? Note the term 'al-munawaba'.
Al-Juwayni emphasizes that both parties are equal in the right of alternation.
3
Analyze the exception for waswasa
Al-Juwayni says to be patient even if the speech sounds like waswasa. Why? Because it might be the actual point of doubt. What does this teach about handling perceived confusion?
The listener should not dismiss unclear points prematurely.
4
Understand the consequences of interruption (taqti')
Trace the scholars' warnings: al-Juwayni says first bear and admonish, then cut off if persistent; Ibn 'Aqil says it prevents understanding; al-Baji says it removes benefit. Summarize why interruption is harmful.
Interruption undermines the purpose of debate.
5
Synthesize the practical advice
Combine the recommendations of al-Baji and al-Khatib: face the opponent, avoid premature judgment, and allow full expression. How do these facilitate effective rebuttal?
Patience and attention enable a more informed and precise response.

الجدل
303
وقال موجها المؤمنين للاستماع إلى القول استماعا تاما ليأخذوا منه أحسنه : ه ... الَّذِينَ يَسْتَمِعُونَ الْقَوْلَ فَيَتَّبِعُونَ أَحْسَنَهُ، ...) (1) .
•
وقد طبق الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ، ذلك ، فكان ينصت لأصحابه حتى يفرغ محدثه منهم من كلامه ثم يبدأ هو بالكلام، وذلك مستفيض ومشهور من مقابلاته وإجابته على أسئلة السائلين ونحو ذلك مما هو معروف من سيرته التي أمرنا بالاقتداء بها»(2). إن سماح كل طرف للطرف الآخر بالتعبير عن رأيه وصبره عليه حتى ينهي کلامه إلى جانب أنه احترام للرأي الآخر ، فيه أيضًا فائدة للجدل، وهي وضوح الرؤية بالنسبة لكل طرف في شأن ما يراه الطرف الآخر ، إذ ربما كان الطرفان على رأي واحد في المسألة وهما يعتقدان أنهما على خلاف فيها فيكون التعبير الكامل عن الرأي طريقا إلى إظهار هذا التوافق بعد اعتقاد التخالف قال الجويني : «عليهما جميعا أن يصبر كل واحد منهما لصاحبه في نوبته، وإن كان ما يسمعه منه شبه الوسواس، لأنهما متساويان في حق المناوبة، فمن لم يصبر منهما لصاحبه، فقد قطع عليه حقه، ولأن ما يظنه السامع وسواسا ربما يكون هو موضع الالتباس والشبهة عنده فلابد من الصبر على سماعه ليصير عنده معلوم الأول والآخر، ثم يتكلم فيه بعده على حقه، وإن كان فيما يصح العلم به صنوان، كان فيما لا يفهم منه شيء بيَّن في نوبته أنه مما لا يلزمه الكلام عليه. ومتى لم يصبر له خصمه، بل داخله بالاعتراض أو الجواب في نوبته، احتمله ووعظه، فإن أصر عليه قطع مكالمته وإن حكم الجاهل من الحاضرين عليه بالانقطاع، لأنه إنما افترض عليه الكشف عن الحق والأمر بالمعروف مع القدرة، ذلك ولا سبيل إليه مع التقطيع، بل يصح مع حسن الاستماع (3) إن حرمان أحد طرفي الجدل من التعبير عن رأيه، إلى جانب أنه ظلم له، هو كذلك ظلم للعملية الجدلية نفسها، حيث نحرم الموضوع من التكامل ونحرم
العلم من الوضوح ونحرم الفكر من الثراء والتنوع. لهذا كان لابد لكل من يدخل عملية الجدل أن يعرف قدر الطرف الآخر فلا يقاطعه في كلامه ولا يضيق عليه في التعبير عن رأيه . قال الباجي : ويقبل على خصمه، فإنه أحسن في الأدب، ويحسن الاستماع إلى كلامه فإنه ربما بان له في كلامه ما رآه دليلا له على فساده، فيكون له عونا على نظره ولا يسمح في النظر ولا يثق بقوته وضعف خصمه، فإن ذلك يفضي إلى الضعف والانقطاع، ولا يداخله في نوبته ويصبر له حتى يفرغ من كلامه، فإن المداخلة تذهب بالفائدة وتدعو إلى الوحشة، ويجتنب إظهار العجب من كلام خصمه والتشنيع عليه في جداله، فإن ذلك يفعله الضعفاء ومن لا إنصاف عنده» (1)
وقال ابن عقيل: «في التقطيع على الخصم في الجدل خروج عن حكم الجدل، إذ كان لا سبيل له إلى فهم الشبهة ليحلها والحجة ليصير إليها إلا بالفهم، والتقطيع مانع من الفهم والتفهم فلا ينبغي أن يشغله عما لا غنى له عنه، كما لا يجوز له أن يشعث عليه أداة من أدوات كلامه وهو دون الفهم، فأولى أن لا يشعث أداة الفهم
وقال الخطيب البغدادي : إذا وقع له شيء في أول كلام الخصم، فلا يعجل بالحكم به فربما كان في آخره ما يبين أن الغرض بخلاف الواقع له، فينبغي أن يتثبت إلى أن ينقضي الكلام (3) إن السماح للمخالف بالتعبير عن رأيه كاملا، إلى جانب إفادته لهذا الطرف في الإيضاح الكامل لرأيه والعرض المفصل لحججه يفيد أيضا مخالفه في إدراك أسلوب تفكيره وطريقة فهمه للأدلة والحجج وربما كان ذلك معينا له في الرد عليه والتفنيد لحججه وأدلته .
قال الخطيب البغدادي: ينبغي أن يكون كل واحد من الخصمين مقبلا على
Debate
303
And He directed the believers to listen to speech completely to take the best of it: '... those who listen to speech and follow the best of it...' (1).
•
And the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) applied that, so he would listen attentively to his companions until the speaker finished his speech, then he would begin to speak. That is well-known and famous from his meetings, his answers to questions of questioners, and the like, from his biography which we are commanded to follow. (2) Indeed, allowing each party to express his opinion and being patient with him until he finishes his speech, besides being respect for the other opinion, also has a benefit for debate: clarity of vision for each party regarding what the other party sees. Perhaps both parties hold the same opinion on the matter while they think they disagree, so the complete expression of the opinion is a way to reveal this agreement after the belief of disagreement. Al-Juwayni said: 'Both of them must each be patient with the other in his turn, even if what he hears from him resembles whispers (waswasa), because they are equal in the right of alternation (al-munawaba). Whoever among them is not patient with his partner has cut off his right. And because what the listener thinks is a whisper might be the place of confusion and doubt for him, so he must be patient in hearing it so that it becomes known to him from beginning to end. Then he speaks about it in his turn appropriately. If it is something sound in knowledge, it is sound; if it is something from which nothing is understood, he makes clear in his turn that it is not incumbent upon him to speak about it. And if his opponent is not patient with him but interrupts him with objection or answer in his turn, he should bear it and admonish him. If he persists, he cuts off his conversation, even if an ignorant person among the attendees judges that he is defeated, because he is only required to uncover the truth and enjoin good with ability, and that is not possible with interruption, but rather with good listening.' (3) Indeed, depriving one of the two parties of debate from expressing his opinion, besides being an injustice to him, is also an injustice to the debate process itself, as we deprive the subject of completeness, and deprive knowledge of clarity, and deprive thought of richness and diversity. Therefore, it is necessary for everyone who enters the debate process to know the status of the other party, so he does not interrupt him in his speech nor restrict him in expressing his opinion. Al-Baji said: 'He should face his opponent, for that is better in etiquette. He should listen well to his speech, for perhaps there becomes clear to him in his speech what he sees as evidence for its invalidity, thus it becomes an aid for his reflection. He should not be lax in reflection nor trust in his own strength and the weakness of his opponent, for that leads to weakness and defeat. He should not interrupt him in his turn and be patient until he finishes his speech, for interruption removes benefit and causes alienation. He should avoid showing astonishment at his opponent's speech and denouncing him in his debate, for that is done by the weak and those who have no fairness.' (1)
Ibn 'Aqil said: 'Interrupting the opponent in debate is a departure from the rule of debate. Since he has no way to understand the doubt to solve it and the proof to arrive at it except through understanding, and interruption prevents understanding and comprehension, he should not busy himself with what he can do without. Just as it is not permissible for him to disrupt any tool of his speech while he is without understanding, so it is even more appropriate that he does not disrupt the tool of understanding.'
Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi said: 'If something occurs to him at the beginning of the opponent's speech, he should not rush to judge it, for perhaps at the end there is something clarifying that the purpose is contrary to what occurred to him. He should be deliberate until the speech concludes.' (3) Indeed, allowing the dissenter to fully express his opinion, besides benefiting that party in fully clarifying his opinion and detailed presentation of his proofs, also benefits his opponent in perceiving his style of thinking and method of understanding evidence and proofs. Perhaps that is a help for him in refuting him and invalidating his proofs and evidence.
Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi said: 'Each of the two opponents should face the other.'
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage discusses the etiquette of listening in the context of jadal (debate). It begins by citing the Quranic directive to listen to speech fully to choose the best, and the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in letting speakers finish before responding. The author then explains the benefit: allowing full expression can reveal that the two parties actually agree, thinking they differ. Al-Juwayni's quote emphasizes patience in each speaker's turn, even if the speech seems like waswasa (confused whispers), because what appears as confusion might be the actual point of doubt. He stresses equal right to alternation, and that if an opponent interrupts, one should first bear and admonish, and if persistence continues, cut off conversation, even if onlookers judge defeat. The author then notes that depriving a party of speech is injustice to that party and to the debate process itself, depriving the subject of completeness and knowledge of clarity. Next, al-Baji advises facing the opponent, listening well as it may reveal flaws in his argument, not being overconfident, avoiding interruption, and avoiding disparagement. Ibn 'Aqil states that interruption prevents understanding of doubts and proofs, so it is a departure from debate rules. Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi counsels not to judge the opponent's argument prematurely, as later parts may clarify. The passage concludes by reiterating that allowing full expression helps the opponent understand the other's thinking and refute effectively.

Rules of Conduct in Dialectical Debate
Author presents adab rules for jadal: listening, non-interruption, fairness in quoting opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of jadal conduct to assess whether a given debate scenario follows proper Islamic dialectical etiquette.

Core Idea
Good jadal is not about winning—it's about listening, fairness, and truth.

The Islamic tradition of dialectical debate (jadal) is not a free-for-all. It is governed by strict ethical rules that ensure the pursuit of truth rather than ego. This passage compiles rules from prominent scholars like Al-Baji and Al-Juwayni, showing how proper conduct is essential for reaching sound conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule being prescribed
Read each scholar's statement and extract the explicit rule of conduct. For example, Al-Baji says to face your opponent and listen well.
Look for imperatives or prohibitions.
2
Understand the rationale
Each rule is accompanied by a reason. For instance, interruption 'prevents understanding the fallacy and the proof' (Ibn 'Aqil).
The rationale shows the rule's function in the pursuit of truth.
3
Compare rules across scholars
Notice how different scholars emphasize different aspects: Al-Baji focuses on external conduct, Al-Khatib on patience, Al-Juwayni on fairness in quoting.
This shows that adab covers both demeanor and content.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
Take a hypothetical debate scenario and check which rules are followed or violated. For example, if a speaker cuts off his opponent, which Ibn 'Aqil rule is violated?
This step bridges theory to practice.

قال الباجي : ويقبل على خصمه، فإنه أحسن في الأدب، ويحسن الاستماع إلى كلامه فإنه ربما بان له في كلامه ما رآه دليلا له على فساده، فيكون له عونا على نظره ولا يسمح في النظر ولا يثق بقوته وضعف خصمه، فإن ذلك يفضي إلى الضعف والانقطاع، ولا يداخله في نوبته ويصبر له حتى يفرغ من كلامه، فإن المداخلة تذهب بالفائدة وتدعو إلى الوحشة، ويجتنب إظهار العجب من كلام خصمه والتشنيع عليه في جداله، فإن ذلك يفعله الضعفاء ومن لا إنصاف عنده». وقال ابن عقيل: «في التقطيع على الخصم في الجدل خروج عن حكم الجدل، إذ كان لا سبيل له إلى فهم الشبهة ليحلها والحجة ليصير إليها إلا بالفهم، والتقطيع مانع من الفهم والتفهم فلا ينبغي أن يشغله عما لا غنى له عنه، كما لا يجوز له أن يشعث عليه أداة من أدوات كلامه وهو دون الفهم، فأولى أن لا يشعث أداة الفهم». وقال الخطيب البغدادي : إذا وقع له شيء في أول كلام الخصم، فلا يعجل بالحكم به فربما كان في آخره ما يبين أن الغرض بخلاف الواقع له، فينبغي أن يتثبت إلى أن ينقضي الكلام. إن السماح للمخالف بالتعبير عن رأيه كاملا، إلى جانب إفادته لهذا الطرف في الإيضاح الكامل لرأيه والعرض المفصل لحججه يفيد أيضا مخالفه في إدراك أسلوب تفكيره وطريقة فهمه للأدلة والحجج وربما كان ذلك معينا له في الرد عليه والتفنيد لحججه وأدلته. قال الخطيب البغدادي: ينبغي أن يكون كل واحد من الخصمين مقبلا على صاحبه بوجهه في حال مناظرته، مستمعًا كلامه إلى أن ينهيه، فإن ذلك طريق معرفته والوقوف على حقيقته، وربما كان في كلامه ما يدله على فساده وينبهه على عواره، فيكون ذلك معونة له على جوابه». إن كل هذا يقتضي أن يتناول كلا طرفي الجدل التعبير عن رأييهما بالتناوب، يتم لكل منهما ذلك حتى يفرغ مما يريد التعبير عنه، وبذلك وحده يحقق الجدل غايته ويصل إلى نتيجته. قال الطوفي: «وليتناوبا الكلام مناوبة لا مناهبة بحيث ينصت المعترض للمستدل حتى يفرغ وتقريره للدليل، ثم المستدل للمعترض حتى يقرر اعتراضه، ولا يقطع أحد منهما على الآخر كلامه في إثباته وإن فهم مقصوده من بعضه». 8 - صدق كل منهما في ما يحكيه من مذهب الآخر : أحيانا يأتي الجدل في سياق الدرس أو المحاضرة بأن يقرر باحث رأيًا في من المسائل ثم يذكر أن هناك رأيا مخالفًا، فيقرر هذا الرأي ثم يذكر حججه وأدلته وأوجه الاستدلال بها ويكر عليها بالرد والنقض ؛ فهذا في حقيقته نوع من المسألة محل الدرس أو المحاضرة ؛ وهنا ينبغي على الباحث أن يكون منصفا في ما ينقل، فيأتي بالرأي المخالف كاملا كما هو دون إضافة أو نقصان، أو تبديل أو تغيير ويعرض حججه أيضًا بوضوح ودونما إبهام. قال إمام الحرمين الجويني : «عليهما أن ينصفا في حكاية كل واحد منهما كلام صاحبه من غير زيادة ولا نقصان إلا فيما يرجع إلى تطويل وتكرير في العبارة، فإن حذف ما هو حشو وفضل في الكلام لا يعود بتحصيل في معنى ولا فائدة، واجب عليهما». لقد كان الأصوليون على جانب واسع من سعة الصدر، ولم يكونوا يجدون أي حرج في نقل مذاهب مخالفيهم بأدلتها كاملة دون نقص. فهم يلتزمون بعرض المذاهب في المسألة موضع الخلاف، ثم بيان أدلتها
Al-Baji said: He should face his opponent, for that is better in etiquette, and he should listen well to his words, for perhaps in his words there appears to him something he sees as evidence for its corruption, thus it becomes an aid for him in his consideration. He should not be lenient in consideration nor rely on his own strength and the weakness of his opponent, for that leads to weakness and disconnection. He should not interrupt him during his turn, and should be patient until he finishes his speech, for interruption removes benefit and invites alienation. He should avoid showing amazement at his opponent's speech and vilifying him in debate, for that is done by the weak and those without fairness. Ibn 'Aqil said: Interrupting the opponent in debate is a departure from the rule of debate, since he has no way to understand the fallacy to solve it and the proof to reach it except through understanding, and interruption prevents understanding and comprehension. So he should not occupy himself with what he cannot do without, just as it is not permissible for him to disturb any tool of his speech while he is below understanding, so how much more should he not disturb the tool of understanding. Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi said: If something occurs to him at the beginning of the opponent's speech, he should not rush to judge it, for perhaps in the end there is something showing that the intent is contrary to what occurred to him, so he should be deliberate until the speech ends. Allowing the opponent to fully express his opinion, besides benefiting this party in fully clarifying his opinion and detailed presentation of his proofs, also benefits his opponent in perceiving his thinking style and method of understanding evidence and proofs, and perhaps that helps him in refuting and demolishing his arguments and proofs. Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi said: Each of the two opponents should face the other with his face during his debate, listening to his speech until he finishes it, for that is the way to know him and grasp his reality, and perhaps in his speech there is something that indicates its corruption and alerts him to its flaw, thus becoming an aid for him in his response. All this requires that both parties to the debate express their opinions alternately, each completing what he wishes to express, and by that alone debate achieves its purpose and reaches its conclusion. Al-Tufi said: They should alternate speech in turn, not snatching, such that the objector listens to the arguer until he finishes and establishes the proof, then the arguer listens to the objector until he establishes his objection, and neither cuts off the other's speech in establishing it, even if he understands his intent from some of it. 8 - Truthfulness of each in what he relates of the opponent's doctrine: Sometimes debate occurs in the context of a lesson or lecture, where a researcher states an opinion on some issue, then mentions that there is an opposing opinion, then states that opinion, then mentions its proofs, evidence, and methods of inference, then reviews them with refutation and rebuttal; this is in reality a type of issue under study or lecture. Here the researcher should be fair in what he transmits, presenting the opposing opinion fully as it is without addition or omission, alteration or change, and also present its proofs clearly and without obscurity. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said: They both must be fair in each one's narration of his companion's speech without increase or decrease, except regarding prolixity and repetition in expression, for deleting what is superfluous and excessive in speech that does not result in meaning or benefit is obligatory upon them. The usul scholars had great open-mindedness, and they did not find any difficulty in transmitting the doctrines of their opponents with their complete proofs without deficiency. They committed themselves to presenting the doctrines on the issue under dispute, then explaining their proofs.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rules of conduct in dialectical debate (adab al-jadal) drawn from several classical scholars: al-Baji, Ibn 'Aqil, al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, al-Tufi, and al-Juwayni. The author is not simply quoting but synthesizing these rules into a coherent set of ethical guidelines for debate. The passage opens with al-Baji's advice: the debater should face his opponent, listen attentively, and not interrupt. The rationale is that listening may reveal flaws in the opponent's argument, aiding in refutation. Interruption is condemned because it prevents understanding and leads to alienation. Then Ibn 'Aqil emphasizes that interrupting prevents grasping the fallacy or proof, so it is a violation of the very purpose of debate. Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi adds that one should not rush to judge based on initial statements, as later parts may clarify intent. Allowing the opponent full expression helps both sides: the speaker clarifies his view, and the listener understands his reasoning, which aids in crafting a proper response. Al-Tufi stresses strict alternation: the objector listens to the arguer's proof, then the arguer listens to the objection; no one cuts off the other even if they think they already understood. Finally, the passage addresses truthfulness in quoting the opponent's doctrine. In academic settings, a researcher must present the opposing view fully and fairly, without distortion. Al-Juwayni mandates fairness in narration, allowing only the deletion of repetitive or irrelevant filler. The passage concludes that usul scholars were broad-minded and scrupulously accurate in transmitting opponents' views along with their proofs. This rule is essential in jadal: misrepresenting the opponent is both unethical and counterproductive, for a weak misrepresentation is easily refuted, while a strong accurate one tests one's own position. The entire passage underscores that jadal is a cooperative search for truth, not a zero-sum contest.

Key Terms
جَدَل (jadal): Dialectical debate aimed at establishing truth through structured exchange of arguments and objections, governed by ethical rules. [Note: In earlier usul works, jadal often refers to a specific method of disputation; later it becomes a broader term for any reasoned debate.]
خَصْم (khasm): The opposing party in a debate; one's interlocutor. [Note: Stable term; no significant shift.]
حِكَايَة (hikāyah): The narration or transmission of an opponent's doctrine or argument, which must be done accurately and fairly. [Note: In jadal literature, hikayah often implies a precise quotation; in other contexts it can mean any report.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn 'Aqil, why is interrupting the opponent in jadal a departure from the rule of debate?
A) Because it shows disrespect
B) Because it prevents understanding the fallacy and the proof
C) Because it wastes time
D) Because it is forbidden by the ruler

Key Takeaway
The adab rules of jadal transform debate from a contest into a cooperative search for truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Turn-Taking and Fairness in Jadal
Author outlines rules of turn-taking and fairness in dialectical debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the two core principles of jadal: turn-taking (al-munawaba) and fairness (al-insaf), and describe their role in achieving truthful debate.

Core Idea
Jadal is a cooperative truth-seeking process governed by strict turn-taking and fair representation.

This lesson explains the fundamental etiquettes of dialectical debate as taught by classical scholars. You will learn why uninterrupted turn-taking and honest transmission of the opponent's view are essential for reaching the truth, and you will see these principles articulated by al-Tufi and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first principle: al-munawaba
Read the opening of the passage: why must both parties alternate speech? What does al-Tufi say about listening and interrupting?
Turn-taking prevents domination and ensures each side is fully heard.
2
Identify the second principle: al-insaf
Find the part about honesty in transmitting the opponent's doctrine. What does the author require when presenting a contrary opinion? What does al-Juwayni allow as the only alteration?
Fairness means no addition, deletion, or distortion; only removal of redundancy is permitted.
3
Connect principles to the goal of jadal
Look at the sentence: 'وبذلك وحده يحقق الجدل غايته ويصل إلى نتيجته'. How do these rules help jadal achieve its aim?
The goal is truth, not victory; fair process leads to sound conclusion.
4
Apply to a hypothetical scenario
Imagine a debate where one interrupts the other: which rule is broken? If one misrepresents the opponent's argument, what is violated?
Interrupting breaks al-munawaba; misrepresentation breaks al-insaf.

إن كل هذا يقتضي أن يتناول كلا طرفي الجدل التعبير عن رأييهما بالتناوب، يتم لكل منهما ذلك حتى يفرغ مما يريد التعبير عنه، وبذلك وحده يحقق الجدل غايته ويصل إلى نتيجته . وأن مسألة الجدل في قال الطوفي: «وليتناوبا الكلام مناوبة لا مناهبة بحيث ينصت المعترض للمستدل حتى يفرغ وتقريره للدليل، ثم المستدل للمعترض حتى يقرر اعتراضه، ولا يقطع أحد منهما على الآخر كلامه في إثباته وإن فهم مقصوده من بعضه»(2) 8 - صدق كل منهما في ما يحكيه من مذهب الآخر : أحيانا يأتي الجدل في سياق الدرس أو المحاضرة بأن يقرر باحث رأيًا في من المسائل ثم يذكر أن هناك رأيا مخالفًا، فيقرر هذا الرأي ثم يذكر حججه وأدلته وأوجه الاستدلال بها ويكر عليها بالرد والنقض ؛ فهذا في حقيقته نوع من المسألة محل الدرس أو المحاضرة ؛ وهنا ينبغي على الباحث أن يكون منصفا في ما ينقل، فيأتي بالرأي المخالف كاملا كما هو دون إضافة أو نقصان، أو تبديل أو تغيير ويعرض حججه أيضًا بوضوح ودونما إبهام. قال إمام الحرمين الجويني : «عليهما أن ينصفا في حكاية كل واحد منهما كلام صاحبه من غير زيادة ولا نقصان إلا فيما يرجع إلى تطويل وتكرير في العبارة، فإن حذف ما هو حشو وفضل في الكلام لا يعود بتحصيل في معنى ولا فائدة، واجب عليهما» (3) لقد كان الأصوليون على جانب واسع من سعة الصدر، ولم يكونوا يجدون أي حرج في نقل مذاهب مخالفيهم بأدلتها كاملة دون نقص . فهم يلتزمون بعرض المذاهب في المسألة موضع الخلاف، ثم بيان أدلتها والأصول التي تنطلق منها ، ثم الرد عليها بهدم تلك الأدلة، وأخيرًا اختيار الراجح من تلك الآراء أو الخروج برأي جديد. من ذلك أن إمام الحرمين حين عقد مسألة في صيغة الأمر» طرح موضوع المسألة أولا، ثم نقل مذاهب العلماء فيها مجملة وبعد أن أتم البيان المجمل لتلك المذاهب شرع في البيان التفصيلي لها، فقال: والذي يقتضيه الترتيب المفضي إلى درك الحق؛ البداية بذكر متعلق مذهب الواقفية والاعتراض عليه، ثم إذا نجز أتبعنا مذهب المعتزلة بالرد عليهم، ثم نذكر معتصم الفقهاء، ثم نختم القول بالحق المبين . فجعل يستعرض أدلة المذاهب ويرد عليها واحدا فواحد، حتى انتهى منها، ليصل إلى نتيجة قررها في قوله : وقد تعين الآن أن نبوح الحق ونقول : افعل طلب محض لا مساغ فيه لتقدير الترك، فهذا مقتضى اللفظ المجرد من القرائن (1) . ومن ذلك أيضًا أن الإمام الرازي عند تناوله لمسألة «فعل الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم - بمجرده - هل يدل على حكم في حقنا أم لا؟ أورد الأقوال الأربعة في المسألة مفصلة ومنسوبة إلى قائليها ثم أورد أدلة كل فريق مفصلة مقرونة بأوجه دلالتها عنده، وأجاب عنها واحدا واحدا وانتهى إلى اختيار الرأي الرابع وهو التوقف» (2)
All of this requires that both parties to the debate express their views alternately, each completing what he wants to say, and only by this does the debate achieve its goal and reach its conclusion. Al-Tufi said: 'Let them alternate speech alternately, not seize, so that the objector listens to the arguer until he finishes and settles the proof, then the arguer listens to the objector until he settles his objection, and neither interrupts the other in his speech even if he understands his intent from some of it.' (2) Honesty of each in what he relates of the other's doctrine: Sometimes debate occurs in the context of a lesson or lecture where a researcher states an opinion on an issue, then mentions there is a contrary opinion, states this opinion, then mentions its evidence and arguments and the ways of inference, and then refutes it; this is in reality a type of the issue under study. Here the researcher must be fair in what he transmits, bringing the contrary opinion completely as it is without addition or deletion, alteration or change, and presenting its evidence clearly and without ambiguity. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said: 'They must be fair in each one's account of the other's speech without addition or deletion except regarding prolongation and repetition in expression; for removing what is superfluous and excess speech that does not return with acquisition of meaning or benefit is obligatory upon them.' (3) The legal theorists were of great open-mindedness and did not find any difficulty in transmitting the schools of their opponents with their evidence completely without deficiency. They commit to presenting the schools on the issue of disagreement, then explaining their evidence and the principles from which they proceed, then refuting them by demolishing that evidence, and finally choosing the strongest of those opinions or producing a new opinion. An example is that al-Juwayni, when he discussed the issue of the command form, first raised the subject of the issue, then transmitted the scholars' schools summarily, and after completing the summary exposition of those schools, he began the detailed exposition, saying: 'What the order leading to attainment of truth requires is: beginning with mentioning the position of the Waqufiyya and objecting to it, then when finished, we follow with the school of the Mu'tazila by refuting them, then we mention the stronghold of the fuqaha', then we conclude with the clear truth.' So he proceeded to present the evidence of the schools and refute them one by one until he finished, reaching a conclusion stated in his words: 'It has now become clear that we must disclose the truth and say: 'Do' is a pure demand with no room for permitting omission; this is the requirement of the bare expression without context.' (1) Also, al-Razi, when he addressed the issue of the Prophet's action alone indicating a ruling for us or not, presented the four opinions on the issue in detail attributed to their proponents, then presented the evidence of each group in detail coupled with their manner of inference, and responded to them one by one, ending with choosing the fourth opinion, which is suspension of judgment. (2)
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage discusses the essential etiquettes and procedures of jadal (dialectical debate). It begins by stating that for debate to achieve its purpose, both parties must express their views alternately without interruption. The author then quotes al-Tufi, who emphasizes that the objector should listen to the arguer until the arguer finishes presenting his proof, and then the arguer should listen to the objector's objection; neither should cut off the other's speech. This ensures each side is fully heard. Next, the passage discusses the principle of honesty (sidq) in transmitting the opponent's doctrine. When a researcher presents a contrary opinion in a lecture, he must be fair, conveying the opinion completely without alteration, and presenting its evidence clearly. This is confirmed by a quote from Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, who states that both parties must be fair in relating each other's speech, with the only exception being the removal of redundant or lengthy expressions that add no meaning. The author then remarks that the classical legal theorists (usuliyyun) were open-minded and did not hesitate to transmit the opinions of their opponents with full evidence. Their method was to present all schools of thought on a disputed issue, explain their evidence and principles, then refute that evidence, and finally choose the strongest opinion or produce a new one. The passage provides two concrete examples. First, al-Juwayni's treatment of the command form: he summarized the schools, then gave a detailed exposition, systematically presenting and refuting the evidence of each group (Waqufiyya, Mu'tazila, fuqaha') and concluding with his own position that the command is a pure demand with no room for omission. Second, al-Razi, when discussing whether the Prophet's action alone indicates a ruling for us, presented all four detailed opinions, their evidence, and then refuted each point by point, ultimately adopting the fourth opinion of suspension (tawaqquf). These examples illustrate the principles of fairness, thoroughness, and systematic refutation in jadal.

Key Terms
الجدل
الإنصاف
المناوبة

A) Adding explanations for clarity
B) Removing redundant and lengthy expressions that add no meaning
C) Correcting errors in evidence
D) Summarizing the main points

Key Takeaway
Fairness and uninterrupted turn-taking are the pillars of productive dialectical debate.

?
Imagine you are in a jadal session. The arguer starts but you believe you already know his point. Should you interrupt to speed up the discussion? Explain why or why not based on al-Tufi's statement.

Applying the Rules: The Method of Al-Juwayni and Al-Razi
Author illustrates the usuli method of presenting and refuting opinions with examples from al-Juwayni and al-Razi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe how al-Juwayni and al-Razi applied the principles of fairness and systematic refutation in their usul al-fiqh works.

Core Idea
Classical usulis demonstrated the rules of jadal by presenting opposing views fully, refuting them one by one, and reaching a reasoned conclusion.

This lesson illustrates how the theoretical rules of turn-taking and fairness were practiced by two great scholars, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni and Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. Their methodical approach to presenting and refuting opposing views serves as a model for rigorous debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general method described by the author
Read the opening of this section: what are the steps that the usuliyyun follow? List them: presenting all schools, explaining evidence, refuting, choosing strongest.
This method ensures each position is given its due.
2
Analyze al-Juwayni’s example
How did al-Juwayni structure his discussion of the command form? Note the order: Waqufiyya, Mu'tazila, fuqaha', then his conclusion. Why this order?
He refuted each in turn, building towards his own position.
3
Analyze al-Razi’s example
How did al-Razi handle the topic of the Prophet’s action? What were the four opinions? What was his final choice (tawaqquf)?
He presented all evidence and responded point by point.
4
Compare the two examples
What common features do you see? How do they embody the rules from Lesson 1 (turn-taking and fairness) even in written form?
Both give full voice to opponents and respond systematically.

The Structured Method of Jadal in Usul al-Fiqh: Juwayni and Razi
Author illustrates the structured dialectical methodology of Imam al-Haramayn and Fakhr al-Din al-Razi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the steps of dialectical methodology used by al-Juwayni and al-Razi in usul al-fiqh debates.

Core Idea
Jadal follows a disciplined order: present doctrines, refute each, then unveil the truth.

This lesson examines how two giants of usul al-fiqh, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni and Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, structured their dialectical engagements. The passage from the coursebook shows that their debates were not random but followed a precise sequence that ensured systematic treatment of all positions. Understanding this order will help you see how usuli scholars arrived at their conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the debate
Find what issue al-Juwayni and al-Razi were addressing. For al-Juwayni it was 'the form of command' (sigha al-amr); for al-Razi it was 'the Prophet's action indicating a ruling for us'.
Each debate begins with a clear question.
2
Recognize the presentation of doctrines
Note how they first summarized all positions before detailed refutation. Al-Juwayni summarized, then detailed; al-Razi listed four opinions with their proponents.
This ensures all views are on the table.
3
Observe the order of refutation
Al-Juwayni's order: Wa'ifiyya, then Mu'tazila, then jurists. Al-Razi presented proofs of each group and responded one by one.
Systematic refutation prevents oversight.
4
Identify the conclusion
See how they reached a decided position after refuting others. Al-Juwayni concluded that 'Do' is a pure request; al-Razi chose suspension (tawaqquf).
The conclusion is the result of the structured process.

من ذلك أن إمام الحرمين حين عقد مسألة في صيغة الأمر» طرح موضوع المسألة أولا، ثم نقل مذاهب العلماء فيها مجملة وبعد أن أتم البيان المجمل لتلك المذاهب شرع في البيان التفصيلي لها، فقال: والذي يقتضيه الترتيب المفضي إلى درك الحق؛ البداية بذكر متعلق مذهب الواقفية والاعتراض عليه، ثم إذا نجز أتبعنا مذهب المعتزلة بالرد عليهم، ثم نذكر معتصم الفقهاء، ثم نختم القول بالحق المبين . فجعل يستعرض أدلة المذاهب ويرد عليها واحدا فواحد، حتى انتهى منها، ليصل إلى نتيجة قررها في قوله : وقد تعين الآن أن نبوح الحق ونقول : افعل طلب محض لا مساغ فيه لتقدير الترك، فهذا مقتضى اللفظ المجرد من القرائن (1) . ومن ذلك أيضًا أن الإمام الرازي عند تناوله لمسألة «فعل الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم - بمجرده - هل يدل على حكم في حقنا أم لا؟ أورد الأقوال الأربعة في المسألة مفصلة ومنسوبة إلى قائليها ثم أورد أدلة كل فريق مفصلة مقرونة بأوجه دلالتها عنده، وأجاب عنها واحدا واحدا وانتهى إلى اختيار الرأي الرابع وهو التوقف» (2) (1) البرهان في أصول الفقه للجويني، ج 1، ص : 156 - 163 ، انظر - كمثال - أيضًا : ج: 1، ص : 168 - 178 . (2) انظر المحصول، ج 3، ص : 229 - 247 : .
Among that is that Imam al-Haramayn, when he held a discussion on the issue of 'the form of command', first presented the subject matter, then transmitted the doctrines of the scholars concerning it in summary, and after completing the summary exposition of those doctrines he began the detailed exposition, saying: 'What is required by the arrangement that leads to attaining the truth: beginning with mentioning the position of the Wa'ifiyya and objecting to it, then when completed we follow it with the doctrine of the Mu'tazila and refute them, then we mention the stronghold of the jurists, then conclude with the clear truth.' So he began to review the proofs of the doctrines and refute them one by one, until he finished them, arriving at a conclusion which he stated in his words: 'It has now become evident that we should declare the truth and say: 'Do' is a pure request with no room for stipulating omission; this is the requirement of the word stripped of contextual evidence.' (1) And also that Imam al-Razi, when he dealt with the issue of 'the action of the Messenger (peace be upon him) by itself — does it indicate a ruling for us or not?' He presented the four opinions on the issue in detail, attributed to their proponents, then presented the proofs of each group in detail, accompanied by their aspects of indication according to him, and answered them one by one, and ended with choosing the fourth opinion, which is suspension (2). (1) Al-Burhan fi Usul al-Fiqh by al-Juwayni, vol. 1, pp. 156-163, see also as an example: vol. 1, pp. 168-178. (2) See al-Mahsul, vol. 3, pp. 229-247.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples from the works of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni and Fakhr al-Din al-Razi to illustrate the structured methodology followed by usul scholars in dialectical debates. The author first cites al-Juwayni's handling of the issue of 'the form of command' (sigha al-amr). Al-Juwayni began by presenting the subject matter, then summarized the various doctrines. He then detailed his approach: first mention the doctrine of the Wa'ifiyya and object to it, then the Mu'tazila and refute them, then the jurists (fuqaha), and finally conclude with the clear truth. He proceeded to review the proofs one by one, refuting each, until he reached his conclusion. This demonstrates a systematic order. Similarly, the author cites al-Razi's treatment of the issue of whether the Prophet's action alone indicates a ruling for us. Al-Razi listed the four opinions with their proponents, presented detailed proofs for each, responded to them, and chose the fourth opinion (tawaqquf, suspension). Both examples show that jadal in usul al-fiqh is not arbitrary but follows a rigorous step-by-step process of presenting, analyzing, and refuting. The footnotes direct the reader to the original sources for further reference.

The First Requirement of Jadal: Knowledge of the Subject Matter
Author explains the first etiquette: debaters must have knowledge of the subject matter under discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why knowledge of the subject matter is essential for productive jadal and distinguish between substantive debate and empty verbal dispute.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the subject is the foundation of productive debate; ignorance breeds empty verbal disputes.

In this passage, the author defines the first etiquette (adab) of jadal: debaters must have knowledge of the controversial issue under discussion. Without this, debates degenerate into futile verbal bickering. This lesson will help you understand why this condition is fundamental and how to apply it in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of jadal
According to the passage, what is the subject (mawdu') of dialectic? Find the definition: it is the controversial issue (al-mas'ala al-khilafiyya) around which the discussion revolves.
This ensures both parties are clear on what is being debated.
2
Recognize the consequence of ignorance
What happens when one or both debaters lack knowledge? The passage describes it as an empty verbal dispute (khussuma lafziyya farigha). Locate this term and note its meaning.
Ignorance leads to debates that are merely shouting matches.
3
Analyze the author's argument
Why does the author claim that knowledge is essential for success? The quote from Fadlallah explains that knowledge enables clarity, calmness, strong argument, and gentle speech. Identify these benefits.
Knowledge allows debaters to engage constructively from start to finish.
4
Apply the principle
In a hypothetical Islamic debate (e.g., on the ruling of a contemporary transaction), how would you ensure both parties have adequate knowledge before engaging? Consider defining key terms and agreeing on the precise issue.
This principle prevents wasted effort and mutual misunderstanding.

المبحث الثالث الآداب المتعلقة بموضوع الجدل موضوع الجدل : هو المسألة الخلافية التي يدور حولها النقاش العلمي بين المتجادلين، والتي يراد بالبحث فيها وإدارة أوجه الحوار حولها الوصول إلى وجه الحق في شأنها ؛ ومن غير المعقول أن يدخل الباحث ميدان الجدل حول مسألة من المسائل دون أن يكون لديه أدنى اطلاع في شأنها أو إحاطة بعناصرها، ولذلك لابد من أن تتوفر في كلا الباحثين جملة من المواصفات فيما يتعلق بموضوع الجدل وطبيعة المسألة التي يدور حولها، وهي: 1- المعرفة بموضوع الجدل : كثيرًا ما نرى صورًا من النزاع الجدلي بين أطراف مختلفة، تبدو في سيرها وتقلبها مجرد خصومة لفظية فارغة، إذ لا يتبين فيها بدقة موضوع الجدل والنقاش، حيث يخوض كل واحد من طرفي الجدل في جانب قد لا يخطر على بال الطرف الآخر أصلا ؛ وقد يكون هذا عائدا - في أغلب أحيانه - إلى جهل أحد الطرفين أو كليهما بموضوع العملية الجدلية وكون أحدهما أو كليهما مجرد متطفل على موضوع العملية الجدلية ومدع للعلم به لا غير، في حين أنه بعيد عنه وقد لا يعرف عنه شيئا في الحقيقة . ولذلك، فلكي تنجح العملية الجدلية وتؤتي ثمراتها «لابد لكل من طرفي الحوار، من التعرف إلى الفكرة التي ينطلقان في طريق إثباتها ونفيها، لأن الجهل بها وبتفاصيلها، يحول الحوار إلى أسلوب من أساليب الشتائم والمهاترات التي يغطي فيها كل منهما ضعفه وعجزه عن الوقوف موقف المدافع القوي عن فكرته، بينما تجعل المعرفة كلا منهما واعيا لما يطرح من فكر ولما يستقبل من فكر، مما يجعله يعرف كيف يبدأ الحوار وكيف يخوض فيه، وكيف ينتهي منه، في وضوح الرؤية، وهدوء الفكر، وقوة ،الحجة، ووداعة الكلمة»(1) فلابد – إذا - من وجود مستوى معين من المعرفة المتعلقة بموضوع المناقشة (1) الحوار في القرآن، لمحمد حسين فضل الله، ج. 1 ص : 50
The third section: Etiquettes related to the subject of dialectic. The subject of dialectic: it is the controversial issue around which the scholarly discussion between the two debaters revolves, and by means of research in it and directing aspects of dialogue around it, the aim is to reach the face of truth concerning it. It is unreasonable for a researcher to enter the arena of dialectic regarding an issue without having at least some familiarity with it or knowledge of its elements. Therefore, both researchers must possess a set of qualifications concerning the subject of dialectic and the nature of the issue around which it revolves, namely: 1. Knowledge of the subject of dialectic. We often see instances of dialectical conflict between different parties that, in their course and fluctuation, appear as mere empty verbal disputes, in which the subject of dialectic and debate is not precisely determined, as each of the two sides of the dialectic delves into an aspect that may not even occur to the other side. This may be due, in most cases, to the ignorance of one or both parties of the subject of the dialectical process, and that one or both are merely intruding on the subject of the dialectical process and claiming knowledge of it without having it, while in reality they are far from it and may know nothing about it. Therefore, for the dialectical process to succeed and bear fruit, 'each of the two sides of the dialogue must familiarize themselves with the idea that they are setting out to prove or disprove, because ignorance of it and its details turns the dialogue into a method of insults and shouting in which each covers their weakness and inability to stand as a strong defender of their idea, while knowledge makes each aware of what is presented of thought and what is received of thought, thus enabling them to know how to begin the dialogue, how to engage in it, and how to conclude it, with clarity of vision, calmness of thought, strength of argument, and gentleness of speech.' (1) Thus, there must be a certain level of knowledge regarding the subject of the discussion. (1) Al-Hiwar fi al-Qur'an by Muhammad Husayn Fadlallah, vol. 1, p. 50.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage defines the first etiquette (adab) of dialectical debate: knowledge of the subject matter. It begins by defining the subject of jadal as the controversial issue (al-mas'ala al-khilafiyya) that is the focus of scholarly discussion. The author then asserts the necessity that both debaters have at least some familiarity with the issue. He explains that often debates devolve into empty verbal disputes (khussuma lafziyya farigha) because one or both parties do not precisely know the subject. They may be intruders claiming knowledge without real understanding. The author then quotes Muhammad Husayn Fadlallah, emphasizing that proper knowledge enables each side to know how to start, engage, and conclude the dialogue effectively, with clarity, calmness, strong argument, and gentle speech. The conclusion reiterates the need for a certain level of knowledge about the topic. This passage sets the foundational condition for productive dialectic: without knowledge, debate becomes mere argumentation without substance.

خصومة لفظية

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of the controversial issue is the first requirement for productive jadal; without it, debate becomes an empty verbal dispute.

Knowledge as a Precondition for Jadal
Author explains why knowledge of the subject is essential for fruitful dialectical debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Qur'anic conditions for legitimate debate (knowledge, guidance, sound book) and apply them to a contemporary Islamic jurisprudential dispute.

Core Idea
Jadal without knowledge is empty hostility; the Qur'an demands 'ilm, huda, and kitab munir.

The author of this passage emphasizes that the first requirement for a successful dialectical debate (jadal) is thorough knowledge of the subject. Without it, debates become pointless shouting matches. He then grounds this requirement in the Qur'an, which condemns those who argue about Allah without knowledge, guidance, or an enlightening book. In this lesson, we will unpack these three conditions and see how they form the ethical foundation of Islamic dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the sentence where the author states that knowledge of the subject is necessary for jadal. Note that he calls it 'unreasonable' (ghayr ma'qul) to enter debate without it.
The claim is in the first paragraph after the definition.
2
Examine the consequence of ignorance
The author describes what happens when one or both parties lack knowledge: the debate becomes 'empty verbal hostility' and 'insults and shouting matches'. Identify the key Arabic phrases for these consequences.
Look for 'khusuma lafziyya farigha' and 'shatā'im wa muhātarāt'.
3
Analyze the Qur'anic verse
The author cites Qur'an 22:8, which condemns arguing about Allah 'without knowledge, guidance, or an enlightening book'. Write down the three Arabic terms: 'ilm, huda, kitab munir.
The verse is used as divine evidence for the necessity of knowledge.
4
Unpack each term
Read the author's commentary on 'ilm (comprehensive correct knowledge), huda (pure thinking free from bias), and kitab munir (a sound book based on critical principles). For each, note what it adds to the concept of legitimate debate.
These three correspond to the epistemic, ethical, and textual foundations of jadal.
5
Apply to a real debate
Imagine two scholars debating the ruling on riba in modern banking. Using the three conditions, assess whether their debate meets the Qur'anic standard. What knowledge would they need? What sources?
This step bridges theory to practice.

The third topic: The etiquettes relating to the subject of dialectic.
The subject of dialectic: It is the controversial issue around which the scholarly discussion between the two disputants revolves, and by researching it and managing the aspects of dialogue around it, the aim is to reach the truth concerning it. It is unreasonable for a researcher to enter the field of dialectic about an issue without having at least the slightest acquaintance with it or familiarity with its elements. Therefore, it is necessary that both researchers possess a set of specifications regarding the subject of dialectic and the nature of the issue under discussion, which are:
1. Knowledge of the subject of dialectic:
We often see forms of dialectical conflict between different parties that appear in their course and fluctuations to be mere empty verbal hostility, since the subject of dialectic and discussion is not precisely identified. Each party dives into an aspect that may not occur to the other party at all. This is often due to ignorance of one or both parties about the subject of the dialectical process, and that one or both of them are merely intruders on the subject of the dialectical process, claiming knowledge of it when in fact they are far from it and may know nothing about it.
Therefore, for the dialectical process to succeed and bear fruit, 'it is necessary for each of the two parties to the dialogue to become acquainted with the idea they are pursuing in the way of proving or disproving it, because ignorance of it and its details turns the dialogue into a style of insults and shouting matches in which each covers his weakness and inability to stand as a strong defender of his idea, while knowledge makes each aware of what he presents and receives, enabling him to know how to begin the dialogue, how to engage in it, and how to conclude it, with clarity of vision, calmness of thought, strength of argument, and gentleness of expression.' (1)
So there must be a certain level of knowledge pertaining to the subject of discussion.
(1) Al-Hiwar fi al-Qur'an, by Muhammad Husayn Fadlallah, vol. 1, p. 50.
(2) We have found in the Noble Qur'an severe condemnation of a group of people whose habit is to argue, dispute, and be stubborn about every subject, whether they have connection or prior knowledge of it or not. Allah the Exalted said: {And among the people is he who disputes about Allah without knowledge, guidance, or an enlightening book.} (Qur'an 22:8). 
Al-'ilm (knowledge) here: is comprehensive knowledge of the characteristics and subtleties of what is being researched, provided that the knowledge is correct and truthful, so no nonsense intrudes upon it, no blasphemy permeates it, and no flawed intention controls its direction.
Al-huda (guidance): is pure thinking free from desire and the bias of partisanship in a framework of good thinking.
Al-kitab al-munir (the enlightening book): is every thought recorded in a sound book based on principles of critical method that guide to truth and correctness, help in perceiving it, and illuminate the knowledge of it in detail and precision, and also instill in the mind a desire to search for causes and reasons and uncover their effects and consequences, and what benefit may come to man from them. (3)
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage defines the subject of dialectic (jadal) and outlines the first essential requirement for a successful dialectical debate: knowledge of the subject. The author begins by defining the subject of dialectic as a controversial issue (mas'ala khilafiyya) around which scholarly discussion revolves, aiming to reach the truth. He then asserts that it is unreasonable to enter debate without at least minimal acquaintance with the issue. The first requirement is 'knowledge of the subject' (ma'rifa bi-mawdu' al-jadal). The author illustrates the consequence of ignorance: debates become empty verbal hostility, with each party talking past the other. Knowledge, on the other hand, empowers each participant to present and receive ideas clearly and effectively. The passage then cites a Qur'anic verse (22:8) condemning those who argue about Allah without knowledge, guidance, or an enlightening book. The author unpacks these three terms: 'ilm (comprehensive, correct knowledge), huda (pure thinking free from bias), and kitab munir (a sound book based on critical principles). This Qur'anic citation reinforces the necessity of knowledge for legitimate debate.

The Subject Must Be Problematic
Author states that the subject of debate must be an issue of genuine disagreement, not self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given topic is suitable for dialectical debate by applying the condition of being problematic (mahall ishkal).

Core Idea
A subject is debatable only if it is not self-evident and the parties disagree.

The second condition for a valid dialectical debate is that the subject must be problematic. This means it cannot be self-evident (badihi) because then both parties would agree immediately, leaving no room for discussion. In this lesson, we will explore what makes a topic problematic and how this shapes the roles of the arguer (mustadill) and objector (naqid) in debate.

Guided Path
1
Define 'problematic' (mahall ishkal)
Identify the key characteristics: the subject must cause disagreement between the participants, and it must not be self-evident. The passage states that a problem exists only in what is not self-evident or where knowledge differs.
2
Distinguish self-evident from non-self-evident
Self-evident propositions (badihiyyat) are immediately known and accepted by all, such as 'the whole is greater than the part'. Non-self-evident topics are those that require proof or investigation. Think of examples from Islamic sciences, like theological issues debated among schools.
3
Apply the condition to a sample topic
Choose a topic from fiqh or kalam and test it: Is it self-evident? Do scholars disagree? For example, 'Is qiyas a valid source of law?' is not self-evident and is debated, so it qualifies as mahall ishkal.
Self-evident topics like 'murder is wrong' are generally agreed upon and thus not debatable.
4
Identify the roles of mustadill and naqid
Once the subject is problematic, the arguer (mustadill) presents proofs, and the objector (naqid) critiques. Their differing views on the subject make the exchange dialectical.

Commentary
This passage presents the second requirement for a valid dialectical debate: the subject must be problematic (mahall ishkal). The author begins by linking this to the first requirement (knowledge), stating that just as the subject must be known, it must also be an issue of genuine disagreement. He quotes a source that explains that dialogue can only be provoked between the arguer (mustadill) and the objector (naqid) if the topic is problematic, meaning the views of the two parties do not coincide. The passage clarifies that a problem exists only in matters that are not self-evident (badihi) or where the knowledge of the two parties differs. In self-evident matters, both parties would immediately agree, so no discussion occurs. This condition ensures that debate is meaningful and not trivial.

Three Conditions for Permissible Jadal: Knowledge, Guidance, Enlightening Book
Author cites Quranic verse and explains key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions mentioned in the Qur'an for permissible argumentation about Allah and explain each in your own words.

Core Idea
Argument without knowledge, guidance, and sound principles is condemned, not argument itself.

The Qur'an does not forbid all disputation; it condemns disputation without proper foundation. In this lesson, we examine the verse that sets the conditions for valid jadal, as explained by the author. Understanding these conditions helps us distinguish legitimate scholarly debate from arrogant contention.

Guided Path
1
Locate the verse and its context
Find the Qur'anic verse quoted. Note that it condemns a specific type of person: one who argues about Allah without three things.
The verse is from Surah Luqman or al-Hajj? Actually the verse is similar to 22:8 but here it's about arguing about Allah. Check tafsir for exact location.
2
Identify the three missing elements
Extract 'ilm, huda, kitab munir. Write them down as the conditions that are absent.
3
Understand the author's definition of each
Read the author's explanation for each term. For 'ilm: comprehensive, accurate, truthful knowledge. For huda: pure thought free from bias. For kitab munir: recorded thought based on critical principles.
The author expands the meanings beyond the literal.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what kind of argumentation would satisfy all three conditions. How does this relate to the rules of jadal in the Islamic tradition?
This sets a high bar for valid theological debate.

وقد وجدنا في القرآن الكريم تشنيعا شديدًا على فئة من الناس ديدنها المجادلة والمنازعة والمكابرة في كل موضوع سواء كانت لها به صلة أو معرفة سابقة، أو لم تكن لها به أي صلة أو معرفة سابقة. قال تعالى: ﴿وَمِنَ النَّاسِ مَن يُجَدِلُ فِي اللهِ بِغَيْرِ عِلْمٍ وَلَا هُدًى وَلَا كِتَبٍ مُّنِيرٍ﴾ فالعلم هنا : هو المعرفة الشاملة بخصائص ودقائق ما يبحث فيه، على أن تكون المعرفة صحيحة صادقة، فلا لغو يرين عليها ولا تجديف يتخللها، ولا قصد مدخول يتحكم في توجيهها . والهدى هو التفكير الخالص المنزه عن الهوى وشطط التحيز في قالب من قوالب التفكير الجيد . . والكتاب المنير : هو كل فكر مسجل في كتاب قويم قائم على أسس من المبادئ النقدية التي تهدي إلى الحق والصواب وتعين على إدراكه، ووتنير إلى معرفته في تفصيل وإحكام ؛ وكذلك تبعث في العقل نزعة التشوف إلى البحث عن الأسباب والعلل والكشف عن آثارها ومعطياتها ؛ وما قد يعود على الإنسان منها
And we have found in the Noble Qur'an a severe condemnation of a group of people whose habit is disputation, contention, and obstinacy in every matter, whether they have a connection to it or prior knowledge, or have no connection or prior knowledge. He, exalted, said: {And among the people is he who argues about Allah without knowledge and without guidance and without an enlightening book.} So knowledge here is the comprehensive acquaintance with the characteristics and intricacies of what is being investigated, provided that the knowledge is correct and truthful, so that no idle talk affects it, blasphemy does not permeate it, and no defective intention controls its direction. And guidance is pure thought, free from whim and the excess of bias, within a framework of good thinking. And the enlightening book is every thought recorded in a correct book based on the foundations of critical principles that guide to the truth and correctness and help in perceiving it, and enlighten one to its knowledge in detail and precision; and it also creates in the mind a tendency to seek research into causes and reasons and to uncover their effects and implications; and what may come back to man from them.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a critical establishment of the moral foundation for jadal. The author begins by citing a Qur'anic verse that condemns people who engage in disputation (jidal) about Allah without knowledge ('ilm), guidance (huda), or an enlightening book (kitab munir). The author then explains each term: 'ilm means comprehensive and accurate knowledge of the subject; huda means pure thinking free from bias; and kitab munir means recorded thought based on sound critical principles. The point is to emphasize that jadal is not simply argumentation; it must be grounded in proper knowledge, clear thinking, and divine or rational guidance. This serves as a warning against invalid forms of jadal.

The Subject Must Be Disputed: Condition for Valid Jadal
Author states the second condition: the subject must be a matter of dispute and not self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposed subject qualifies for jadal by applying the condition that it must be a matter of dispute and not self-evident.

Core Idea
Jadal requires genuine disagreement; self-evident truths cannot sustain a debate.

Just as a fire needs fuel, a meaningful debate requires a disputed topic. In this lesson, we explore the second condition of jadal: the subject must be a matter of dispute (mahall ishkal). The author explains why self-evident matters are unsuitable, and draws on Ibn 'Aqil to show that the debater must know the opponent's statement and the doubt behind it. Understanding this condition will help you evaluate whether a given debate is valid and purposeful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Condition
Read the first sentence: 'أن يكون الموضوع محل إشكال'. The author states that the subject must be a matter of dispute. Note that the subject should also be known (as per the first condition, not detailed here).
The term 'محل إشكال' means a problematic or contested issue.
2
Distinguish Self-Evident vs. Disputed
The author argues that talk about self-evident matters (بديهيات) is uniform, so there is no disagreement. A valid jadal requires difference in perspective or knowledge. Ask: is the topic something both parties already agree on? If yes, it fails this condition.
Self-evident truths are known to all; they cannot be the basis of a dialectical exchange.
3
Analyze the Rationale for Dispute
The author explains that difference of vision is required for the dialectical process to be valid and justified. The aim is to reach the truth. If both already agree, debate is futile. Consider why disagreement is necessary for the pursuit of truth.
This is a teleological argument: jadal is for truth-seeking, not for arguing without purpose.
4
Examine Ibn 'Aqil's Elaboration
The quote from Ibn 'Aqil breaks down the prerequisites: knowledge of the disagreement in the statement (المقالة) and the doubt (الشبهة) the opponent leans on. The statement must be known to break it; the doubt must be known to treat it like a doctor. This stresses preparation before debate.
Ibn 'Aqil compares the debater to a physician, emphasizing targeted refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - أن يكون الموضوع محل إشكال : وكما ينبغي أن يكون موضوع الجدل والنقاش معروفًا، كذلك فإن موضوع الحديث لا يمكن أن يثير الحوار بين المستدل والناقض - المتحدث والمستمع – إلا إذا كان محل إشكال، بحيث لا تتطابق على الموضوع رؤية المتحاورين، ولا إشكال إلا فيما ليس بديهيا أو فيما تختلف فيه معارف المستدل و معارف الناقض، إذ الحديث في البدهيات سيكون واحدًا لدى المتحاورين، فلا يكونان في حالة نقاش فاختلاف رؤية كلا طرفي الجدل إلى المسألة مطلوب لكي تكون العملية الجدلية سائغة ومبررة لأن غرض كل منهما هو الوصول إلى الحق في المسألة المختلف فيها، فإذا كانا متفقين في رؤيتهما إلى المسألة وغير مختلفين في شأنها فلا فائدة من إدارة جدل عقيم لا ينتج شيئًا حولها . وهذا ما أكده الإمام ابن عقيل حين قال : كل جدل فإنه لابد فيه من علم الاختلاف في المقالة وما يعتمد عليه المخالف للحق من الشبهة، أما المقالة، فلتتمكن من كسرها ولا سبيل لك إلى ذلك من غير أن تعلم ماهيتها، وأما الشبهة فلتداوي صاحبها من الوجه الذي قد دخلت عليه البلية فيها، لأن مثلك في ذلك مثل الطبيب الذي يعالج بما يصلح من الدواء، وأول كل جدل الاختلاف في المقالة، وتحقيقه : أن يكون أحد الخصمين فيه على الموجبة والآخر على السالبة، فإذا ظهر الخلاف وقع الحجاج بعده على طريقة السؤال والجواب، والسائل مخير أن يلزم خصمه ليعجزه عن الانفصال، أو يسأله ليعجزه عن إقامة البرهان
2 - That the subject be a matter of dispute (mushkil): And just as the subject of debate and discussion should be known, similarly, the topic of conversation cannot stimulate dialogue between the arguer and the critic - the speaker and the listener - unless it is a matter of dispute, such that the perspectives of the two discussants do not coincide on the subject. There is no dispute except in what is not self-evident or in what the knowledge of the arguer and the knowledge of the critic differ. For talk about self-evident matters will be uniform for the two discussants, so they will not be in a state of debate. Thus, a difference in vision of each party of the debate concerning the issue is required for the dialectical process to be valid and justified, because the aim of each is to reach the truth in the disputed matter. If they agree in their vision of the issue and do not differ concerning it, then there is no benefit in conducting a futile debate that produces nothing around it. This is what Imam Ibn 'Aqil affirmed when he said: Every debate necessarily requires knowledge of the disagreement in the statement (al-maqala) and what the opponent relies on from the doubt (shubha) against the truth. As for the statement, it is so that you can break it, and you have no way to do that without knowing its essence. As for the doubt, it is to treat its owner from the angle through which affliction has entered him, for your example in that is like the doctor who treats with what is appropriate of medicine. The beginning of every debate is the disagreement in the statement, and its realization is that one of the two opponents is for the affirmative (al-mujiba) and the other for the negative (al-saliba). When disagreement appears, argumentation thereafter occurs in the manner of question and answer. The questioner has the choice to either compel his opponent to incapacitate him from disengagement, or to ask him to incapacitate him from establishing the proof.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents the second condition for valid jadal: the subject must be a matter of dispute (mahall ishkal). The author explains that for dialogue to occur, the topic must not be self-evident or agreed upon; there must be a difference in the knowledge or perspective of the two parties. If both share the same view, debate is futile. The author then supports this with a quote from Imam Ibn 'Aqil, who emphasizes that every debate requires knowledge of the disagreement (al-ikhtilaf) in the statement (maqala) and the doubt (shubha) of the opponent. The debater must understand the opponent's position to break it, and the doubt to treat it like a doctor. The essence of debate is that one party affirms and the other denies, leading to a structured exchange of questions and answers aimed at disproving or proving the case.

Key Terms
محل إشكال
الموجبة والسالبة
الشبهة
البرهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To ensure both parties have something to talk about
B) Because self-evident matters lead to disagreement
C) So that the dialogue is valid and justified, with the aim of reaching truth
D) To make the debate difficult

Key Takeaway
For jadal to be valid, the subject must be non-self-evident and genuinely disputed, with one affirming and one denying, allowing a structured question-answer process aimed at truth.
