The Third Condition: Precise Definition of the Disputed Issue
Author explains the third condition: the disputed issue must be precisely defined with clear terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of tahrir mahall al-niza' to identify the precise point of contention in a jadal dispute and distinguish it from vague or general disagreement.

Core Idea
Without pinpointing the exact locus of dispute, jadal becomes aimless.

This passage establishes the third condition for valid jadal: the problem under debate must be precisely defined. Knowing the general topic and that there is disagreement is not enough. You must identify exactly which aspect is in dispute and describe it with clear, non-vague terms. This ensures that the debate stays focused and productive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall topic of dispute
Start by stating the broad subject that the two parties disagree about. For example, 'the nature of divine attributes'.
This is just the entry point.
2
List the multiple aspects of that topic
Intellectual issues often have several facets. For 'divine attributes', aspects include: whether they are eternal or contingent, whether they are identical to God's essence or distinct, whether they are known by reason or revelation, etc.
The author says intellectual issues have 'multiple aspects'.
3
Determine which specific aspect is actually disputed
Not all aspects are necessarily in dispute. The disagreement may be limited to one. For instance, both parties might agree attributes are eternal, but disagree on whether they are identical to the essence.
This is tahrir mahall al-niza'.
4
Formulate the point of dispute in precise, unambiguous terms
Write down the exact proposition that one side affirms and the other denies. Use clear and specific wording. Avoid 'vague words' (kalimat fudfuda) that could be interpreted in multiple ways.
The author stresses avoiding 'كلمات فضفاضة'.
5
Verify that your terms are not too broad or ambiguous
Check each key term: does it have more than one common meaning? If so, choose a different term or define it operationally. For example, instead of 'justice', specify 'justice as rewarding obedience and punishing disobedience'.
Vague terms 'water down the debate'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
3 - أن تكون مشكلة الموضوع محددة بدقة : لا يكفي أن يكون موضوع الجدل معروفًا، وأن يكون طرفا الجدل مختلفين حول المسألة، وإنما لابد - إضافة إلى ذلك - من أن يتم تحرير محل النزاع في المسألة المختلف فيها ، لأن المسائل الفكرية التي تختلف حولها وجهات النظر كثيرا ما تكون لها أطراف متعددة، وليس من الضروري أن يكون الخلاف دائرًا حول كل الأطراف، بل ربما كان متعلقا بطرف دون البقية، وهنا لابد من تحديد هذا الطرف وتوضيح وجه الخلاف فيه بدقة ثم إجراء الجدل والحوار حوله وتداول وجهات النظر فيه . وإضافة إلى تحرير محل النزاع، لابد أيضًا من التقيد بكلمات محددة ومضبوطة تكشف صراحة عن طبيعة المسألة المختلف فيها وعدم استعمال الكلمات الفضفاضة ذات الدلالات المتنوعة التي من شأنها أن تميع الجدل وتخرج به عن حدوده المرسومة، ذلك أن من مستلزمات الجدال الهادف أن يكون الموضوع
3 - That the problem of the subject be precisely defined: It is not sufficient that the subject of debate be known and that the two parties differ about the issue; rather, in addition to that, it is necessary to determine the precise locus of dispute (tahrir mahall al-niza') in the differed-upon issue. Because intellectual issues about which perspectives differ often have multiple aspects, and it is not necessary that the disagreement revolves around all aspects; rather, it may be related to one aspect without the rest. Here it is necessary to specify this aspect and clarify the point of disagreement in it precisely, then conduct the debate and dialogue about it and exchange perspectives. In addition to determining the locus of dispute, it is also necessary to adhere to specific and precise words that clearly reveal the nature of the differed-upon issue, and not to use vague words with diverse connotations that would water down the debate and take it out of its prescribed limits. That is because among the requirements of purposeful jadal is that the subject...
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage states the third condition for valid jadal: the problem (mushkilat al-mawdu') must be precisely defined. The author clarifies that even if the subject is known and there is disagreement, one must further 'tahrir mahall al-niza'' (determine the exact locus of dispute). Intellectual issues often have multiple facets; the disagreement may concern only one. Without specifying this, the debate becomes unfocused. Additionally, the debaters must use precise, unambiguous terms. Vague words cause confusion and dilute the argument. The passage is cut off, but the author clearly emphasizes that purposeful jadal requires both a precise identification of the point of contention and careful terminology.

Prerequisites for Valid Jadal: Disagreement and Precise Definition
Author outlines prerequisites for valid jadal: difference of opinion and precise definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the two essential prerequisites for valid jadal as outlined by the author: genuine disagreement and precise definition (tahrir mahall al-niza').

Core Idea
Valid jadal requires both genuine disagreement and precise definition of the disputed issue.

In this passage, the author lays out the foundational conditions for meaningful dialectical debate. Without genuine disagreement, debate is sterile; without precise definition, it descends into confusion. Mastering these prerequisites will enable you to conduct or evaluate structured theological and juristic debates effectively.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first prerequisite – genuine disagreement
Look for the statement that debate requires 'ikhtilaf' (difference) between the parties. The author quotes Ibn 'Aqil that the two opponents must be one affirming and one denying.
This ensures the debate has a purpose.
2
Recognize the second prerequisite – precise definition
The author argues that merely knowing the subject is insufficient; one must delimit the exact point of disagreement (tahrir mahall al-niza'), as issues often have multiple aspects.
This prevents debating a non-existent disagreement.
3
Understand the requirement for clear terminology
The author stresses using specific, unambiguous words to avoid dilution of the debate. Loose terms lead to confusion and wasted effort.
Precision in wording is part of precise definition.
4
Apply these conditions to a real Islamic debate
Consider a debate on whether qiyas is a source of law. To set it up validly, both parties must first agree on the definition of qiyas and confirm they genuinely differ on its status.
Without these prerequisites, the debate would be invalid.

فاختلاف رؤية كلا طرفي الجدل إلى المسألة مطلوب لكي تكون العملية الجدلية سائغة ومبررة لأن غرض كل منهما هو الوصول إلى الحق في المسألة المختلف فيها، فإذا كانا متفقين في رؤيتهما إلى المسألة وغير مختلفين في شأنها فلا فائدة من إدارة جدل عقيم لا ينتج شيئًا حولها . وهذا ما أكده الإمام ابن عقيل حين قال : كل جدل فإنه لابد فيه من علم الاختلاف في المقالة وما يعتمد عليه المخالف للحق من الشبهة، أما المقالة، فلتتمكن من كسرها ولا سبيل لك إلى ذلك من غير أن تعلم ماهيتها، وأما الشبهة فلتداوي صاحبها من الوجه الذي قد دخلت عليه البلية فيها، لأن مثلك في ذلك مثل الطبيب الذي يعالج بما يصلح من الدواء، وأول كل جدل الاختلاف في المقالة، وتحقيقه : أن يكون أحد الخصمين فيه على الموجبة والآخر على السالبة، فإذا ظهر الخلاف وقع الحجاج بعده على طريقة السؤال والجواب، والسائل مخير أن يلزم خصمه ليعجزه عن الانفصال، أو يسأله ليعجزه عن إقامة البرهان» 3 - أن تكون مشكلة الموضوع محددة بدقة : لا يكفي أن يكون موضوع الجدل معروفًا، وأن يكون طرفا الجدل مختلفين حول المسألة، وإنما لابد - إضافة إلى ذلك - من أن يتم تحرير محل النزاع في المسألة المختلف فيها ، لأن المسائل الفكرية التي تختلف حولها وجهات النظر كثيرا ما تكون لها أطراف متعددة، وليس من الضروري أن يكون الخلاف دائرًا حول كل الأطراف، بل ربما كان متعلقا بطرف دون البقية، وهنا لابد من تحديد هذا الطرف وتوضيح وجه الخلاف فيه بدقة ثم إجراء الجدل والحوار حوله وتداول وجهات النظر فيه . وإضافة إلى تحرير محل النزاع، لابد أيضًا من التقيد بكلمات محددة ومضبوطة تكشف صراحة عن طبيعة المسألة المختلف فيها وعدم استعمال الكلمات الفضفاضة ذات الدلالات المتنوعة التي من شأنها أن تميع الجدل وتخرج به عن حدوده المرسومة، ذلك أن من مستلزمات الجدال الهادف أن يكون الموضوع المبسوط على مائدة النقاش مصاغا بكلمات محددة الدلالات، مصورًا بتعابير محكمة، حتى يتمكن السامع من تصور المضمون تصورًا واضحا، لأن الكلمات الفضفاضة والتعابير المطاطة، فضلا عن كونها تنم عن فوضى فكرية، فإنها كثيرًا ما تلقي بالناس في متاهات لا صلة لها بالواقع، ولا علاقة لها بالحقيقة، وبالتالي فإنها لا تعين على فهم المقاصد، ولا تساعد على التقريب بين وجهات النظر، ولا على تكوين عقلية تحليلية قادرة على الاستيعاب بارعة في النقد، حاذقة في الاستنتاج . فكم من معارك كلامية استنفدت الطاقات واستغرقت الأوقات، وكانت نتيجتها التباعد بدل التقارب وضياع الحقيقة بدل الكشف عنها والعثور عليها، كل ذلك بسبب عدم الدقة في التعبير، وعدم تحديد مدلولات الكلمات، وعدم الاتفاق على مضامين محددة» يقول الشيخ يوسف القرضاوي : من أهم ما يطلب في الحوار بين طرفين مختلفين : تحديد المفاهيم التي يتنازعون حولها تحديدا دقيقا، يكشف عن ماهيتها ومدلولها فلا تظل مائعة رجراجة، يفسرها كل طرف بما يحلو له . ومن فوائد هذا التحديد : وضع الأشياء في موضعها وعدم إضاعة الجهد والوقت والفكر في إبطال شيء لا يقول به الخصم أساسا، وإنما هو من تفسيره الخاص لمفاهيمه ومصطلحاته، وقد تنفض المعركة نهائيًا إذا اتضح للفريقين أنهما غير متباينين. وقد تكون المعركة أخف حرارة وحدة إذا اتضح أنهما غير متباينين تباينا شديداً شاسعاً . وقد يعرف من أول الأمر أن التناقض بين الطرفين جوهري وأساسي، وأن التقارب بينهما مستحيل، لاختلاف المنطلقات والأهداف والمناهج والمسلمات عند كل منهما ؛ فلا فائدة من الحوار بين اثنين بينهما ما بين المشرق والمغرب، وبقدر ما تقرب من أحدهما تبتعد عن الآخر.
The difference in the view of both parties to the debate concerning the issue is required so that the dialectical process is permissible and justified, because the goal of each of them is to reach the truth in the disputed issue. If they are agreed in their view of the issue and not differing regarding it, then there is no benefit from conducting a sterile debate that yields nothing about it. This is what Imam Ibn 'Aqil affirmed when he said: 'Every debate necessarily requires knowledge of the difference concerning the article (al-maqala) and what the opponent relies upon of the sophism against the truth. As for the article, it is so that you can refute it, and you have no way to do that without knowing its essence. As for the sophism, it is so that you can treat its possessor from the aspect through which the affliction entered him, for your likeness in that is like the physician who treats with what is suitable of medicine. The beginning of every debate is difference concerning the article, and its realization is that one of the two opponents be upon the affirmative and the other upon the negative. When the difference appears, argumentation takes place afterwards in the manner of question and answer. The questioner has the choice to obligate his opponent to disable him from separating, or to ask him to disable him from establishing a demonstration.' 3 - That the problem of the subject be precisely defined: It is not enough that the subject of the debate be known and that the two parties of the debate be differing regarding the issue, rather it is necessary - in addition to that - to delimit the locus of dispute (tahrir mahall al-niza') in the issue under disagreement, because intellectual issues about which viewpoints differ often have multiple aspects, and it is not necessary that the disagreement revolves around all the aspects, but perhaps it is related to one aspect without the rest. Here it is necessary to specify this aspect and clarify the point of disagreement in it precisely, then conduct the debate and dialogue about it and exchange viewpoints concerning it. In addition to delimiting the locus of dispute, it is also necessary to adhere to specific and precise words that explicitly reveal the nature of the disputed issue, and not to use loose words with multiple significations that would dilute the debate and take it out of its defined boundaries. That is because from the requirements of purposeful debate is that the subject spread on the table of discussion be formulated with words of specific significations, depicted with precise expressions, so that the listener can conceptualize the content clearly. Because loose words and elastic expressions, besides indicating intellectual chaos, often throw people into labyrinths unrelated to reality and unrelated to truth, and thus they do not help in understanding objectives, nor do they assist in bringing viewpoints closer, nor in forming an analytical mind capable of comprehension, proficient in criticism, and skilled in deduction. So many verbal battles exhausted energies and consumed time, and their result was distancing instead of closeness, and loss of truth instead of revealing it and finding it. All that is due to lack of precision in expression, lack of specifying the significations of words, and lack of agreement on specific contents.' Sheikh Yusuf al-Qaradawi says: 'Among the most important things required in dialogue between two differing parties is: precise definition of the concepts they dispute over, revealing their essence and signification so they do not remain fluid and sloshy, each party interpreting them as they please. Among the benefits of this definition: placing things in their proper place and not wasting effort, time, and thought in refuting something that the opponent does not essentially hold, but rather it is from their own interpretation of their concepts and terminology. The battle may end completely if it becomes clear to the two parties that they are not divergent. And the battle may be less hot and intense if it becomes clear that they are not severely and vastly divergent. And it may be known from the outset that the contradiction between the two sides is essential and fundamental, and that rapprochement between them is impossible, due to the difference in starting points, goals, methods, and premises of each. So there is no benefit in dialogue between two between whom is what is between east and west; to the extent you approach one you distance from the other.'
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a methodological instruction on the prerequisites for valid dialectical debate (jadal). The author begins by emphasizing that genuine disagreement on the issue is essential; if both parties share the same view, debate is sterile and pointless. This is corroborated by a quotation from Imam Ibn 'Aqil, who explains that every debate requires knowledge of the difference concerning the 'article' (al-maqala) and the sophistical argument (al-shubha) the opponent uses. Knowing the article allows one to refute it; knowing the sophism allows one to treat its holder like a physician. The essence of debate is that one opponent affirms and the other denies a proposition. After disagreement is clear, debate proceeds through question and answer. The author then lists a third condition: precise definition of the problem. It is not enough to know the subject and that there is disagreement; one must delimit the locus of dispute (tahrir mahall al-niza'), because intellectual issues have multiple aspects and disagreement may only concern one. After specifying that aspect, debate should focus on it. Additionally, the author insists on using specific, unambiguous terminology, avoiding loose words that dilute the debate and lead to confusion. He warns that imprecise expression causes wasted effort and loss of truth. The passage concludes with a quote from Yusuf al-Qaradawi stressing precise definition of concepts, which prevents pointless refutations and clarifies whether differences are essential or merely apparent. If divergence is fundamental, dialogue may be futile. The examples used are general; the commentary should keep to Islamic sciences examples: e.g., in usul al-fiqh, debating whether qiyas is a source of law requires precisely defining qiyas and specifying which type of qiyas is under dispute.

تحرير محل النزاع
الشبهة

Checkpoint
Two scholars are debating whether 'blood money' (diyah) is due for killing by mistake. One defines blood money as a fixed amount, the other as a negotiable amount. According to the conditions for valid jadal in this passage, what is the first step?
A) Immediately argue the ruling using evidence.
B) Delimit the locus of dispute: define 'blood money' precisely and agree on its meaning.
C) Determine which scholar is more knowledgeable.
D) Conclude that since they disagree fundamentally, no debate is possible.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a dialectical debate hinges on two conditions: genuine disagreement (ikhtilaf) and precise definition of the issue under dispute (tahrir mahall al-niza').

Precise Definitions in Dialectic
Author argues that precise terminology is essential for productive debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why precise definitions are necessary for productive dialectical debate and analyze how the usuliyyun applied this principle.

Core Idea
Clear definitions turn debate from chaos into discovery.

Have you ever been in a discussion where everyone used the same words but meant different things? This lesson explores why precise definitions are the foundation of meaningful debate. The author argues that vague language leads to wasted energy and confusion, while clear definitions focus the argument on real issues. We'll see how scholars of usul al-fiqh (usuliyyun) put this into practice before diving into their subjects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem with Vague Language
The author warns that loose words and elastic expressions throw people into irrelevant tangents. Find in the passage where he describes the consequences of imprecise language: wasted energies, time lost, increased distance from truth.
This sets up the need for precise definitions.
2
Understand the Solution: Precise Definitions
The author quotes al-Qaradawi: concepts must be defined 'precisely, revealing their essence and meaning so they do not remain fluid and wavering.' Why does this fix the problem? Because it forces both parties to agree on what they are debating.
3
Examine the Three Possible Outcomes
Al-Qaradawi lists three scenarios after precise definition: the battle ends (if no real disagreement), it becomes less intense (if minor differences), or it is revealed as fundamental (making dialogue futile). Can you find these in the text?
This shows how definitions clarify the nature of the dispute.
4
Apply to Usul al-Fiqh Practice
The author notes that usuliyyun always clarify definitions before entering their subjects. Think of a fiqh debate: for example, defining 'khamr' (intoxicant) precisely—does it include all alcohols? Only grape wine? The definition determines the ruling.
This illustrates the practical importance in Islamic legal theory.

الجدل المبسوط على مائدة النقاش مصاغا بكلمات محددة الدلالات، مصورًا بتعابير محكمة، حتى يتمكن السامع من تصور المضمون تصورًا واضحا، لأن الكلمات الفضفاضة والتعابير المطاطة، فضلا عن كونها تنم عن فوضى فكرية، فإنها كثيرًا ما تلقي بالناس في متاهات لا صلة لها بالواقع، ولا علاقة لها بالحقيقة، وبالتالي فإنها لا تعين على فهم المقاصد، ولا تساعد على التقريب بين وجهات النظر، ولا على تكوين عقلية تحليلية قادرة على الاستيعاب بارعة في النقد، حاذقة في الاستنتاج . فكم من معارك كلامية استنفدت الطاقات واستغرقت الأوقات، وكانت نتيجتها التباعد بدل التقارب وضياع الحقيقة بدل الكشف عنها والعثور عليها، كل ذلك بسبب عدم الدقة في التعبير، وعدم تحديد مدلولات الكلمات، وعدم الاتفاق على مضامين محددة» (1) يقول الشيخ يوسف القرضاوي : من أهم ما يطلب في الحوار بين طرفين مختلفين : تحديد المفاهيم » التي يتنازعون حولها تحديدا دقيقا، يكشف عن ماهيتها ومدلولها فلا تظل مائعة رجراجة، يفسرها كل طرف بما يحلو له . ومن فوائد هذا التحديد : وضع الأشياء في موضعها وعدم إضاعة الجهد والوقت والفكر في إبطال شيء لا يقول به الخصم أساسا، وإنما هو من تفسيره الخاص لمفاهيمه ومصطلحاته، وقد تنفض المعركة نهائيًا إذا اتضح للفريقين أنهما غير متباينين. وقد تكون المعركة أخف حرارة وحدة إذا اتضح أنهما غير متباينين تباينا شديدًا شاسعاً . وقد يعرف من أول الأمر أن التناقض بين الطرفين جوهري وأساسي، وأن التقارب بينهما مستحيل، لاختلاف المنطلقات والأهداف والمناهج والمسلمات عند كل منهما ؛ فلا فائدة من الحوار بين اثنين بينهما ما بین المشرق والمغرب، وبقدر ما تقرب من أحدهما تبتعد عن الآخر (2) . لذلك وجدنا الأصوليين يطبقون قاعدة توضيح الحدود ببيان مضمونها، قبل أن يخوضوا في موضوعاتهم، وهذه قاعدة حميدة، جعلت من طبقها منهم على قدر كبير من الوضوح في الأداء العلمي لمختلف آرائه(1) فلابد - إذا إذا - من لغة مشتركة يتم بها التفاهم»(2)، ولذلك كان ضبط التعريفات للمصطلحات المستعملة له أهميته البالغة من حيث إنه يحسم كثيرًا من دواعي النزاع والتدافع ويحصر الجدل في نقطة محددة واضحة يفهمها كلا الطرفين .
Dialectic spread on the table of discussion, formulated in words of specific connotations, depicted with precise expressions, so that the listener can form a clear picture of the content, because loose words and elastic expressions, besides indicating intellectual disorder, often throw people into labyrinths unrelated to reality and truth, and consequently they do not help in understanding objectives, nor in bringing points of view closer, nor in forming an analytical mind capable of comprehension, proficient in criticism, skilled in deduction. How many verbal battles have exhausted energies and consumed time, and their result was distance instead of closeness and loss of truth instead of discovering and finding it, all due to lack of precision in expression, failure to define the meanings of words, and lack of agreement on specific contents (1). Shaykh Yusuf al-Qaradawi says: One of the most important requirements in dialogue between two differing parties is precisely defining the concepts they are disputing, revealing their essence and meaning so they do not remain fluid and wavering, each party interpreting them as they please. Among the benefits of this definition: placing things in their proper place and not wasting effort, time, and thought in invalidating something the opponent does not actually hold, but rather it is from his own interpretation of his concepts and terms; and the battle may end entirely if it becomes clear to the two parties that they are not divergent. And the battle may be less heated and intense if it becomes clear that they are not widely divergent. And it may be known from the outset that the contradiction between the two parties is essential and fundamental, and that rapprochement between them is impossible, due to differences in starting points, goals, methods, and postulates of each; so there is no benefit in dialogue between two who are as far apart as east and west, and the more you approach one, the more you distance yourself from the other (2). Therefore we find the usuliyyun (scholars of legal theory) applying the rule of clarifying definitions by explaining their content before delving into their subjects, and this is a commendable rule that made those who applied it among them possess a great degree of clarity in the scientific presentation of their various opinions (1). So it is necessary, then, to have a common language through which understanding occurs (2), and therefore controlling the definitions of used terms has great importance in that it resolves many causes of conflict and contention and confines dialectic to a specific, clear point understood by both parties.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is a methodological discussion on the role of precise definitions in dialectical debate (jadal). The author begins by stating that dialectic should be conducted with words that have specific, fixed connotations and precise expressions so that the listener can form a clear mental picture. Vague and elastic language, he argues, not only indicates intellectual disorder but also leads people into irrelevant tangents, hindering understanding and analytical thinking. He provides a concrete consequence: many verbal battles have exhausted energy and time yet resulted in increased distance and loss of truth, all due to imprecise expression and failure to agree on meanings. The author then quotes Shaykh Yusuf al-Qaradawi, who emphasizes that in dialogue between differing parties, precisely defining the concepts under dispute is paramount. This definition reveals their essence and prevents each side from interpreting terms arbitrarily. Al-Qaradawi lists several benefits: it places issues in their proper context, avoids wasting effort on arguments the opponent does not actually hold, and can either end the dispute entirely if the parties are not actually opposed, or reduce its intensity if the opposition is not extreme. Conversely, it may reveal from the outset that the contradiction is fundamental, making dialogue futile—a situation akin to east and west. The author then notes that the usuliyyun (scholars of usul al-fiqh) applied this principle by clarifying definitions before engaging in their subjects, a commendable practice that brought clarity to their scientific output. The passage concludes that a common language is essential for understanding, and controlling definitions is of great importance because it resolves many causes of conflict and confines debate to a clear, specific point. In the context of Islamic sciences, one can see this in fiqh debates: for example, defining 'khamr' (intoxicant) precisely avoids disputes over whether a specific substance is forbidden—if both parties agree on the definition, the discussion moves to whether the substance fits that definition. Similarly, in usul al-fiqh, defining 'qiyas' (analogy) precisely prevents confusion with other forms of reasoning.

Key Takeaway
Precise definitions are the cornerstone of productive dialectic: they prevent wasted effort, reveal true points of contention, and focus debate on real issues.

The Necessity of Defining Terms in Dialectic
Author explains why defining terms is essential to avoid disputes in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why clarifying definitions is essential for productive dialectic, using examples from usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Without a common language, dialectic becomes pointless wrangling.

This lesson explores a foundational principle of Islamic dialectic: before arguing, both sides must agree on what their terms mean. The passage we will study shows how the usuliyyun (scholars of legal methodology) operationalised this principle by clarifying definitions before delving into substantive issues. This practice, praised by the author, ensures clarity and confines debate to specific points.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Practice of Usuliyyun
Read the passage and note what the author says about the usuliyyun: they apply the principle of clarifying definitions before discussing their subjects. Why is this described as 'praiseworthy'?
The author emphasizes that this brings clarity to their scientific performance.
2
Understand the Need for a Common Language
The author asserts that a common language (lugha mushtaraka) is necessary for mutual understanding. Reflect on what happens if two parties use the same term but mean different things.
This prevents hidden disagreements from derailing the debate.
3
Analyze the Role of Definitions
The author says that regulating definitions 'settles many causes of conflict and confines dialectic to a specific clear point.' How does defining terms reduce conflict?
By making each party's position explicit, misunderstandings are minimized.
4
Connect to Real Dialectical Practice
Consider the classical Islamic debate on free will: the term 'action' (fi'l) was defined differently by the Qadariyya and Jabriyya. How might defining 'action' have helped clarify their dispute?
This illustrates the author's principle in action.

لذلك وجدنا الأصوليين يطبقون قاعدة توضيح الحدود ببيان مضمونها، قبل أن يخوضوا في موضوعاتهم، وهذه قاعدة حميدة، جعلت من طبقها منهم على قدر كبير من الوضوح في الأداء العلمي لمختلف آرائه(1) فلابد - إذا إذا - من لغة مشتركة يتم بها التفاهم»(2)، ولذلك كان ضبط التعريفات للمصطلحات المستعملة له أهميته البالغة من حيث إنه يحسم كثيرًا من دواعي النزاع والتدافع ويحصر الجدل في نقطة محددة واضحة يفهمها كلا الطرفين .
Therefore we find that the specialists in legal methodology (usuliyyun) apply the principle of clarifying definitions by explaining their content before delving into their subjects, and this is a praiseworthy principle that made those who applied it among them achieve a great degree of clarity in the scientific performance of their various opinions. So, then, a common language must exist through which understanding is achieved. Therefore, the regulation of definitions for the terms used has its utmost importance in that it settles many causes of conflict and struggle and restricts dialectic to a specific clear point that both parties understand.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage highlights a key methodological principle for dialectic: the necessity of a shared language through precise definitions. The author begins by observing that the usuliyyun (scholars of usul al-fiqh) consistently clarify the definitions of their terms before discussing their substantive topics. This practice, described as 'praiseworthy,' brings clarity to their academic discourse. The author then asserts that a common language (lugha mushtaraka) is indispensable for mutual understanding. He explains that carefully defining the terms used resolves many causes of dispute and confines the dialectical discussion to a specific, clear point that both parties can grasp. This principle is foundational for productive debate.

D) It is a practice only for beginners.

Key Takeaway
Defining terms is essential for dialectic because it creates a common language that prevents misunderstandings and confines debate to the real issues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the classical debate on free will between the Qadariyya and Jabriyya, identify two key terms (e.g., 'action' and 'capacity') that would need clear definition for the debate to be productive. Explain how ambiguity in these terms could lead to false disagreements.

The First Etiquette of Dialectic: Present Only What Is Necessary
Author states the first condition for dialectical style: only present what is needed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify violations of the first etiquette of dialectic in a debate and explain why unnecessary speech is detrimental.

Core Idea
In dialectic, speech must be directly aimed at the truth — circling wastes effort and opens errors.

This lesson explores the first condition for a sound dialectical style: presenting only what is necessary. The author warns against 'al-laff wa al-dawaran' (circling and wandering) and cites al-Juwayni and al-Baji to emphasize that irrelevant speech distracts, wastes time, and allows opponents to exploit misplaced arguments. You will learn to spot digressions in debates and understand the rationale behind this etiquette.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central rule
Locate the first condition listed: 'Commitment to presenting only what is needed in dialectic.' This is the foundational etiquette under discussion.
2
Understand the justifications
The author gives two reasons: (1) dialectic is a cooperative process to reach truth, so go directly; (2) circling harms the process and the disputant, causing errors that may be irresolvable.
The term 'al-laff wa al-dawaran' describes this harmful digression.
3
Analyze al-Juwayni's warning
Al-Juwayni advises introducing only the needed speech. Reason: if you insert unnecessary speech, your opponent can corrupt it out of context, making it difficult to return to when needed.
This shows that unnecessary speech can be strategically exploited.
4
Apply al-Baji's principles
Al-Baji adds two constraints: (a) do not speak about what you do not know; (b) speak only about the intended purpose, avoiding incidental matters.
These reinforce the rule with personal discipline and relevance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع الآداب المتعلقة بأسلوب الجدل أسلوب الجدل هو الطريقة التي يجري بها والأسلوب الذي يدار به، وحتى يكون هذا الأسلوب علميًا وجاريًا على أساس سليم ينبغي أيضًا أن تتوفر في طريقة الجدل الذي يجري بين المتخالفين في المسألة جملة من الشروط التي ينبغي أن يلتزم بها كلا طرفي العملية الجدلية، وهي: 1 - الالتزام بإيراد ما يحتاج إليه في الجدل فقط : بما أن الجدل عملية مشتركة يراد منها التعاون للوصول إلى الحق في المسألة المتنازع فيها فإن ذلك يقتضي أن يذهب المتجادلان إلى تحقيق هذه الغاية، رأسًا دون اللف والدوران حول مسائل أو من خلال مسالك ربما تؤدي إلى الحياد عن الحق والوقوع في الباطل. واللف والدوران في الجدل كما يضر بموضع العملية الجدلية، يضر كذلك بقدرات المجادل ويوقعه في أخطاء قد لا يمكنه الفكاك منها . لذلك كان لابد لكلا طرفي الجدل من أن يكون كلامهما «ملائما للموضوع، ليس فيه خروج عما هما بصدده، لأن ذلك يشتت الذهن ويضيع الوقت، ويعدم التركيز المطلوب، ويهدر الجهد (1) قال إمام الحرمين الجويني: لا تورد في كل موضع من الكلام إلا قدر ما يحتاج إليه، وهو نصيحة المشايخ، يقولون لأصحابهم: اتفقوا في المناظرات بالمعروف، وإنما قيل ذلك، لأنه ربما تورِدُ ههنا كلاما لا تحتاج إليه فيفسده الخصم عليك في غير موضعه، لأنه في غير موضعه فيصعب عليك العود إليه في موضع الحاجة»(2) وقال الباجي : لا يتكلم على ما لم يقع له العلم به من جهته، ولا يتكلم إلا على المقصود من كلامه، ولا يتعرض لما لا يقصده مما جرى في خلاله،
The Fourth Section: Etiquettes Related to the Style of Dialectic. The style of dialectic is the method by which it is conducted and the manner in which it is managed. For this style to be scientific and proceeding on a sound basis, a number of conditions must also be present in the method of dialectic that takes place between disputants on an issue, conditions that both parties to the dialectical process must adhere to. They are: 1. Commitment to presenting only what is needed in dialectic. Since dialectic is a joint process intended for cooperation to arrive at the truth in the disputed issue, this requires that the two disputants proceed directly to achieve this goal without circling and wandering around issues or through paths that might lead to deviation from the truth and falling into falsehood. Circling and wandering in dialectic harms the subject of the dialectical process as well as the abilities of the disputant and causes him errors from which he may not be able to extricate himself. Therefore, it is necessary for both parties in dialectic that their speech be 'appropriate to the subject, without deviation from what they are addressing, because that distracts the mind, wastes time, eliminates required concentration, and wastes effort.' (1) Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said: 'Do not introduce in any place of speech more than what is needed. This is the advice of the shaykhs; they say to their companions: 'Agree in debates with propriety.' That is said because perhaps you will introduce here speech that you do not need, so your opponent will corrupt it against you out of its proper place, and because it is out of its proper place it becomes difficult for you to return to it when needed.' (2) Al-Baji said: 'He should not speak about what he does not have knowledge of from his own side, and he should not speak except about the intended purpose of his speech, and he should not address what he does not intend from what occurs during it.'
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage begins the fourth section on the etiquettes of the style of dialectic. The author defines the style of dialectic as the method and manner in which it is conducted. He then states that for the style to be scientific and sound, several conditions must be observed by both parties. The first condition is: commitment to presenting only what is needed in the dialectic. The author explains that dialectic is a cooperative process aimed at reaching the truth in a disputed matter. Therefore, the disputants must go directly to this goal without unnecessary circling (laff wa dawaran) that could deviate from the truth. Such circling harms the process and the abilities of the disputant, leading to errors. He emphasizes that speech must be appropriate to the subject and not stray from the topic, as digression distracts, wastes time, and wastes effort. He then cites two authoritative sources: First, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, who advises to introduce only the needed amount of speech, because introducing unnecessary speech allows the opponent to corrupt it out of context, making it hard to return to it when needed. Second, al-Baji, who adds that one should not speak about what one does not know, and should speak only about the intended purpose, avoiding irrelevant matters that arise incidentally.

Stick to Necessary Statements in Debate
First rule of dialectical method: relevance and brevity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether statements in a dialectical debate are necessary, applying the first condition of jadal.

Core Idea
Efficient dialectic requires each statement to serve the truth-seeking goal.

The first condition of proper jadal demands that both parties present only what is needed to resolve the dispute. This lesson unpacks that condition through the advice of classical scholars and shows how to apply it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the goal of the debate
Recognize that jadal aims at cooperative truth-seeking. Every statement should directly advance this goal.
If a statement does not help discover the truth, it is likely unnecessary.
2
Check each statement for relevance
Ask: Does this statement address the disputed issue? Avoid circumlocution (al-laff wa al-dawaran) that distracts from the main point.
Al-Baji advises speaking only about the intended subject.
3
Apply al-Juwayni's warning
Present only what is needed; irrelevant speech can be exploited by the opponent and is hard to defend when needed.
4
Avoid specious arguments
Al-Tufi warns against introducing arguments that are not binding or that the opponent cannot refute, as they waste time.
These are 'aghlutat' (fallacies) that derail the debate.
5
Decide whether to include the statement
If the statement does not meet the above criteria, omit it. This ensures focus, saves time, and maintains clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع
الآداب المتعلقة بأسلوب الجدل
أسلوب الجدل هو الطريقة التي يجري بها والأسلوب الذي يدار به، وحتى يكون هذا الأسلوب علميًا وجاريًا على أساس سليم ينبغي أيضًا أن تتوفر في طريقة الجدل الذي يجري بين المتخالفين في المسألة جملة من الشروط التي ينبغي أن يلتزم بها كلا طرفي العملية الجدلية، وهي:
1 - الالتزام بإيراد ما يحتاج إليه في الجدل فقط : بما أن الجدل عملية مشتركة يراد منها التعاون للوصول إلى الحق في المسألة المتنازع فيها فإن ذلك يقتضي أن يذهب المتجادلان إلى تحقيق هذه الغاية، رأسًا دون اللف والدوران حول مسائل أو من خلال مسالك ربما تؤدي إلى الحياد عن الحق والوقوع في الباطل. واللف والدوران في الجدل كما يضر بموضع العملية الجدلية، يضر كذلك بقدرات المجادل ويوقعه في أخطاء قد لا يمكنه الفكاك منها . لذلك كان لابد لكلا طرفي الجدل من أن يكون كلامهما «ملائما للموضوع، ليس فيه خروج عما هما بصدده، لأن ذلك يشتت الذهن ويضيع الوقت، ويعدم التركيز المطلوب، ويهدر الجهد (1)
قال إمام الحرمين الجويني: لا تورد في كل موضع من الكلام إلا قدر ما يحتاج إليه، وهو نصيحة المشايخ، يقولون لأصحابهم: اتفقوا في المناظرات بالمعروف، وإنما قيل ذلك، لأنه ربما تورِدُ ههنا كلاما لا تحتاج إليه فيفسده الخصم عليك في غير موضعه، لأنه في غير موضعه فيصعب عليك العود إليه في موضع الحاجة»(2)
وقال الباجي : لا يتكلم على ما لم يقع له العلم به من جهته، ولا يتكلم إلا على المقصود من كلامه، ولا يتعرض لما لا يقصده مما جرى في خلاله، فإن الكلام على ما لم يقصده عدول عن الغرض المطلوب» (1) . فالواجب على المشارك في عملية الجدل أن يلتزم بإيراد رأيه في المسألة موضع أدلته النزاع، يورد وحججه واضحة بينة، كما یورد ردوده على ما يأتي به مخالفه من أدلة وبينات، مع الحذر في كل هذه الأطوار من الوقوع في الأغاليط والشبهات لا فائدة منها سوى تطويل العملية الجدلية والانحراف بها عن مسارها . قال الطوفي: «ولا يورد أحدهما على الآخر شبهة يعلم أنها لا ترد عليه أو لا تلزمه لأن الزمان أقصر من أن يضيع في الأغلوطات والتغليطات، وفي تحقيق الحق ما يستغرق الوقت»(2).
The fourth topic: Manners related to the method of dialectical debate. The method of dialectical debate is the way it is conducted and the style by which it is managed. In order for this method to be scientific and soundly based, a number of conditions must also be present in the method of dialectical debate that takes place between those who differ on a matter, conditions that both parties to the dialectical process must adhere to. They are: 1 - Commitment to only presenting what is needed in the debate: Since dialectical debate is a shared process intended for cooperation to reach the truth in the disputed matter, this requires that the two disputants go directly towards achieving this goal, without circumlocution and digression around issues or through paths that might lead to deviation from truth and falling into falsehood. Circumlocution and digression in dialectical debate harm both the position of the dialectical process and the capabilities of the debater, causing errors from which he may not be able to extricate himself. Therefore, both parties to the debate must make their speech 'appropriate to the subject, without straying from what they are discussing, because that distracts the mind, wastes time, eliminates needed focus, and squanders effort.' (1) Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said: 'Do not present in any place of speech except what is needed. This is the advice of the shaykhs; they say to their students: Be moderate in debates. And that was said because perhaps you present here speech you do not need, and the opponent corrupts it against you out of its proper place, because it is out of place, making it difficult for you to return to it when needed.' (2) Al-Baji said: 'One should not speak about what he does not have knowledge of from his own side, nor speak except about the intended point of his speech, nor address what he does not intend from what occurred during it, because speaking about what one did not intend is a deviation from the desired purpose.' (1) Thus, the participant in the dialectical process must commit to presenting his opinion on the matter under dispute with his proofs and clear evidence, and also present his responses to what his opponent brings of proofs and evidences, while being cautious in all these stages against falling into fallacies and specious arguments that serve no purpose other than prolonging the dialectical process and diverting it from its path. Al-Tufi said: 'Neither party should present to the other a specious argument that he knows would not be refuted against him or would not be binding, because time is too short to waste on quandaries and deceptive arguments, and in establishing the truth there is what occupies the entire time.' (2)
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a methodological definition of the first condition for proper dialectical debate (jadal). It begins by defining the method of jadal as the way and style of conducting debate, emphasizing the need for scientific and sound foundations. The condition is: only present what is necessary. The rationale is that jadal aims at cooperative truth-seeking, so debaters must go directly to that goal without circumlocution (al-laff wa al-dawaran), which harms the process and the debater. The author then cites three authorities: Al-Juwayni advises presenting only what is needed and warns that irrelevant speech can be exploited by the opponent. Al-Baji stresses speaking only about what one knows and the intended subject. Al-Tufi warns against introducing specious arguments that waste time. The overall lesson is that discipline in speech is essential for effective and truthful debate.

Use the Strongest Evidence in Debate
Author explains the need for debaters to rely on their strongest proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify the principle of using the strongest evidence in debate based on al-Juwayni's strategic reasoning.

Core Idea
Lead with your strongest argument to preserve the truth's brilliance.

In this lesson, we explore a critical strategic principle for any dialectician: always rely on your strongest proofs first. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni provides a compelling rationale, warning that weak arguments can undermine your entire case. By understanding his reasoning, you will learn why the order and strength of your evidence matters as much as its content.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The author states the second condition: 'al-tamassuk bi-aqwa al-adilla' (adherence to the strongest evidence). Recognize that this is a methodological rule for debaters.
2
Extract al-Juwayni's core warning
Al-Juwayni says: 'Beware of relying in inference except on the strongest point in the matter.' This is a direct command to avoid weak arguments entirely at the start.
3
Analyze the consequences of weak arguments
Al-Juwayni gives several reasons: a weak opponent may exploit it; you'll need to replace it later, diminishing the truth's 'nadrat al-haqq' (luster); the audience will despise your speech, weakening your own resolve.
Note the term 'nadrat al-haqq' – the compelling beauty of truth that inspires confidence.
4
Formulate the strategic takeaway
The proper order is to open with your strongest evidence. This secures the truth's brilliance, impresses the audience, and leaves weaker points to follow naturally.
This is not just about winning, but about faithfully representing the truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - التمسك بأقوى الأدلة :
إن عناية الباحث المجادل بالمسألة موضع النزاع وحرصه على تحقيق الحق فيها وشعوره بأنه يمثل هذا الحق في هذه المسألة من شأن ذلك كله أن يدفعه إلى البحث عن أقوى الأدلة وأسطع البراهين التي تثبت رأيه وتكشف تهافت رأي مخالفه، وأي تهاون منه في ذلك يكون دليلا على عدم يقينه بكون الحق في جانب رأيه وشعوره بأنه في جانب مخالفه . قال إمام الحرمين الجويني : إياك أن تتعلق عند الاستدلال إلا بأقوى ما في المسألة، ولا يغرنك ضعف السائل فربما يكون في الحاضرين من يضيق – بقوته في العلم - عليك الدنيا، وقد يتضح للخصم الضعيف عند التعلق بالشيء الذي لا يقوى في الاحتجاج ما يصعب عليك الخروج منه، ولأنك إذا تعلقت بأقوى ما في المسألة، راح بعده ما هو أضعف منه ، وإذا تعلقت بالضعيف، احتجت بعده إلى وضع القوي موضع الضعيف فيذهب عند ذلك رونق نضرة الحق وبهائها، ولأنك إذا قدمت الضعيف استرذل الحاضرون كلامك، فيضعف خاطرك بعده عن إيراد ما هو أقوى منه إذا لم يستحوذوا في الأول كلامك، فتترك جميع ما تقوله عندهم، فلا تظهر نضرة الحق، ولأنك إذا بدهت الخصم بأقوى شيء في المسألة
2 - Adherence to the strongest evidence: The care of the researching debater for the disputed matter, his eagerness to realize the truth in it, and his feeling that he represents this truth in this matter – all of this should drive him to seek the strongest evidence and most brilliant proofs that establish his opinion and reveal the collapse of his opponent's opinion. Any negligence on his part in that is evidence of his lack of certainty that truth is on his side and his feeling that it is on his opponent's side. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said: 'Beware of relying in inference except on the strongest point in the matter. Do not be deceived by the weakness of the questioner, for perhaps among those present there is someone who – with his strength in knowledge – will make life difficult for you. And perhaps the weak opponent, when you rely on something that is not strong in argumentation, will make clear to you something from which it will be difficult for you to exit. And because if you rely on the strongest point in the matter, what is weaker than it will subsequently disappear. And if you rely on the weak, you will afterwards need to put the strong in place of the weak, and then the luster and beauty of the truth will vanish. And because if you present the weak, the audience will despise your speech, and then your resolve will weaken afterwards in presenting what is stronger than it, if they have not taken control of your speech at first. Then you will leave all that you say unaccepted by them, and the luster of truth will not appear. And because if you initially confront the opponent with the strongest thing in the matter...'
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents the second condition for proper dialectical debate: using the strongest evidence. The author argues that a debater's commitment to truth should drive him to seek the most powerful proofs. Any laziness indicates a lack of conviction. The core of the lesson is al-Juwayni's extended advice: never rely on weak arguments, because even a weak opponent might exploit them, and you will have to replace them later, diminishing the truth's appeal. Presenting weak evidence first causes the audience to dismiss your speech, making it hard to later present stronger points. Therefore, always start with your strongest argument to secure the truth's brilliance.

Rule 1: Avoid Irrelevant Objections in Jadal
Author states first rule: avoid irrelevant objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify irrelevant objections and fallacies in a dialectical debate and explain why they are prohibited.

Core Idea
Time is too short to waste on fallacies; jadal is about truth, not trickery.

In this lesson, we explore the first rule of dialectical debate: avoid introducing irrelevant objections or fallacies. The author warns that such tactics only prolong the debate and distract from the goal of establishing truth. Al-Tufi's quote underscores that time is too precious for deception. This rule keeps the debate focused and productive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule as stated by the author
Read the first part of the Arabic passage. The author says the debater must avoid fallacies and ambiguities that have no benefit except prolonging the debate and diverting it.
The key term here is 'shubhah' (ambiguity/fallacy).
2
Extract the justification from al-Tufi's quote
Al-Tufi says: 'Neither of them should present to the other an ambiguity that he knows will not be refuted against him or does not obligate him, because time is too short to waste on fallacies.'
This is a practical ethical guideline.
3
Analyze the consequence of violating this rule
Violating this rule leads to 'prolonging the dialectical process and diverting it from its path.' The debate becomes about deception rather than truth.
4
Apply to a hypothetical debate
Imagine a debate about the validity of qiyas in usul al-fiqh. An irrelevant objection would be attacking the opponent's personal character instead of addressing the evidence. Recognize why this violates the rule.
Character attacks are a common fallacy (ad hominem) that jadal rules prohibit.

الكلام على ما لم يقصده عدول عن الغرض المطلوب. فالواجب على المشارك في عملية الجدل أن يلتزم بإيراد رأيه في المسألة موضع أدلته النزاع، يورد وحججه واضحة بينة، كما يورد ردوده على ما يأتي به مخالفه من أدلة وبينات، مع الحذر في كل هذه الأطوار من الوقوع في الأغاليط والشبهات لا فائدة منها سوى تطويل العملية الجدلية والانحراف بها عن مسارها. قال الطوفي: «ولا يورد أحدهما على الآخر شبهة يعلم أنها لا ترد عليه أو لا تلزمه لأن الزمان أقصر من أن يضيع في الأغلوطات والتغليطات، وفي تحقيق الحق ما يستغرق الوقت». التمسك بأقوى الأدلة: إن عناية الباحث المجادل بالمسألة موضع النزاع وحرصه على تحقيق الحق فيها وشعوره بأنه يمثل هذا الحق في هذه المسألة من شأن ذلك كله أن يدفعه إلى البحث عن أقوى الأدلة وأسطع البراهين التي تثبت رأيه وتكشف تهافت رأي مخالفه، وأي تهاون منه في ذلك يكون دليلا على عدم يقينه بكون الحق في جانب رأيه وشعوره بأنه في جانب مخالفه. قال إمام الحرمين الجويني: إياك أن تتعلق عند الاستدلال إلا بأقوى ما في المسألة، ولا يغرنك ضعف السائل فربما يكون في الحاضرين من يضيق – بقوته في العلم - عليك الدنيا، وقد يتضح للخصم الضعيف عند التعلق بالشيء الذي لا يقوى في الاحتجاج ما يصعب عليك الخروج منه، ولأنك إذا تعلقت بأقوى ما في المسألة، راح بعده ما هو أضعف منه، وإذا تعلقت بالضعيف، احتجت بعده إلى وضع القوي موضع الضعيف فيذهب عند ذلك رونق نضرة الحق وبهائها، ولأنك إذا قدمت الضعيف استرذل الحاضرون كلامك، فيضعف خاطرك بعده عن إيراد ما هو أقوى منه إذا لم يستحوذوا في الأول كلامك، فتترك جميع ما تقوله عندهم، فلا تظهر نضرة الحق، ولأنك إذا بدهت الخصم بأقوى شيء في المسألة، هابك في الاحتجاج، فتؤثر هيبته تلك في تجنبه شبهته وتقوية بدعته. ويقتضي هذا أن يكون لدى المجادل دراسة سابقة للموضوع وخبرة قديمة به، وبراهينه وإحاطة كاملة بوجوه دلالتها، لأنه إن استدل بدليل لم يعرف وجه دلالته بدقة أو كان دليلا ضعيفا غير شاف في المسألة ربما كان ذلك سببا لضعف حجته وسقوط رأيه حتى وإن كان الحق في جانبه هو دون جانب مخالفه. ولذلك يؤكد الباجي على المجادل أن لا يستدل إلا بدليل قد وقف عليه وخبره وامتحنه قبل ذلك وعرف صحته وسلامته لأنه ربما يستدل بما لم يُمْعِنْ تأمله ولا تصحيحه، فيظفر به خصمه ويبين انقطاعه. ضرورة التثبت في الكلام: الجدل بما أنه مبادلة للكلام بين المتخالفين في مسألة من مسائل العلم والمعرفة، ربما كان سببًا إلى أن ينسى المجادل باعثه الأساس على الجدل وغايته الأولى منه، فينساق وراء المنازعة اللفظية ويكثر من الكلام بغرض إفحام المخالف، وقد يفضي به هذا إلى أن يتحول إلى مدافع عن الباطل بعد أن كان باعثه هو الدفاع عن الحق. لذلك من الضروري أن يدرك الباحث أن الجدل ليس غاية في حد ذاته وإنما وسيلة لنصرة الحق في المسألة المتنازع فيها، ولذلك لابد له أن يحرص على أن لا يقول إلا كلاما موزونا يعرف بداياته ونهاياته ويملك التحكم فيه وتوجيهه إلى هدفه. وقد أوصى إمام الحرمين الجويني كل من يشارك في عملية من عمليات الجدل قائلا: «لا تعود نفسك الإسهاب والجدال بالباطل والمبادرة إلى كل ما سبق إليه الخاطر واللسان، حتى إذا أورد ما أورده أو سمع ما سمعه، يكون في جميعه على التثبت والتيقظ، فإن الكلام إذا طال واشتمل على الغث والسمين، مجته الآذان.
Speech about what is not intended is a deviation from the desired goal. So it is incumbent upon the participant in the process of dialectic to commit to presenting his opinion on the issue under dispute, presenting his evidence and proofs clearly, as well as presenting his responses to what his opponent brings of evidence and proofs, while being cautious in all these stages against falling into fallacies and ambiguities that have no benefit except prolonging the dialectical process and diverting it from its path. Al-Tufi said: 'Neither of them should present to the other an ambiguity that he knows will not be refuted against him or does not obligate him, because time is too short to waste on fallacies and deceptions, and in establishing the truth there is enough to occupy the time.' Adhering to the strongest evidence: Indeed, the care of the researching debater for the issue under dispute and his eagerness to establish the truth in it and his feeling that he represents this truth in this issue – all of that should drive him to search for the strongest evidence and clearest proofs that establish his opinion and reveal the incoherence of his opponent's opinion. Any negligence on his part in that is evidence of his lack of certainty that the truth lies with his opinion and his feeling that it lies with his opponent. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said: 'Beware of relying in argumentation except on the strongest point in the issue. Do not be deceived by the weakness of the questioner, for perhaps among those present there is one who – by his strength in knowledge – will make life difficult for you. And it may become clear to the weak opponent, when relying on something that is not strong in argument, what makes it difficult for you to extricate yourself. Because if you rely on the strongest point in the issue, what is weaker than it will be set aside afterward. If you rely on the weak, you will later need to replace the weak with the strong, and then the luster and beauty of truth will disappear. And if you present the weak, the audience will despise your speech, and then your mind will weaken thereafter in presenting what is stronger than it, if they did not overwhelm your speech at first, so you leave all that you say with them, and the luster of truth does not appear. And if you confront the opponent with the strongest thing in the issue, he will fear you in argument, and that fear will affect him in avoiding his ambiguity and strengthening his innovation.' This necessitates that the debater have prior study of the subject and long experience with it, and his proofs and complete knowledge of the aspects of their indication, because if he argues with a proof without knowing precisely its aspect of indication, or if it is a weak proof not sufficient for the issue, that might be a cause for the weakness of his argument and the downfall of his opinion, even if the truth is on his side and not on his opponent's side. Therefore, al-Baji emphasizes that the debater should not argue except with a proof that he has stopped at, examined, tested before, and known its correctness and soundness, because perhaps he argues with something that he has not thoroughly contemplated or corrected, so his opponent gets hold of it and demonstrates its discontinuity. The necessity of carefulness in speech: Since dialectic is an exchange of speech between those who differ on a matter of knowledge and cognition, it may cause the debater to forget his primary motive for dialectic and its first goal, and he may get carried away with verbal wrangling and increase speech for the purpose of silencing the opponent, and this may lead him to become a defender of falsehood after his motive was to defend truth. Therefore, it is necessary for the researcher to realize that dialectic is not an end in itself but a means to support truth in the disputed issue. Therefore, he must be careful to say only measured speech whose beginnings and ends he knows, and he can control and direct it toward its goal. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni advised everyone who participates in a process of dialectic, saying: 'Do not accustom yourself to verbosity and arguing with falsehood and rushing into everything that comes to mind and tongue, so that when you present what you present or hear what you hear, you are in all of it careful and alert. For if speech becomes long and includes the worthless and the valuable, ears become tired.'
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents three fundamental rules for the practitioner of dialectical debate (jadal). The author first warns against introducing irrelevant objections or fallacies that only prolong the debate and deviate it from its purpose. He quotes al-Tufi to emphasize that time is too precious to waste on deceptive arguments; the goal is establishing truth. The second rule is to always rely on the strongest evidence available. The author cites al-Juwayni's lengthy advice explaining that using weak arguments undermines one's credibility and the truth itself. Even if the opponent seems weak, a strong argument is necessary because knowledgeable listeners may be present. Using the strongest evidence first sets a tone of authority and forces the opponent into a defensive position. The author adds that this requires the debater to have thorough prior knowledge of the topic and to have examined each proof for its soundness, as al-Baji warns against using untested proofs that the opponent might expose. The third rule is the necessity of careful speech. The author reminds that the purpose of jadal is to support truth, not to win a verbal contest. Excessive and uncontrolled speech can lead the debater astray, even turning him into a defender of falsehood. Al-Juwayni's final advice stresses measured, deliberate speech, avoiding verbosity and rushing. This passage is an expansion on the ethics and methodology of dialectic, drawing on classical authorities to provide practical guidance.

شبهة

Checkpoint
According to al-Tufi's quote, why should debaters avoid presenting ambiguous objections (shubhah)?
A) Because the opponent will use them against you
B) Because time is too short to waste on fallacies and truth-seeking should occupy it
C) Because the audience will be confused
D) Because it makes the debate longer

Key Takeaway
The first rule of jadal is to avoid irrelevant objections and fallacies, as they waste time and divert from truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a potential irrelevant objection in a debate about the permissibility of riba (usury) in modern banking. Explain why it violates the first rule.
?
In a jadal session on the eternity of the Quran, a debater brings up the opponent's past mistakes. Apply al-Tufi's quote to critique this tactic.

Rule 2: Use the Strongest Evidence
Author states second rule: adhere to the strongest evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate why a debater must use the strongest evidence and identify the consequences of weak arguments even if the position is true.

Core Idea
Weak arguments undermine truth; the strongest evidence establishes authority and protects the truth.

This lesson covers the second rule: always rely on the strongest evidence. The author cites al-Juwayni's extensive advice explaining that using weak arguments harms the debater's credibility and may cause the truth to be lost. Prior preparation and knowledge of the evidence are essential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule as stated
The author says the debater must search for the strongest evidence and clearest proofs. Negligence indicates lack of certainty that truth is on his side.
Key term: 'dalil' (proof).
2
Examine al-Juwayni's justification
Al-Juwayni warns against relying on weak arguments. He gives several reasons: it can backfire even against a weak opponent, it damages the luster of truth, and it may make the debater appear weak.
The quote is lengthy; focus on the logical points.
3
Understand the necessity of prior preparation
The author adds that the debater must have prior study and complete knowledge of the evidence's implications. Using a proof without knowing its exact indication can cause the argument to collapse.
This links to al-Baji's advice in the next rule.
4
Apply to a theological debate
Consider a debate about God's attribute of speech. If a debater uses a weak Qur'anic verse with ambiguous interpretation instead of a clear one, the opponent might refute it, undermining the truth of the position.
This shows the practical importance of choosing the strongest evidence.

Speech about what is not intended is a deviation from the desired goal. So it is incumbent upon the participant in the process of dialectic to commit to presenting his opinion on the issue under dispute, presenting his evidence and proofs clearly, as well as presenting his responses to what his opponent brings of evidence and proofs, while being cautious in all these stages against falling into fallacies and ambiguities that have no benefit except prolonging the dialectical process and diverting it from its path. Commentary
This passage presents three fundamental rules for the practitioner of dialectical debate (jadal). The author first warns against introducing irrelevant objections or fallacies that only prolong the debate and deviate it from its purpose. He quotes al-Tufi to emphasize that time is too precious to waste on deceptive arguments; the goal is establishing truth. The second rule is to always rely on the strongest evidence available. The author cites al-Juwayni's lengthy advice explaining that using weak arguments undermines one's credibility and the truth itself. Even if the opponent seems weak, a strong argument is necessary because knowledgeable listeners may be present. Using the strongest evidence first sets a tone of authority and forces the opponent into a defensive position. The author adds that this requires the debater to have thorough prior knowledge of the topic and to have examined each proof for its soundness, as al-Baji warns against using untested proofs that the opponent might expose. The third rule is the necessity of careful speech. The author reminds that the purpose of jadal is to support truth, not to win a verbal contest. Excessive and uncontrolled speech can lead the debater astray, even turning him into a defender of falsehood. Al-Juwayni's final advice stresses measured, deliberate speech, avoiding verbosity and rushing. This passage is an expansion on the ethics and methodology of dialectic, drawing on classical authorities to provide practical guidance.

Rule 3: Exercise Careful Speech
Author states third rule: necessity of careful speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of careful, measured speech in jadal to avoid defending falsehood and to keep the debate focused on truth.

Core Idea
Dialectic is a means to support truth, not an end; guarded speech prevents slipping into falsehood.

The third rule warns debaters to exercise careful speech (tathabbut). The author explains that jadal can easily become a verbal contest, causing the debater to forget the goal of truth and even defend falsehood. Al-Juwayni's final advice emphasizes measured, deliberate speech over verbosity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the danger of uncontrolled speech
The author says the debater may forget his primary motive and get carried away with verbal wrangling, turning into a defender of falsehood.
Key term: 'tathabbut' (carefulness, verification).
2
Understand the purpose of jadal
Jadal is a means to support truth, not an end in itself. The debater must always remember this to avoid misusing debate for ego or victory.
3
Extract al-Juwayni's advice on speech
Al-Juwayni advises: 'Do not accustom yourself to verbosity... be careful and alert. If speech becomes long and mixed with worthless content, ears become tired.'
This is a practical guideline for self-discipline.
4
Apply to a fiqh debate
In a debate about the ruling of riba, a debater might start arguing about peripheral issues. The third rule requires him to stay focused, speak only what is relevant, and avoid excessive elaboration that could lead to error.
This preserves the quality and direction of the debate.

الكلام على ما لم يقصده عدول عن الغرض المطلوب. فالواجب على المشارك في عملية الجدل أن يلتزم بإيراد رأيه في المسألة موضع أدلته النزاع، يورد وحججه واضحة بينة، كما يورد ردوده على ما يأتي به مخالفه من أدلة وبينات، مع الحذر في كل هذه الأطوار من الوقوع في الأغاليط والشبهات لا فائدة منها سوى تطويل العملية الجدلية والانحراف بها عن مسارها. قال الطوفي: «ولا يورد أحدهما على الآخر شبهة يعلم أنها لا ترد عليه أو لا تلزمه لأن الزمان أقصر من أن يضيع في الأغلوطات والتغليطات، وفي تحقيق الحق ما يستغرق الوقت». التمسك بأقوى الأدلة: إن عناية الباحث المجادل بالمسألة موضع النزاع وحرصه على تحقيق الحق فيها وشعوره بأنه يمثل هذا الحق في هذه المسألة من شأن ذلك كله أن يدفعه إلى البحث عن أقوى الأدلة وأسطع البراهين التي تثبت رأيه وتكشف تهافت رأي مخالفه، وأي تهاون منه في ذلك يكون دليلا على عدم يقينه بكون الحق في جانب رأيه وشعوره بأنه في جانب مخالفه. قال إمام الحرمين الجويني: إياك أن تتعلق عند الاستدلال إلا بأقوى ما في المسألة، ولا يغرنك ضعف السائل فربما يكون في الحاضرين من يضيق – بقوته في العلم - عليك الدنيا، وقد يتضح للخصم الضعيف عند التعلق بالشيء الذي لا يقوى في الاحتجاج ما يصعب عليك الخروج منه، ولأنك إذا تعلقت بأقوى ما في المسألة، راح بعده ما هو أضعف منه، وإذا تعلقت بالضعيف، احتجت بعده إلى وضع القوي موضع الضعيف فيذهب عند ذلك رونق نضرة الحق وبهائها، ولأنك إذا قدمت الضعيف استرذل الحاضرون كلامك، فيضعف خاطرك بعده عن إيراد ما هو أقوى منه إذا لم يستحوذوا في الأول كلامك، فتترك جميع ما تقوله عندهم، فلا تظهر نضرة الحق، ولأنك إذا بدهت الخصم بأقوى شيء في المسألة، هابك في الاحتجاج، فتؤثر هيبته تلك في تجنبه شبهته وتقوية بدعته. ويقتضي هذا أن يكون لدى المجادل دراسة سابقة للموضوع وخبرة قديمة به، وبراهينه وإحاطة كاملة بوجوه دلالتها، لأنه إن استدل بدليل لم يعرف وجه دلالته بدقة أو كان دليلا ضعيفا غير شاف في المسألة ربما كان ذلك سببا لضعف حجته وسقوط رأيه حتى وإن كان الحق في جانبه هو دون جانب مخالفه. ولذلك يؤكد الباجي على المجادل أن لا يستدل إلا بدليل قد وقف عليه وخبره وامتحنه قبل ذلك وعرف صحته وسلامته لأنه ربما يستدل بما لم يُمْعِنْ تأمله ولا تصحيحه، فيظفر به خصمه ويبين انقطاعه. ضرورة التثبت في الكلام: الجدل بما أنه مبادلة للكلام بين المتخالفين في مسألة من مسائل العلم والمعرفة، ربما كان سببًا إلى أن ينسى المجادل باعثه الأساس على الجدل وغايته الأولى منه، فينساق وراء المنازعة اللفظية ويكثر من الكلام بغرض إفحام المخالف، وقد يفضي به هذا إلى أن يتحول إلى مدافع عن الباطل بعد أن كان باعثه هو الدفاع عن الحق. لذلك من الضروري أن يدرك الباحث أن الجدل ليس غاية في حد ذاته وإنما وسيلة لنصرة الحق في المسألة المتنازع فيها، ولذلك لابد له أن يحرص على أن لا يقول إلا كلاما موزونا يعرف بداياته ونهاياته ويملك التحكم فيه وتوجيهه إلى هدفه. وقد أوصى إمام الحرمين الجويني كل من يشارك في عملية من عمليات الجدل قائلا: «لا تعود نفسك الإسهاب والجدال بالباطل والمبادرة إلى كل ما سبق إليه الخاطر واللسان، حتى إذا أورد ما أورده أو سمع ما سمعه، يكون في جميعه على التثبت والتيقظ، فإن الكلام إذا طال واشتمل على الغث والسمين، مجته الآذان.
Speech about what is not intended is a deviation from the desired goal. So it is incumbent upon the participant in the process of dialectic to commit to presenting his opinion on the issue under dispute, presenting his evidence and proofs clearly, as well as presenting his responses to what his opponent brings of evidence and proofs, while being cautious in all these stages against falling into fallacies and ambiguities that have no benefit except prolonging the dialectical process and diverting it from its path. Al-Tufi said: 'Neither of them should present to the other an ambiguity that he knows will not be refuted against him or does not obligate him, because time is too short to waste on fallacies and deceptions, and in establishing the truth there is enough to occupy the time.' Adhering to the strongest evidence: Indeed, the care of the researching debater for the issue under dispute and his eagerness to establish the truth in it and his feeling that he represents this truth in this issue – all of that should drive him to search for the strongest evidence and clearest proofs that establish his opinion and reveal the incoherence of his opponent's opinion. Any negligence on his part in that is evidence of his lack of certainty that the truth lies with his opinion and his feeling that it lies with his opponent. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said: 'Beware of relying in argumentation except on the strongest point in the issue. Do not be deceived by the weakness of the questioner, for perhaps among those present there is one who – by his strength in knowledge – will make life difficult for you. And it may become clear to the weak opponent, when relying on something that is not strong in argument, what makes it difficult for you to extricate yourself. Because if you rely on the strongest point in the issue, what is weaker than it will be set aside afterward. If you rely on the weak, you will later need to replace the weak with the strong, and then the luster and beauty of truth will disappear. And if you present the weak, the audience will despise your speech, and then your mind will weaken thereafter in presenting what is stronger than it, if they did not overwhelm your speech at first, so you leave all that you say with them, and the luster of truth does not appear. And if you confront the opponent with the strongest thing in the issue, he will fear you in argument, and that fear will affect him in avoiding his ambiguity and strengthening his innovation.' This necessitates that the debater have prior study of the subject and long experience with it, and his proofs and complete knowledge of the aspects of their indication, because if he argues with a proof without knowing precisely its aspect of indication, or if it is a weak proof not sufficient for the issue, that might be a cause for the weakness of his argument and the downfall of his opinion, even if the truth is on his side and not on his opponent's side. Therefore, al-Baji emphasizes that the debater should not argue except with a proof that he has stopped at, examined, tested before, and known its correctness and soundness, because perhaps he argues with something that he has not thoroughly contemplated or corrected, so his opponent gets hold of it and demonstrates its discontinuity. The necessity of carefulness in speech: Since dialectic is an exchange of speech between those who differ on a matter of knowledge and cognition, it may cause the debater to forget his primary motive for dialectic and its first goal, and he may get carried away with verbal wrangling and increase speech for the purpose of silencing the opponent, and this may lead him to become a defender of falsehood after his motive was to defend truth. Therefore, it is necessary for the researcher to realize that dialectic is not an end in itself but a means to support truth in the disputed issue. Therefore, he must be careful to say only measured speech whose beginnings and ends he knows, and he can control and direct it toward its goal. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni advised everyone who participates in a process of dialectic, saying: 'Do not accustom yourself to verbosity and arguing with falsehood and rushing into everything that comes to mind and tongue, so that when you present what you present or hear what you hear, you are in all of it careful and alert. For if speech becomes long and includes the worthless and the valuable, ears become tired.'
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents three fundamental rules for the practitioner of dialectical debate (jadal). The author first warns against introducing irrelevant objections or fallacies that only prolong the debate and deviate it from its purpose. He quotes al-Tufi to emphasize that time is too precious to waste on deceptive arguments; the goal is establishing truth. The second rule is to always rely on the strongest evidence available. The author cites al-Juwayni's lengthy advice explaining that using weak arguments undermines one's credibility and the truth itself. Even if the opponent seems weak, a strong argument is necessary because knowledgeable listeners may be present. Using the strongest evidence first sets a tone of authority and forces the opponent into a defensive position. The author adds that this requires the debater to have thorough prior knowledge of the topic and to have examined each proof for its soundness, as al-Baji warns against using untested proofs that the opponent might expose. The third rule is the necessity of careful speech. The author reminds that the purpose of jadal is to support truth, not to win a verbal contest. Excessive and uncontrolled speech can lead the debater astray, even turning him into a defender of falsehood. Al-Juwayni's final advice stresses measured, deliberate speech, avoiding verbosity and rushing. This passage is an expansion on the ethics and methodology of dialectic, drawing on classical authorities to provide practical guidance.

The Obligation of Polite Speech in Jadal
Author emphasizes the necessity of refined speech in jadal, citing Quran and scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why polite speech is obligatory in jadal and identify appropriate and inappropriate expressions in dialectical debate, citing Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Polite speech in jadal is a Quranic requirement that ensures the pursuit of truth over victory.

This passage from our coursebook establishes that refined speech is an absolute requirement for anyone engaging in jadal. The author not only states the rule but grounds it in the Quran and the practice of the early scholars, including warnings against common pitfalls like using vulgar phrases. By the end of this lesson, you will understand both the theoretical basis and practical application of this essential rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule and Its Rationale
Read the opening lines. The author states that both parties must adhere to 'refined speech' (al-qawl al-muhadhdhab). Note the rationale: it ensures success of the debate, fosters cooperation, and proves sincerity in seeking truth.
2
Locate the Quranic Evidence
Find the two Quranic verses cited: Surah An-Nahl (16:125) and Surah Al-Ankabut (29:46). Notice how they command arguing in 'the best way' and include exceptions only for wrongdoers.
The verses are the primary textual basis for the rule.
3
Analyze the Consequences of Violation
Read the section on what happens if polite speech is abandoned. The author warns that hurtful words lead to mutual fanaticism, loss of truth, and increased clinging to falsehood.
4
Examine Specific Scholarly Recommendations
Focus on the quotes from al-Baji and al-Tufi. Al-Baji advises avoiding debates with unjust or vulgar opponents, and if such behavior appears, gently forbid it. Al-Tufi lists specific vulgar expressions to avoid (e.g., elongated 'la', 'dha alayk') and recommends proper technical terms like 'la nusallim'.
Pay attention to al-Tufi's defense of 'la nusallim' against those who consider it rude.
