التقيد بالقول المهذب:
على كل واحد من طرفي الجدل أن يتقيد بالقول المهذب، البعيد عن كل طعن أو تجريح أو هزء أو سخرية أو احتقار لوجهة النظر التي يدعيها أو يدافع عنها من يحاوره.
إن تقيد المجادل بالقول المهذب في جدله ضروري لنجاح العملية الجدلية، وضمان استمرار روح الشعور بالتعاون المشترك للوصول إلى الحق في المسألة، كما أن التمسك بالقول المهذب واحترام المخالف وعدم الطعن فيه وتجريحه دليل على أن الهدف من الجدل هو ابتغاء الحق، وليس حب الظهور والغلبة على المخالف.
أما إن كان من ديدن المجادل أن يحتقر خصمه ويستعمل الألفاظ الجارحة في حواره معه، فإن ذلك دليل على عدم الإخلاص للحق، وهذا ما قد يدفع المخالف إلى معاملته بالمثل، ولا شك أن جدلاً أو حوارًا يجري بهذه الطريقة لن تكون نهايته إلا مزيدا من التمسك بالباطل والتعصب له على حساب الحق، وضياع الحقيقة العلمية في خضم السباب والشتائم المتبادلة.
وقد وجهنا القرآن الكريم إلى ضرورة التقيد في الجدل والحوار مع المخالفين بالقول المهذب البعيد عن كل طعن أو تجريح أو هزء أو سخرية، أو احتقار لوجهة النظر التي يدعيها أو يدافع عنها من نحاوره.
وقد أرشدنا الإسلام إلى التقيد بهذه القاعدة في نصوص كثيرة، منها قول الله تعالى لنبيه : ﴿ وَجَادِلْهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ ﴾ [سورة النحل: 125]، وقوله تعالى للمؤمنين : ﴿ وَلَا تُجَادِلُوا أَهْلَ الْكِتَابِ إِلَّا بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ إِلَّا الَّذِينَ ظَلَمُوا مِنْهُمْ وَقُولُوا آمَنَّا بِالَّذِي أُنزِلَ إِلَيْنَا وَأُنزِلَ إِلَيْكُمْ وَإِلَهُنَا وَإِلَهُكُمْ وَاحِدٌ وَنَحْنُ لَهُ مُسْلِمُونَ ﴾ [سورة العنكبوت: 46].
والتوجيه إلى الخطة التي هي أحسن، يشمل كل ما يتعلق بالجدال ويرتبط به ويرافقه ويصاحبه من قول وفكر وعمل، فالمسلم مطالب بأن يلتزم في مجادلته لإثبات الحق الذي يؤمن به وإقناع الناس به الخطة التي هي أحسن من كل خطة يمكن أن يتخذها الناس في مجادلاتهم، لذلك كان من أخلاق المسلم وآدابه مع خصوم دينه ومخالفي عقيدته، فضلا عن إخوانه المؤمنين أنه لا يسلك مسالك السب والشتم والطعن واللعن والهمز واللمز، والهزء والسخرية، والفحش والبذاءة.
لذلك كان لابد على الباحث عن الحق أن لا يجادل ولا يناظر من لا ينصف نفسه، ولا من عادته التسفه في الكلام ولا من عادته التفظيع، فإنه لا يستفيد بكلامه فائدة، فإن ظهر له من خصمه شيء من ذلك نهاه عنه بلطف ورفق، فإن اللطف في الأمور أنفع والرفق أنجع، فإن لم ينته عن ذلك، أعرض عن كلامه، ولم يقابله في أفعاله.
وقد أكد الإمام الطوفي ضرورة أن يلتزم كلا طرفي الجدل بهذا الأدب ويحرصا عليه جهدهما، ولا يحيدا عنه إلى غيره مهما كانت الظروف، فقال: وليجتنبا في مناظرتهما الألفاظ العامية السخيفة كقوله في المنع: (لا) ويطولها، أو في قلب الدليل أو فساد الوضع : ذا عليك) كما يستعمله بعض أخشان الزمان، أو يجعلان بحثهما مثل لغو اليمين فيقول هذا: (لا ولله)، وهذا : (بلى والله وليقل عوض : (ذا) عليك) : هذا الدليل ينعكس أو ينقلب أو هو فاسد الوضع وهو الذي يقتضي خلاف ما استدل به عليه، وعوض (لا) المطولة : لم قلت؟ أو لا نسلم واعلم أن كثيرًا من الأغمار ينقبض من (لا نسلم) ويزعم أنها رد أو تكذيب يضع من الرتبة ويغض من المنصب، وذلك غفلة عن معاني
Adherence to refined speech:
Each of the two sides in a dialectical debate must adhere to refined speech, far from any insult, discrediting, mockery, ridicule, or contempt for the viewpoint that one's interlocutor claims or defends.
Indeed, the debater's adherence to refined speech in his dialectic is necessary for the success of the dialectical process, and for ensuring the continuation of the spirit of feeling of shared cooperation to reach the truth in the matter. Moreover, clinging to refined speech, respecting the opponent, and not insulting or discrediting him is evidence that the goal of the dialectic is seeking the truth, and not love of appearance and victory over the opponent.
But if it is the debater's habit to despise his opponent and use hurtful words in his dialogue with him, then that is evidence of insincerity to the truth, and this may drive the opponent to treat him likewise. Undoubtedly, a debate or dialogue conducted in this manner will end only in increased clinging to falsehood and fanaticism for it at the expense of truth, and the loss of scientific truth in the midst of mutual insulting and reviling.
The Noble Qur'an has guided us to the necessity of adhering in dialectic and dialogue with opponents to refined speech, far from any insult, discrediting, mockery, ridicule, or contempt for the viewpoint that one we dialogue with claims or defends.
Islam has guided us to adhere to this rule in many texts, including the saying of Allah the Exalted to His Prophet: "And argue with them in a way that is best" [Surah An-Nahl: 125], and His saying to the believers: "And do not argue with the People of the Scripture except in a way that is best, except those who do wrong among them, and say, 'We believe in that which has been revealed to us and revealed to you, and our God and your God is one, and we are Muslims to Him in submission'" [Surah Al-Ankabut: 46].
The direction to the plan that is best includes everything related to dialectic, connected to it, accompanying and attending it of speech, thought, and action. The Muslim is required to adhere in his dialectic to prove the truth he believes in and to convince people of it, to the plan that is better than any plan people might adopt in their debates. Therefore, it is among the character traits and etiquette of the Muslim towards the opponents of his religion and those who disagree with his creed, let alone his believing brothers, that he does not take the paths of insult, abuse, slander, cursing, backbiting, belittling, mockery, ridicule, indecency, and obscenity.
Therefore, it is necessary for the seeker of truth not to engage in jadal or munazarah with one who is not just with himself, nor with one whose habit is to be foolish in speech, nor with one whose habit is to exaggerate, for he does not benefit from his speech. If something of that appears from his opponent, he should forbid him from it with gentleness and kindness, for gentleness in matters is more beneficial and kindness is more effective. If he does not desist from that, he should turn away from his speech and not reciprocate his actions.
Indeed, Imam al-Tufi emphasized the necessity that both sides in a debate adhere to this etiquette and strive hard for it, and not deviate from it to something else whatever the circumstances. He said: Let them both avoid in their disputation vulgar colloquial expressions such as saying in objection: 'No' and lengthening it, or in reversing the evidence or the corruption of the premise: 'That is against you' as is used by some recent ignoramuses, or making their discussion like useless oaths: one says 'No, by Allah' and the other 'Yes, by Allah'. Instead of 'That is against you', let him say: This evidence is reversed or convertible or its premise is corrupt, and it requires the opposite of what was argued with it. And instead of the elongated 'No', let him say: Why did you say? or: We do not accept. Know that many inexperienced people shrink from 'We do not accept' and claim it is a rejection or denial that lowers rank and diminishes status. That is heedlessness of the meanings.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the essential rule of polite speech (al-qawl al-muhadhdhab) in jadal (dialectical debate). The author opens by stating that both parties in a debate must adhere to refined speech, avoiding all forms of insult, discrediting, mockery, ridicule, or contempt toward the opponent's viewpoint. He then explains why: polite speech is necessary for the success of the debate, ensuring a cooperative spirit to reach truth. It is evidence that the goal is truth, not victory. Conversely, using hurtful words indicates insincerity, and will only lead to mutual fanaticism and loss of truth.

The author supports this with Quranic verses: Allah commands His Prophet to argue in the best way (Surah An-Nahl: 125) and believers to argue with People of the Scripture except wrongdoers in the best way (Surah Al-Ankabut: 46). The guidance to the best plan covers all aspects of debate: speech, thought, and action. Thus, a Muslim's character in debate includes avoiding insult, cursing, etc.

The passage then quotes Abu al-Walid al-Baji: the seeker of truth should not debate with those who are unjust or habitually foolish/vulgar; if such behavior appears, he should gently forbid it, and if that fails, turn away.

Finally, Imam al-Tufi's detailed advice: avoid vulgar colloquialisms like elongated 'No' or 'That is against you', and instead use proper technical expressions. He warns against taking offense at 'We do not accept' (la nusallim), which some mistakenly consider a slight.

Critique of 'La Nusallim' and Ibn 'Aqil's Rules for Stopping Debate
Author critiques misuse of 'la nusallim' and quotes Ibn 'Aqil on halting debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the psychological pitfalls of using 'la nusallim' in dialectics and apply Ibn 'Aqil's criteria for when to halt a debate.

Core Idea
The phrase 'la nusallim' can conceal arrogance; debate must stop when it devolves into abuse.

This passage addresses a common dialectical practice: refusing to concede an opponent's premise with the phrase 'la nusallim' (I do not concede). The author warns that this phrase is often misused as a cover for arrogance rather than a legitimate objection. He then quotes Ibn 'Aqil, who provides clear guidelines for when a debate must be halted: when one party engages in irrelevant or abusive speech, the other is not obliged to continue. This lesson examines the ethical and procedural dimensions of dialectical engagement.

Guided Path
1
Understand the function of 'la nusallim'
In dialectics, 'la nusallim' is used to reject a premise. But the author points out that it does not necessarily mean the premise is false in reality; the objector may be mistaken.
The term's standard use is a refusal to concede, but its psychological motivation matters.
2
Analyze the author's critique of psychological misuse
The author claims that repeated or stubborn use of 'la nusallim' often stems from arrogance (الكبر ورؤية النفس) and a desire to show status, not a quest for truth.
Recognize this as a critique of the debater's intention.
3
Examine Ibn 'Aqil's rule for stopping debate
Imam Ibn 'Aqil states that if an opponent starts using speech that is clearly not part of question and answer (e.g., cursing, irrelevant poetry), the other party is not required to listen. The debate should be halted to preserve its purpose.
The purpose is to maintain the honor and truth-seeking function of debate.
4
Apply the criterion to a hypothetical scenario
Consider a debate where one participant starts insulting the other's teacher. According to Ibn 'Aqil, the other participant may stop. This preserves the dignity of debate and ensures it remains a tool for truth, not partisanship.

الألفاظ وموضوعاتها، وذلك لأن موضوع (لا) نسلم لغة واصطلاحًا: «إني أنقاد لما تقول لأنه لم تثبت عندي صحته ولا يلزم من عدم ثبوت صحته عند الخصم أن لا يكون ثابتا في نفس الأمر لجواز أن يكون القائل (لا نسلم) مخطئًا في المنع وعدم التسليم في نفس الأمر، ثم لا يخلو إما أن تقول: كل مجتهد فلا مخطئ منهما، والمنع يتوجه إلى إصابة الأشبه أو الأرجح أو غير ذلك مع إصابة كل منهما في الجملة، أو تقول: ليس كل مجتهد مصيبا لكن المصيب غير معين فلا يلزم من قول القائل (لا نسلم) تخطئة أحد، نعم أحسب أن نفور النافر من (لا نسلم) إنما هو استعظامًا لمحل نفسه أن يرد عليه، لكن ذلك عن مصيب أنه مشير إلى الكبر ورؤية النفس وقصد إظهار المكانة لا إظهار الحق والإبانة، وفي ذلك من الذم ما يكفي، وللقلم عن استيعابه تجفي» كما أكد الإمام ابن عقيل ضرورة الكف عن الخوض والاستمرار في الجدل إذا بدا من أحد الطرفين ما يدل على انحراف العملية الجدلية عن مسارها الصحيح واتجاهها وجهة التعصب للرأي وبطر الحق، فقال: وبكل حال فإنه لا يصلح الاستبقاء على الخصم إذا ابتدأ بما يتموه أنه سؤال أو جواب، وأما إن أتى بما لا يتموه مثله فليس على خصمه استماعه، وذلك إذا أخذ في السباب وما جرى مجرى ذلك من التخليط الذي لا يشكل على عاقل ليس من السؤال والجواب في شيء، ومثله في ذلك مثل من ابتدأ في إنشاد الشعر على طريق الاستشهاد وابتدأ في شيء من أخبار الزمان وتصرف الأحوال الذي لا يشتبه على عاقل أنه ليس من السؤال والجواب في شيء) والغرض من الصد عن الجدل في مثل هذه الحالات هو الحفاظ على شرف العملية الجدلية، والتأكيد على ضرورة انصرافها إلى تحقيق الحق وإبطال الباطل، والارتفاع بها عن أن تتحول إلى أداة لنصرة الآراء الباطلة والتعصب لأهلها.
[This concerns] expressions and their subjects, because the subject of 'I do not concede' linguistically and terminologically: 'I submit to what you say because its correctness is not established for me, and the fact that it is not established for the opponent does not necessitate that it is not established in reality, for it is possible that the one saying 'I do not concede' is mistaken in the prohibition and non-concession in reality. Then it is either that you say: Every mujtahid is not mistaken from among them, and the prohibition is directed to the correctness of the most similar or the most probable or something else, with each of them being correct in general; or you say: Not every mujtahid is correct, but the correct one is unspecified, so the statement of the one saying 'I do not concede' does not necessitate declaring anyone mistaken. Yes, I think that the aversion of the avoider to 'I do not concede' is only due to his magnifying his own station that [the statement] is directed against him, but that indicates arrogance and self-importance and the intent to show status, not to show truth and clarification. In that there is sufficient blame, and the pen refrains from encompassing it.' As Imam Ibn 'Aqil emphasized the necessity of refraining from delving and persisting in debate if something appears from one of the two parties indicating deviation of the dialectical process from its correct path and its direction towards partisanship and arrogance of truth. He said: In any case, it is not appropriate to persist with the opponent if he begins with what he pretends to be a question or answer, but if he brings what is not pretended like that, then his opponent is not obliged to listen to him. That is when he takes to cursing and what runs in that vein of confusion that is not ambiguous to a rational person that it is not part of question and answer. Its example in that is like one who begins reciting poetry by way of citation and begins something of the news of the time and the disposition of circumstances that it is not ambiguous to a rational person that it is not part of question and answer. The purpose of preventing debate in such situations is to preserve the honor of the dialectical process, and to emphasize the necessity of its turning to achieving truth and abolishing falsehood, and elevating it from becoming a tool for supporting false opinions and partisanship for their people.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the misuse of the phrase 'la nusallim' (I do not concede) in debate, followed by a guideline from Imam Ibn 'Aqil on when to abandon a debate. The author begins by explaining that when someone says 'la nusallim,' it does not necessarily mean the opponent's argument is false; the speaker may be mistaken in refusing to concede. He then analyzes the psychology: often, refusal to concede stems from arrogance and a desire to show status, not from a truth-seeking motive. This is blameworthy. Then, Ibn 'Aqil is quoted: debate should be stopped if the opponent begins with irrelevant or abusive speech that is clearly not genuine question-and-answer, such as cursing or reciting poetry. The purpose is to preserve the dignity of debate and ensure it remains a tool for truth, not for partisanship.

ابن عقيل

Key Takeaway
In dialectics, the phrase 'la nusallim' should be used only as a genuine objection, not a cover for arrogance; and debate must be halted when it becomes abusive or irrelevant, to preserve its truth-seeking purpose.

The Rule of Narration and Claim: Burden of Proof in Debate
Author outlines two logical methods and a rule for verifying transmitted vs. claimed statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule 'if narrating, verify authenticity; if claiming, provide proof' to distinguish between narrative and claim in a dialectical exchange and identify the appropriate requirement.

Core Idea
In debate, know your burden: narrators verify chains; claimants produce proofs.

The scholars of adab al-bahth distilled a core principle that governs every dialectical exchange: any proposition is either a transmitted report or an asserted claim. This lesson unpacks that rule and shows you how to decide what is required of each party.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Listen to the statement in the debate. Is it presented as something received from an authority (khabar) or as a personal assertion (da'wa)?
If it begins with 'the Prophet said' or 'the scholars agreed', it is a narration.
2
If narration, demand authentication
The narrator must verify the chain of transmission (sanad) and remove any doubt ('dakhīla') about its connection to the source.
This mirrors hadith criticism: checking continuity and reliability of narrators.
3
If claim, demand proof
The claimant must provide evidence (dalīl) appropriate to the nature of the claim. No proof, no acceptance.
The type of proof depends on the subject matter, as explained in Lesson 2.
4
Apply the rule to a sample debate
Take a debate on the permissibility of ribā: one cites a hadith (narration), another argues by analogy (claim). Check which requirement applies to each.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
5 - التزام الطرق المنطقية السليمة في الجدل: إن آداب الجدل السابق ذكرها لا يمكن تحقيق أي منها إذا ما جرت العملية الجدلية على غير الطرق المنطقية والفطرية السليمة التي يقتضيها العقل وتتطلبها الرغبة الصادقة في الوصول إلى الحق في المسألة المختلف فيها. ومن التزام الطرق المنطقية السليمة، ما يلي: أولا: تقديم الأدلة المثبتة أو المرجحة للأمور المدعاة. ثانيا: إثبات صحة النقل للأمور المنقولة أو المروية. ومن ذلك أخذ علماء فن آداب البحث والمناظرة قاعدتهم المشهورة: إن كنت ناقلا فالصحة أو مدعيا فالدليل. والالتزام بهذه القاعدة من كلا طرفي العملية الجدلية، يجمع بين طياته كل آداب الجدل السابق ذكرها، ويكفل - بالتأكيد - الوصول إلى الحق المبتغى في المسألة. وتفصيل معنى هذه القاعدة: أن موضوع البحث لا يخلو دائما من أن يكون خبرا منقولا، أو دعوى مزعومة. فأما ما قد يكون منه خبرا، فإن البحث فيه ينبغي أن يكون محصورا في تحقيق النسبة بينه وبين مصدره إذ هي التي تكون مثارا للاحتمال والدخيلة والريب، فإن زال الاحتمال وانجابت الغاشية انبثقت من ذلك الخبر حقيقة علمية معينة، بشرط أن يكون ذا دلالة قطعية. وأما ما يكون منه ادعاء، فإن البحث فيه ينبغي أن يتجه إلى الأدلة العلمية المنسجمة معه والتي من شأنها أن تكشف مدى صدق هذا الادعاء. ولكل نوع من الدعاوى نوع من الأدلة العلمية يناسبها، لا يستبدل به غيره؛ فالدعاوى المتعلقة بطبائع الأشياء المادية وجوهرها لا تنهض بغير البراهين العلمية التجريبية المحسوسة، والدعاوى المتعلقة بالمجردات كالأرقام والنفس والمنطق لا يقبل معها إلا براهينها القانونية المسلمة، والدعاوى المتعلقة بالحقوق والأحوال المدنية لا ينفع معها إلا البينات والحجاج المتفق على ضرورة ارتباطها بها. وهكذا لا تصبح الدعوى حقيقة علمية ثابتة إلا بعد أن يقترن بها دليلها الذي يناسبها، فالدليل الذي قد يساق إلى الدعوى ليست له أي قيمة علمية ما لم يكن بينهما انسجام في الطبيعة والنوع.
5 - Adherence to Sound Logical Methods in Dialectics: The previously mentioned etiquette of dialectics cannot be achieved if the dialectical process proceeds according to other than sound logical and innate methods required by the intellect and demanded by the sincere desire to reach the truth in the disputed matter. Among the adherence to sound logical methods are the following: First: Presenting proofs that establish or preponderate for the claimed matters. Second: Establishing the authenticity of transmission for narrated or reported matters. From this, the scholars of the art of the etiquette of investigation and debate took their famous rule: 'If you are a narrator, then [establish] authenticity; if you are a claimant, then [provide] proof.' Adherence to this rule by both sides of the dialectical process combines all the previously mentioned etiquette of dialectics and guarantees - certainly - reaching the desired truth in the matter. The elaboration of the meaning of this rule: The subject of investigation is always either a narrated report or a claimed assertion. As for what may be a report, the investigation should be confined to verifying the relationship between it and its source, for that is what gives rise to possibility, extraneousness, and doubt. If the possibility disappears and the covering is removed, a definite scientific fact emerges from that report, provided it is of definitive indication. As for what may be a claim, the investigation should be directed to the scientific evidence consistent with it, which would reveal the extent of the truthfulness of this claim. For each type of claim there is a type of scientific evidence that suits it, not to be substituted by another; for claims concerning the natures of material things and their essence cannot be established except by empirical, sensory, scientific proofs; claims concerning abstractions such as numbers, the soul, and logic are only accepted with their recognized legal proofs; claims concerning rights and civil conditions are only helped by clear proofs and arguments that are agreed upon as necessarily connected to them. Thus a claim does not become a fixed scientific fact unless it is accompanied by its appropriate evidence, and the evidence that may be adduced for the claim has no scientific value unless there is harmony in nature and type between them.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage outlines the necessity of adhering to sound logical methods in dialectic. It begins by stating that proper etiquette cannot be realized without following logical and innate methods demanded by reason and the sincere desire for truth. The author then lists two concrete requirements: first, provide proofs that establish or preponderate for claimed matters; second, verify the authenticity of transmitted reports. From these, the scholars derived the famous rule: 'If you are a narrator, ensure authenticity; if you are a claimant, provide proof.' This rule encapsulates all prior etiquette. The passage elaborates: every subject of investigation is either a transmitted report (khabar) or a claim (da'wa). For a report, the investigation focuses on verifying its chain to the source, removing doubt; if the report is definitive in indication, a scientific fact emerges. For a claim, investigation seeks evidence appropriate to its type. The author then categorizes claims: material claims require empirical proof; abstract claims (numbers, soul, logic) require rational/legal proofs; social/civil claims require documentary evidence. The key principle is that evidence must suit the nature of the claim; otherwise it has no scientific value.

ناقلا
مدعيا
دخيلة

A) Authenticity of transmission
B) Proof appropriate to the claim
C) A citation from a classical text
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
The fundamental rule of dialectic is: narrators authenticate, claimants provide proof; evidence must match the nature of the claim.

Matching Evidence to Claim: Material, Abstract, and Civil
Author categorizes claims and specifies appropriate proof types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a claim as material, abstract, or civil and identify the corresponding evidence type required (empirical, rational, or documentary).

Core Idea
Evidence must match the nature of the claim: sensory for matter, rational for abstractions, documentary for civil matters.

Not all evidence is created equal. The same rule that demands proof for claims also requires that the proof be of the right kind. This lesson breaks down the three categories of claims and their appropriate proofs as outlined by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the domain of the claim
Listen to the claim. Is it about physical objects and their natures (material), about concepts like numbers, the soul, or logic (abstract), or about rights and social affairs (civil)?
Example: 'Fire burns' is material; '2+2=4' is abstract; 'Zakat is due on wealth' is civil.
2
Determine the evidence type
For material claims, evidence must be empirical: experimentation and sensory observation. For abstract claims, evidence must be rational or axiomatic ('barāhīn qānūniyya'). For civil claims, evidence is documentary (bayyināt) and agreed-upon arguments.
The author uses 'barāhīn qānūniyya' to mean proofs that are legally recognised as sound.
3
Check harmony between claim and evidence
Ask: Is the evidence of the same nature and type as the claim? If you use a rational proof for a material claim, it is inappropriate and has no scientific value.
Example: Using logical syllogism to prove the chemical composition of water is insufficient without empirical testing.
4
Apply to a sample from fiqh
Take the claim 'Wine is prohibited because it intoxicates' (illegal cause). The claim is about a ruling (civil/legal), but the evidence involves a material property (intoxication). Is this appropriate? It is a mix: the 'illah is a property, but the ruling is legal; this is common in qiyas.
Consider whether the evidence matches the claim's nature according to the author's categories.

The Foundational Rule of Dialectic: Narration vs. Claim
Author states the pivotal dialectical rule and explains its application to narration and claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the dialectical rule 'If you are a narrator, then accuracy; if a claimant, then evidence' to categorize any proposition in a debate and determine the appropriate method of verification.

Core Idea
In Islamic debate, every proposition is either a narrated report requiring authentication or a claim requiring appropriate evidence.

Dialectic (jadal) is the structured method of debate in the Islamic tradition. At its heart lies a simple yet profound rule that distinguishes between two types of propositions: reports that must be verified for authenticity, and claims that must be supported by evidence. This rule, formulated by the scholars of adab al-bahth, governs how every debate proceeds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
In any debate, determine whether the proposition under discussion is a transmitted report (khabar) or a claim (da'wa). A report is something attributed to an external source; a claim is an assertion that stands on its own.
2
For reports: assess authenticity (sihha)
If the proposition is a report, the debate should focus on verifying its chain of transmission and the reliability of its source. The goal is to determine whether the report is accurate (sahih) or not.
The passage stresses that the research is restricted to verifying the relationship between the report and its source.
3
For claims: identify the appropriate evidence (dalil)
If it is a claim, you must bring evidence that matches the nature of the claim. The passage outlines three types: empirical proofs for material things, rational proofs for abstractions, and legal proofs for rights and civil matters.
4
Check harmony between claim and evidence
Even if evidence is provided, it has no value unless it is in harmony with the claim in nature and type. For example, a legal claim about inheritance cannot be proven by an empirical experiment.
The passage states that evidence without harmony has no scientific value.
5
Apply the rule to practice
When you hear an argument in a debate, immediately classify the proposition and check if the debater is following the rule. If they are a narrator, have they verified accuracy? If a claimant, have they provided the right kind of evidence?

الجدلية على غير الطرق المنطقية والفطرية السليمة التي يقتضيها العقل وتتطلبها الرغبة الصادقة في الوصول إلى الحق في المسألة المختلف فيها و من التزام الطرق المنطقية السليمة، ما يلي: أولا : تقديم الأدلة المثبتة أو المرجحة للأمور المدعاة. ثانيا : إثبات صحة النقل للأمور المنقولة أو المروية . (1) ومن ذلك أخذ علماء فن آداب البحث والمناظرة قاعدتهم المشهورة: إن كنت ناقلا ،فالصحة أو مدعيًا فالدليل والالتزام بهذه القاعدة من كلا طرفي العملية الجدلية، يجمع بين طياته كل آداب الجدل السابق ذكرها ، ويكفل - بالتأكيد - الوصول إلى الحق المبتغى في المسألة وتفصيل معنى هذه القاعدة: أن موضوع البحث لا يخلو دائما من أن يكون خبرًا منقولا، أو دعوى مزعومة، فأما ما قد يكون منه خبرًا، فإن البحث فيه ينبغي أن يكون محصورا في تحقيق النسبة بينه وبين مصدره إذ هي التي تكون مثارًا للاحتمال والدخيلة والريب، فإن زال الاحتمال وانجابت الغاشية انبثقت من ذلك الخبر حقيقة علمية معينة، بشرط أن يكون ذا دلالة قطعية، وأما ما يكون منه ادعاء، فإن البحث فيه أن ينبغي يتجه إلى الأدلة العلمية المنسجمة معه والتي من شأنها أن تكشف مدى صدق هذا الادعاء، ولكل نوع من الدعاوى نوع من الأدلة العلمية يناسبها، لا يستبدل به غيره ؛ فالدعاوى المتعلقة بطبائع الأشياء المادية وجوهرها لا تنهض بغير البراهين العلمية التجريبية المحسوسة، والدعاوى المتعلقة بالمجردات كالأرقام والنفس والمنطق لا يقبل معها إلا براهينها القانونية المسلمة، والدعاوى المتعلقة بالحقوق والأحوال المدنية لا ينفع معها إلا البينات والحجاج المتفق على ضرورة ارتباطها بها، وهكذا لا تصبح الدعوى حقيقة علمية ثابتة إلا بعد أن يقترن بها دليلها الذي يناسبها، فالدليل الذي قد يساق إلى الدعوى ليست له أي قيمة علمية ما لم يكن بينهما انسجام في الطبيعة والنوع (2) .
Dialectic that does not follow the sound logical and intuitive methods required by reason and demanded by the sincere desire to reach the truth in the disputed matter. And from the adherence to sound logical methods are the following: First: presenting proofs that confirm or give weight to the claimed matters. Second: verifying the authenticity of transmitted or narrated matters. (1) From that, the scholars of the art of debate and disputation derived their famous rule: 'If you are a narrator, then [seek] accuracy; if a claimant, then [provide] evidence.' Adherence to this rule by both parties of the dialectical process encompasses all the previously mentioned etiquettes of debate and guarantees - certainly - reaching the desired truth in the matter. The detailed meaning of this rule: the subject of research never fails to be either a transmitted report or a claimed assertion. As for what may be a report, the research concerning it should be restricted to verifying the relationship between it and its source, for that is what gives rise to possibility, interpolation, and doubt. If the possibility disappears and the covering is removed, a certain scientific truth emerges from that report, provided that it has definitive indication. As for what may be a claim, the research concerning it should be directed towards the scientific evidence consistent with it and which would reveal the extent of the truthfulness of this claim. For every type of claim there is a type of scientific evidence appropriate to it, not replaceable by another. Claims concerning the natures of material things and their essence are not established except by empirical scientific experimental proofs. Claims concerning abstractions like numbers, the soul, and logic are only accepted with their established legal proofs. Claims concerning rights and civil conditions are only useful with proofs and arguments agreed upon as necessarily connected to them. Likewise, a claim does not become a fixed scientific truth except after it is accompanied by its appropriate evidence. Evidence that may be brought to a claim has no scientific value unless there is harmony between them in nature and type. (2)
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage defines the foundational principle of Islamic dialectic: every subject of debate is either a transmitted report (khabar) or a claim (da'wa). For reports, the focus must be on verifying authenticity (sihha) through isnad criticism, ensuring the report has definitive indication (dalala qat'iyya). For claims, appropriate evidence (dalil) must be brought, tailored to the nature of the claim: empirical proofs for material objects, rational proofs for abstractions, and legal proofs for rights and civil matters. The rule 'If you are a narrator, then accuracy; if a claimant, then evidence' summarizes this. The passage emphasizes that evidence without harmony with the claim has no scientific value. In the Islamic intellectual tradition, this rule is applied, for instance, when debating a hadith: one must first verify its chain, then if it is authentic, use it as evidence. When debating a legal ruling, one must bring appropriate scriptural or analogical evidence.

مدعيا
دليل
خبر
دعوى

C) Use any argument regardless of its type.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic debate, every proposition is either a report to be verified for authenticity or a claim to be supported by evidence appropriate to its nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Ethical Imperative Against Deceptive Tricks in Dialectic
Categorization of trick-users and examples of deceptive strategies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and categorize deceptive tricks used in Islamic dialectic and explain why they are prohibited according to al-Juwayni.

Core Idea
Truth is not a license for trickery; even the truth-holder must argue fairly.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), the goal is to establish truth through fair argumentation. However, some debaters resort to deceptive tricks (hiyal) to win. This lesson, based on Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, categorizes those who use tricks and gives examples of common deceptive strategies, teaching you to recognize and avoid them in your own debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of trick-users
Find in the passage the two categories: the mubtil (falsehood-holder who knows the truth is against him) and the muhaqq (truth-holder who lacks proper argumentation). Note how both are condemned.
2
Recognize the first trick: ambiguous language
The trickster uses obscure phrases so the opponent understands little. When challenged, he denies his own words. Analyze how this blocks true discussion.
This is a form of evasion.
3
Recognize the second trick: linguistic nitpicking
When unable to answer, the trickster harps on the opponent's every word, distracting from the substance. Notice how it derails the debate.
4
Recognize the third trick: irrelevant eloquence
The trickster uses bombastic, eloquent phrases unrelated to the issue to change the subject. Identify how this misleads.
5
Connect to the Islamic ethical framework
The passage says this is the habit of the wicked and distances one from the path of good. See how al-Juwayni's prohibition ties to commanding good and forbidding evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
6 - الاحتراز من استعمال الحيل والمغالطات في الجدل : الأسباب الدافعة إلى تضييع الجدل لهدفه الذي أقيم لأجله؛ استعمال الحيلة فيه، والذي يقوم بهذا العمل أحد رجلين : من إما مبطل يعرف أن الحق في جانب مخالفه ولذلك يعمل على تضليله ومحاولة تغليطه . أو محق ولكن لا يعرف الوسائل المشروع استعمالها في الدفاع عن الحق الذي معه، حيث تعوزه الأدلة أو يفتقد إلى القدرة على الجدل. وفي كلتا الحالتين فإن استعمال الحيلة في الجدل عمل غير مشروع، بل لقد اعتبره إمام الحرمين الجويني عملا محظورًا، يجب الاجتناب عنه، وهو من دأب أهل الفسوق في المناظرة، ومن عمل من قصده بالمناظرة المماراة لأهل السفه، ومجانبًا لطريق أهل الديانة والنصيحة، بعيدًا عن سلوك سبيل الخير والأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر (1) هذا، وإن صور الحيل التي قد تستعمل في الجدل كثيرة لا تحصى، أورد منها إمام الحرمين عددًا من الصور، نختار بعضها : منها : أن يحتال أحد المتجادلين على الآخر باستعمال الغامض من العبارات لا يفهم مخالفه من كلامه إلا القليل، لكثرة ما يكون فيه من الغموض والإجمال وغريب اللغة، فإذا أراد المخالف أن يرد عليه أو يحكي من كلامه شيئًا ليعلق عليه اعترضه بقوله : لم أقل هكذا ولم أرد ما تحكيه، أو يقول : لم تفهم حتى ما قلت . ومنها : أنه إذا أحس بقصوره عن الجواب على اعتراضات المخالف، فإنه يتعلق بعبارات وألفاظ مخالفه ويضايقه في كل حرف ويناقشه كل كلمة. ويحاول - إضافة إلى ذلك - أن يخرجه عن الموضوع بإيراد العبارات الطنانة وبترديد الفصاحات البليغة فيما لا يتعلق بفائدة المسألة ومنها : أن يحتال المسؤول على السائل فيخرجه عن سؤاله، أو يحتال السائل ...
6 - Avoidance of using tricks and fallacies in debate: The reasons that drive debate away from its goal for which it was established are the use of tricks. The one who does this is one of two men: either a falsehood-holder who knows the truth is with his opponent and therefore works to mislead him and attempt to cause him to err; or a truth-holder but who does not know the legitimate means to use in defending the truth he has, lacking evidence or lacking ability in debate. In both cases, using tricks in debate is illegitimate. Indeed, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni considered it a prohibited act, which must be avoided. It is the habit of the wicked in debate, and whoever aims by debate to contest with the foolish, distancing from the path of the religious and the sincere, far from following the way of good and commanding good and forbidding evil. (1) And indeed, the forms of tricks that may be used in debate are numerous and uncountable. Imam al-Haramayn listed a number of them, from which we select some: Among them: that one of the debaters tricks the other by using ambiguous phrases such that his opponent understands only a little from his speech, due to the abundance of obscurity, ambiguity, and strange language. When the opponent wants to respond or quote something from his speech to comment on it, he objects saying, 'I did not say this,' or 'I did not mean what you are quoting,' or says, 'You did not understand what I said.' Among them: when he feels his inability to answer the opponent's objections, he clings to the opponent's phrases and words and harasses him over every letter and argues over every word. He also tries to divert him from the subject by using bombastic phrases and repeating eloquent expressions that have no benefit to the issue. Among them: that the respondent tricks the questioner by diverting him from his question, or the questioner...
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage addresses the ethical dimension of dialectic, explicitly prohibiting deceptive tricks. It identifies two types of trick-users: the false opponent (mubtil) who knows the truth is against him and intentionally misleads, and the truth-holder (muhaqq) who lacks proper means or ability and resorts to tricks out of weakness. Both are condemned, and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni is cited as declaring it haram (prohibited). The passage then provides real examples of tricks used in debate: using ambiguous language to evade understanding, then denying what was said; nitpicking over words to distract; and diverting the topic with irrelevant eloquent speech. These examples serve as warnings to debaters. In Islamic scholarship, such practices are contrasted with the ideal of sincere debate aimed at truth, where clarity, honesty, and relevance are paramount. For instance, in a fiqh debate, a debater might deliberately use obscure terminology to confuse the opponent, which is condemned here.

D) Recommended for novice debaters.

Key Takeaway
Deceptive tricks have no place in Islamic dialectic; even the truth-holder must argue fairly and avoid all forms of manipulation.

The Prohibition of Tricks in Jadal
Author enumerates prohibited tricks in jadal citing Juwayni.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of people who resort to tricks in jadal and explain why Juwayni considered such tricks prohibited.

Core Idea
Tricks in jadal undermine truth; Juwayni declared them prohibited and characteristic of immoral disputants.

Dialectical disputation (jadal) is a tool for truth, but it can be corrupted when participants use tricks. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni strongly condemned such practices. In this passage, the author explains why people resort to tricks—either out of falsehood or inability—and lists several specific maneuvers to avoid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of trick users
The passage states that a trick user is either a falsifier (mubtil) who knows the truth is against him, or a truth-holder (muhaqq) who lacks proper argumentative tools.
2
Understand Juwayni's ruling
Al-Juwayni considered using tricks a prohibited act (mahzur) and a habit of immoral people. Note the moral and religious dimension: it is against the command to enjoin good and forbid evil.
3
Examine the first trick: obscure language
One disputant uses ambiguous, rare expressions so that the opponent understands little; when the opponent responds, he denies saying what was understood.
4
Examine the second trick: nitpicking words
When unable to answer, the disputant harps on every word and letter of the opponent's speech, diverting from the core issue.
5
Examine the third trick: feigning distraction
If overwhelmed, the disputant pretends to be distracted, looking away or starting another conversation, to imply the opponent's argument is weak while secretly seeking a response.

6 - الاحتراز من استعمال الحيل والمغالطات في الجدل : الأسباب الدافعة إلى تضييع الجدل لهدفه الذي أقيم لأجله؛ استعمال الحيلة فيه، والذي يقوم بهذا العمل أحد رجلين : إما مبطل يعرف أن الحق في جانب مخالفه ولذلك يعمل على تضليله ومحاولة تغليطه . أو محق ولكن لا يعرف الوسائل المشروع استعمالها في الدفاع عن الحق الذي معه، حيث تعوزه الأدلة أو يفتقد إلى القدرة على الجدل. وفي كلتا الحالتين فإن استعمال الحيلة في الجدل عمل غير مشروع، بل لقد اعتبره إمام الحرمين الجويني عملا محظورًا، يجب الاجتناب عنه، وهو من دأب أهل الفسوق في المناظرة، ومن عمل من قصده بالمناظرة المماراة لأهل السفه، ومجانبًا لطريق أهل الديانة والنصيحة، بعيدًا عن سلوك سبيل الخير والأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر (1) هذا، وإن صور الحيل التي قد تستعمل في الجدل كثيرة لا تحصى، أورد منها إمام الحرمين عددًا من الصور، نختار بعضها : منها : أن يحتال أحد المتجادلين على الآخر باستعمال الغامض من العبارات لا يفهم مخالفه من كلامه إلا القليل، لكثرة ما يكون فيه من الغموض والإجمال وغريب اللغة، فإذا أراد المخالف أن يرد عليه أو يحكي من كلامه شيئًا ليعلق عليه اعترضه بقوله : لم أقل هكذا ولم أرد ما تحكيه، أو يقول : لم تفهم حتى ما قلت . ومنها : أنه إذا أحس بقصوره عن الجواب على اعتراضات المخالف، فإنه يتعلق بعبارات وألفاظ مخالفه ويضايقه في كل حرف ويناقشه كل كلمة. ويحاول - إضافة إلى ذلك - أن يخرجه عن الموضوع بإيراد العبارات الطنانة وبترديد الفصاحات البليغة فيما لا يتعلق بفائدة المسألة ومنها : أن يحتال المسؤول على السائل فيخرجه عن سؤاله، أو يحتال السائل على المسؤول فيخرجه عن جوابه إلى غيره فإذا لم ينتبه كل واحد منهما لذلك عن صاحبه، لم تظهر صحة السؤال ولا صحة الجواب ولا الفساد، فيحصل الكلام بلا فائدة ومنها أن الواحد منهم إذا اختبر بقوة كلام خصمه وعلم من نفسه أنه لا يمكنه الرد عليه أو التخلص من مقتضاه ؛ أوهم الحاضرين ضعف كلام خصمه بأن يترك الإقبال عليه ويلتفت يمينا وشمالا، كالمتغافل المشتغل في فكره وخاطره بشيء آخر، أو يأخذ مع غيره في حديث، محاولا أن يوهم أن خصمه لا يجد عليه إلا ما هو ضعيف ركيك لا يساوي الاستماع إليه، وهو في التفاتاته وإبداء تغافله يتفكر ويتدبر ويرقي في طلب الجواب عنه، فإن حضره الجواب أجاب بما يحضره، وإن تعذر عليه الجواب تهاون بذلك، وقال : قد أكثرنا وفرغنا عما فيه التحصيل من السؤال ومنها : أنه إذا علم ضعف حجته، يحتال في ترويج ما عنده بالالتفات والإقبال على كل أحد من أهل المجلس، مع التبسم، مستشهدا بهم، مستميلا في إقباله عليهم لحركوا له رؤوسهم ويوافقوا على كلامه»(1) وقد جمع بعض العلماء صور الحيل والمغالطات في الجدل في أنواع ثلاثة، هي: «أحدها : نقل السائل عن سؤال ؛ وإنما يكون عند استشهاده على المجيب بما يلزمه بقطعه، فإذا أراد المجيب نقله جحد بعض ما استشهد به عليه وإن كان واضحا، فإذا بينه اختلط الكلامان وبه ينقله من المسألة الأولى إلى غيرها، فيجب على السائل إمعان النظر في مثل هذا والثاني : تقسيم السؤال ؛ وهو أن ينظر المجيب إلى أحواله، فإن كان محتملا لوجوه شتی قسمه على وجوهه ليطيل مناظرة السائل بشغل قلبه عن قوة المناظرة، فيبطل غرض السائل في الجدل، فالواجب على السائل أن لا يمكنه من التقسيم لئلا يلتبس غرضه عليه الثالث : ضرب الأمثال ؛ وهو أن يقصد تكثير الأمثال المضروبة في القرآن ليجبن خصمه، مثل أن يورد في جواب دعواه قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّ أَوْهَنَ الْبُيُوتِ لَبَيْتُ الْعَنكَبوتِ لَو كَانُوا يَعْلَمُونَ ﴾ (2) ، وقوله : ﴿كَرَمَادٍ اشْتَدَّتْ بِهِ الرِّيحُ فِي يَوْمٍ عَاصِفٍ (3) ، فإذا
6 - Avoiding the use of tricks and fallacies in dialectics: The reasons driving the waste of dialectics from its goal for which it was established are the use of tricks in it; and the one who does this is one of two men: either a falsifier who knows that the truth is on the side of his opponent and therefore works to mislead him and attempt to make him err; or a truth-holder but who does not know the legitimate means to be used in defending the truth he has, lacking proofs or lacking ability in dialectics. In both cases, using trickery in dialectics is an illegitimate act; indeed Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni considered it a prohibited act that must be avoided, and it is the habit of immoral people in disputation, and the practice of one whose aim in disputation is wrangling with the foolish, shunning the way of religious people and sincere advisors, far from the path of goodness and enjoining good and forbidding evil. (1) This, and the forms of tricks that may be used in dialectics are many and uncountable; Imam al-Haramayn listed a number of them; we select some: Among them: that one of the two disputants uses trickery on the other by using obscure expressions such that his opponent understands little of his speech, due to the abundance of ambiguity, generality, and strange language; when the opponent wants to respond or report something from his speech to comment on it, he objects by saying: 'I did not say this, and I did not mean what you are reporting,' or he says: 'You did not even understand what I said.' Among them: that when he senses his inability to answer the opponent's objections, he clings to the opponent's expressions and words, harasses him over every letter, and argues over every word. He also tries to divert him from the subject by uttering flamboyant phrases and repeating eloquent speech on matters unrelated to the benefit of the issue. Among them: that the respondent uses trickery on the questioner to divert him from his question, or the questioner uses trickery on the respondent to divert him from his answer to something else; if each of them is not aware of that from his companion, then neither the correctness of the question nor the correctness of the answer nor the corruption is manifest, so speech occurs without benefit. Among them: that when one of them tests the strength of his opponent's speech and knows from himself that he cannot refute it or escape its implication, he makes the attendees imagine the weakness of his opponent's speech by turning away from it and looking right and left, like one feigning heedlessness occupied in his thought and mind with something else, or he starts a conversation with someone else, trying to pretend that his opponent only offers what is weak and flimsy, not worth listening to; while in his turnings and feigning heedlessness he reflects and ponders and seeks a response to it; if an answer occurs to him, he responds with what is present; if he is unable to answer, he belittles it and says: 'We have done enough and finished with what is productive of the question.' Among them: that when he knows the weakness of his evidence, he uses trickery to promote what he has by turning and facing everyone in the gathering, with a smile, seeking their testimony, inclining in his approach to them so they would nod their heads and agree to his speech. (1) And some scholars have collected the forms of tricks and fallacies in dialectics into three types: 'First: Shifting the questioner from his question; this occurs when he cites against the respondent something that obligates him necessarily; when the respondent wants to shift it, he denies some of what was cited against him even if it is clear; when he clarifies, the two discourses become confused, and thereby he shifts from the first issue to another; so the questioner must scrutinize such situations. Second: Dividing the question; this is that the respondent looks at the circumstances; if it can bear several aspects, he divides it into its aspects to prolong the questioning of the questioner by occupying his heart from the strength of disputation, thus nullifying the questioner's purpose in dialectics; so the questioner must not allow him to divide, lest his purpose become ambiguous to him. Third: Striking examples; this is that he intends to multiply the examples given in the Qur'an to intimidate his opponent, such as citing in response to his claim the verses: 'And indeed the weakest of houses is the house of the spider, if they but knew' (2), and His saying: 'Like ashes which the wind blows fiercely on a stormy day' (3). Then if
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage discusses the prohibition of using tricks and fallacies in jadal (dialectical disputation). It begins by identifying two types of people who resort to such methods: a falsifier who knows the truth is against him and seeks to mislead his opponent, or a truth-holder who lacks the legitimate means or ability to defend the truth. In both cases, using trickery is illegitimate. The author cites Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, who considered it a prohibited act (mahzur) and characteristic of immoral disputants. The passage then lists several forms of tricks from Juwayni's 'al-Kafiya fi al-Jadal'. These include: using obscure language to confuse the opponent and then denying having said it; nitpicking over words when unable to answer; diverting the topic with flamboyant speech; the respondent or questioner shifting the focus of the question or answer; feigning distraction to imply the opponent's argument is weak while secretly seeking a response; and if one's own argument is weak, using ingratiating gestures to gain approval from the audience. Following this, the passage presents another classification by some scholars into three types: first, shifting the question (naql al-sa'il) — the respondent denies a clear citation to confuse; second, dividing the question (taqsim al-su'al) — the respondent splits the question into multiple aspects to overwhelm the questioner; third, striking examples (darb al-amthal) — multiplying Quranic comparisons to intimidate, like citing the spider's house or ashes in a windstorm. The passage thus provides a practical guide for recognizing and avoiding these unethical maneuvers in scholarly debate.

Three Types of Tricks in Jadal: Shifting, Dividing, and Intimidation
Another scholar's classification of tricks into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of tricks—shifting the question, dividing the question, and striking examples—and describe how each undermines the debate.

Core Idea
Tricks in jadal fall into three patterns: shifting the issue, splitting it endlessly, or using Quranic verses to intimidate.

In addition to the specific tricks listed from Juwayni, some scholars have classified all deceptive maneuvers into three types: shifting the question (naql al-sā'il), dividing the question (taqsīm al-su'āl), and striking examples (ḍarb al-amthāl). Each type has a distinct mechanism and a recommended counter-strategy for the honest debater.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first trick: shifting the question
The respondent denies a clear citation given by the questioner, even if obvious, thereby confusing the issue and moving to a different topic.
2
Identify the second trick: dividing the question
The respondent splits the question into many aspects to prolong the debate and overwhelm the questioner's concentration.
3
Identify the third trick: striking examples
The respondent multiplies Quranic parables (like the spider's house or ashes in the wind) not as evidence but to intimidate and silence the opponent.
4
Learn the proper response
For each trick, the passage gives the correct counter: against shifting, the questioner must scrutinize; against dividing, the questioner must not allow division; against striking examples, no explicit counter given but it implies rejecting the intimidation.

إضافة إلى ذلك - أن يخرجه عن الموضوع بإيراد العبارات الطنانة وبترديد الفصاحات البليغة فيما لا يتعلق بفائدة المسألة ومنها : أن يحتال المسؤول على السائل فيخرجه عن سؤاله، أو يحتال السائل على المسؤول فيخرجه عن جوابه إلى غيره فإذا لم ينتبه كل واحد منهما لذلك عن صاحبه، لم تظهر صحة السؤال ولا صحة الجواب ولا الفساد، فيحصل الكلام بلا فائدة ومنها أن الواحد منهم إذا اختبر بقوة كلام خصمه وعلم من نفسه أنه لا يمكنه الرد عليه أو التخلص من مقتضاه ؛ أوهم الحاضرين ضعف كلام خصمه بأن يترك الإقبال عليه ويلتفت يمينا وشمالا، كالمتغافل المشتغل في فكره وخاطره بشيء آخر، أو يأخذ مع غيره في حديث، محاولا أن يوهم أن خصمه لا يجد عليه إلا ما هو ضعيف ركيك لا يساوي الاستماع إليه، وهو في التفاتاته وإبداء تغافله يتفكر ويتدبر ويرقي في طلب الجواب عنه، فإن حضره الجواب أجاب بما يحضره، وإن تعذر عليه الجواب تهاون بذلك، وقال : قد أكثرنا وفرغنا عما فيه التحصيل من السؤال ومنها : أنه إذا علم ضعف حجته، يحتال في ترويج ما عنده بالالتفات والإقبال على كل أحد من أهل المجلس، مع التبسم، مستشهدا بهم، مستميلا في إقباله عليهم لحركوا له رؤوسهم ويوافقوا على كلامه»(1) وقد جمع بعض العلماء صور الحيل والمغالطات في الجدل في أنواع ثلاثة، هي: «أحدها : نقل السائل عن سؤال ؛ وإنما يكون عند استشهاده على المجيب بما يلزمه بقطعه، فإذا أراد المجيب نقله جحد بعض ما استشهد به عليه وإن كان واضحا، فإذا بينه اختلط الكلامان وبه ينقله من المسألة الأولى إلى غيرها، فيجب على السائل إمعان النظر في مثل هذا والثاني : تقسيم السؤال ؛ وهو أن ينظر المجيب إلى أحواله، فإن كان محتملا لوجوه شتی قسمه على وجوهه ليطيل مناظرة السائل بشغل قلبه عن قوة المناظرة، فيبطل غرض السائل في الجدل، فالواجب على السائل أن لا يمكنه من التقسيم لئلا يلتبس غرضه عليه الثالث : ضرب الأمثال ؛ وهو أن يقصد تكثير الأمثال المضروبة في القرآن ليجبن خصمه، مثل أن يورد في جواب دعواه قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّ أَوْهَنَ الْبُيُوتِ لَبَيْتُ الْعَنكَبوتِ لَو كَانُوا يَعْلَمُونَ ﴾ (2) ، وقوله : ﴿كَرَمَادٍ اشْتَدَّتْ بِهِ الرِّيحُ فِي يَوْمٍ عَاصِفٍ (3) ، فإذا
6 - Avoiding the use of tricks and fallacies in dialectics: The reasons driving the waste of dialectics from its goal for which it was established are the use of tricks in it; and the one who does this is one of two men: either a falsifier who knows that the truth is on the side of his opponent and therefore works to mislead him and attempt to make him err; or a truth-holder but who does not know the legitimate means to be used in defending the truth he has, lacking proofs or lacking ability in dialectics. In both cases, using trickery in dialectics is an illegitimate act; indeed Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni considered it a prohibited act that must be avoided, and it is the habit of immoral people in disputation, and the practice of one whose aim in disputation is wrangling with the foolish, shunning the way of religious people and sincere advisors, far from the path of goodness and enjoining good and forbidding evil. (1) This, and the forms of tricks that may be used in dialectics are many and uncountable; Imam al-Haramayn listed a number of them; we select some: Among them: that one of the two disputants uses trickery on the other by using obscure expressions such that his opponent understands little of his speech, due to the abundance of ambiguity, generality, and strange language; when the opponent wants to respond or report something from his speech to comment on it, he objects by saying: 'I did not say this, and I did not mean what you are reporting,' or he says: 'You did not even understand what I said.' Among them: that when he senses his inability to answer the opponent's objections, he clings to the opponent's expressions and words, harasses him over every letter, and argues over every word. He also tries to divert him from the subject by uttering flamboyant phrases and repeating eloquent speech on matters unrelated to the benefit of the issue. Among them: that the respondent uses trickery on the questioner to divert him from his question, or the questioner uses trickery on the respondent to divert him from his answer to something else; if each of them is not aware of that from his companion, then neither the correctness of the question nor the correctness of the answer nor the corruption is manifest, so speech occurs without benefit. Among them: that when one of them tests the strength of his opponent's speech and knows from himself that he cannot refute it or escape its implication, he makes the attendees imagine the weakness of his opponent's speech by turning away from it and looking right and left, like one feigning heedlessness occupied in his thought and mind with something else, or he starts a conversation with someone else, trying to pretend that his opponent only offers what is weak and flimsy, not worth listening to; while in his turnings and feigning heedlessness he reflects and ponders and seeks a response to it; if an answer occurs to him, he responds with what is present; if he is unable to answer, he belittles it and says: 'We have done enough and finished with what is productive of the question.' Among them: that when he knows the weakness of his evidence, he uses trickery to promote what he has by turning and facing everyone in the gathering, with a smile, seeking their testimony, inclining in his approach to them so they would nod their heads and agree to his speech. (1) And some scholars have collected the forms of tricks and fallacies in dialectics into three types: 'First: Shifting the questioner from his question; this occurs when he cites against the respondent something that obligates him necessarily; when the respondent wants to shift it, he denies some of what was cited against him even if it is clear; when he clarifies, the two discourses become confused, and thereby he shifts from the first issue to another; so the questioner must scrutinize such situations. Second: Dividing the question; this is that the respondent looks at the circumstances; if it can bear several aspects, he divides it into its aspects to prolong the questioning of the questioner by occupying his heart from the strength of disputation, thus nullifying the questioner's purpose in dialectics; so the questioner must not allow him to divide, lest his purpose become ambiguous to him. Third: Striking examples; this is that he intends to multiply the examples given in the Qur'an to intimidate his opponent, such as citing in response to his claim the verses: 'And indeed the weakest of houses is the house of the spider, if they but knew' (2), and His saying: 'Like ashes which the wind blows fiercely on a stormy day' (3). Six Types of Dialectical Objections to Quranic Arguments
Author enumerates six types of jadal objections arising from Quranic reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify any dialectical objection to a Quranic argument into one of the six types described in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Six distinct categories of dialectical objections, not mere disagreement.

When a jurist uses a Quranic verse to prove a legal opinion, the opponent may object in various ways. The author of this passage, after surveying the dialectical practice, reduces all such objections to six well-defined types. Mastering these types equips you to systematically analyze and respond to challenges in Quranic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of the objection
First, determine whether the objector denies that the verse indicates the claimed meaning at all (type 1) or concedes the usuli significance but contests its practical implication (type 2).
2
Check for alternative extraction or readings
If the objector accepts the verse as evidence, see if he extracts a different meaning from the same verse (type 3) or uses a different qira'ah (variant reading) to support a different meaning (type 4).
3
Consider abrogation and interpretation
Examine whether the objection is based on the verse being abrogated (mansukh) or containing an abrogated ruling (type 5), or whether the objector offers a different ta'wil (interpretation) that yields a conflicting meaning (type 6).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا يثور الجدل بين المستدل بهذا الدليل ومن يخالفه في دلالته على الرأي المستدل به لإثباته . وقد تبين من استقراء أنواع الجدل التي تدور حول الاستدلال بالقرآن الكريم، أنها ترجع إلى ستة أنواع من الاعتراضات : - فهي إما اعتراضات من جهة أن المستدل بالآية القرآنية على معنى ما، لا يقول بدلالتها أصوليا على ذلك المعنى . أو اعتراضات بالتسليم بموجب الآية أصوليًا لكن مع المنازعة في مقتضاها الذي تدل عليه عمليا . — أو اعتراضات من جهة أن الدليل الذي أخذ به المستدل يأخذ به المعترض كذلك ولكن يستخرج منه معنى آخر غير المعنى الذي استخرجه المستدل. - أو اعتراضات من جهة اختلاف القراءات حيث يأخذ المستدل بالآية على قراءة ما، ويأخذ المعترض بالآية نفسها لكن على قراءة ثانية قد تفيد معنى غير معنى الأولى. - أو اعتراضات من جهة النسخ، حيث يستدل المدعي بآية ما، فيعترضه المخالف بأنها منسوخة أو تتضمن معنى منسوخا . - أو اعتراضات من جهة التأويل، حيث يستدل المدعي بآية لمعنى ما، فيؤول المعترض الآية إلى معنى آخر مباين. ونتناول فيما يلي كل واحد من هذه الأنواع بالتحليل والتفصيل والتمثيل، كلا في مبحث مستقل .
Here the dialectic arises between the one who argues with this evidence and whoever opposes him regarding its indication of the opinion he seeks to prove. It has become clear from surveying the types of dialectic that revolve around reasoning by the Noble Quran that they reduce to six types of objections: - Either objections from the aspect that the one who argues with a Quranic verse for a certain meaning does not, in usul, claim its indication of that meaning; or objections by conceding the import of the verse in usul but contesting its implication that it practically indicates; — or objections from the aspect that the evidence taken by the arguer is also taken by the objector, but he extracts from it another meaning different from the one extracted by the arguer; - or objections from the aspect of differences in readings, where the arguer takes the verse according to one reading, and the objector takes the same verse but according to a second reading that may yield a meaning different from the first; - or objections from the aspect of abrogation, where the claimant argues with a verse, and the opponent objects that it is abrogated or contains an abrogated meaning; - or objections from the aspect of interpretation, where the claimant argues with a verse for a meaning, and the objector interprets the verse to a different meaning. We will treat each of these types below with analysis, detail, and exemplification, each in an independent section.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a classification of dialectical objections that arise when using Quranic verses as evidence in a dispute. The author begins by noting that jadal occurs because the evidence from the Quran is often not explicit, leading to disagreement about its implication for the opinion being argued. Then, based on a survey of the types of jadal, he reduces them to six categories of objections. The six types are: (1) The objector denies that the verse, in usul al-fiqh, actually indicates the claimed meaning at all. (2) The objector concedes the usuli significance of the verse but disputes the practical implication that the arguer derives from it. (3) The objector accepts the same verse as evidence but extracts a different meaning from it than the arguer did. (4) The objection is based on different qira'at (variant readings) of the verse, where each reading supports a different meaning. (5) The objection is that the verse is abrogated (mansukh) or contains an abrogated ruling. (6) The objection is through ta'wil (interpretation), where the objector interprets the verse to an incompatible meaning. The author promises to treat each type separately in subsequent sections.

الاستدلال بالقرآن
الاعتراض
الأصولي

Key Takeaway
Dialectical objections to Quranic arguments are systematically reducible to six types based on the nature of the challenge to the evidence.

Six Types of Objections to Qur'anic Reasoning
Author enumerates six types of objections to Qur'anic reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the six types of objections to Qur'anic reasoning in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Six systematic types of objections structure every debate over Qur'anic evidence.

In jadal over Qur'anic evidence, disagreements often arise not from the text itself but from its indication. The author classifies these disputes into six distinct types of objections. Understanding this taxonomy equips you to recognize and respond to arguments in usul al-fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the dispute
Understand that reliance on non-explicit wording leads to objections. The author notes that reasoning with the Qur'an is often not with an explicit text, so acceptance is not guaranteed.
2
Identify the six types
Read the passage and list each type: objections regarding uṣūlī indication, practical implication, alternative meaning from the same evidence, variant recitations, abrogation, and interpretation.
3
Distinguish the basis of each objection
For each type, note whether the objection challenges the uṣūlī validity, the practical implication, the meaning derived, the recitation, the legal status (abrogated), or the interpretation.
4
Apply the taxonomy
Given a hypothetical objection, classify which type it is. For example, if an opponent says 'the verse is abrogated', that is type 5.

الاستدلال لرأيه في مسألة معينة - إلى القرآن الكريم، فيحاول أن يجد فيه دليلا يسند رأيه أو يحتمله ولو من بعيد. لكن الاستدلال بالقرآن الكريم لا يكون دائما استدلالا بنص صريح في معناه، بل في أكثر الأحيان يكون استدلالا بلفظ غير صريح، وهو ما يعني أن التسليم بهذا الاستدلال قد لا يحصل دائمًا من قبل المخالف في الرأي، مما يعني أن خلافًا جديدًا سيدور حول الدليل ذاته من حيث دلالته على المعنى المطلوب أو عدم دلالته . وهنا يثور الجدل بين المستدل بهذا الدليل ومن يخالفه في دلالته على الرأي المستدل به لإثباته . وقد تبين من استقراء أنواع الجدل التي تدور حول الاستدلال بالقرآن الكريم، أنها ترجع إلى ستة أنواع من الاعتراضات : - فهي إما اعتراضات من جهة أن المستدل بالآية القرآنية على معنى ما، لا يقول بدلالتها أصوليا على ذلك المعنى . أو اعتراضات بالتسليم بموجب الآية أصوليًا لكن مع المنازعة في مقتضاها الذي تدل عليه عمليا . — أو اعتراضات من جهة أن الدليل الذي أخذ به المستدل يأخذ به المعترض كذلك ولكن يستخرج منه معنى آخر غير المعنى الذي استخرجه المستدل. - أو اعتراضات من جهة اختلاف القراءات حيث يأخذ المستدل بالآية على قراءة ما، ويأخذ المعترض بالآية نفسها لكن على قراءة ثانية قد تفيد معنى غير معنى الأولى. - أو اعتراضات من جهة النسخ، حيث يستدل المدعي بآية ما، فيعترضه المخالف بأنها منسوخة أو تتضمن معنى منسوخا . - أو اعتراضات من جهة التأويل، حيث يستدل المدعي بآية لمعنى ما، فيؤول المعترض الآية إلى معنى آخر مباين. ونتناول فيما يلي كل واحد من هذه الأنواع بالتحليل والتفصيل والتمثيل، كلا في مبحث مستقل .
The argument for one's opinion in a particular issue - to the Noble Qur'an, so he tries to find in it evidence that supports his opinion or accommodates it, even from afar. But reasoning with the Noble Qur'an is not always reasoning with an explicit text in its meaning, but rather most often it is reasoning with a non-explicit wording, which means that acceptance of this reasoning may not always be obtained from the opponent, meaning that a new dispute will arise about the evidence itself regarding its indication of the intended meaning or lack thereof. And here dialectic arises between the one who uses this evidence and the one who disagrees with its indication of the opinion that the former seeks to prove. It has become clear from surveying the types of dialectic that revolve around reasoning with the Noble Qur'an that they reduce to six types of objections: - They are either objections regarding the fact that the one who argues with a Qur'anic verse for a certain meaning does not accept its indication according to uṣūl for that meaning. Or objections that concede the import of the verse according to uṣūl but dispute its implication that it indicates practically. — Or objections regarding the fact that the evidence taken by the arguer is also taken by the objector, but he extracts from it a meaning other than the meaning extracted by the arguer. - Or objections regarding differences in recitations, where the arguer takes the verse according to one recitation, and the objector takes the same verse but according to a second recitation that may convey a meaning different from that of the first. - Or objections regarding abrogation, where the claimant argues with a verse, and the opponent objects that it is abrogated or includes an abrogated meaning. - Or objections regarding interpretation, where the claimant argues with a verse for a meaning, and the objector interprets the verse to another divergent meaning. We will address each of these types with analysis, detail, and examples, each in a separate section.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of objections (i'tiradat) that arise in jadal (dialectic) when disputants use the Qur'an as evidence. The author begins by noting that reasoning with the Qur'an often involves non-explicit wording, leading to disagreements about the evidence's indication. Through survey, six types of objections are identified: (1) objection that the arguer does not accept the uṣūlī indication of the verse for that meaning; (2) objection that concedes the uṣūlī import but disputes its practical implication; (3) objection that the same evidence yields a different meaning when used by the objector; (4) objection based on variant qira'at (recitations) giving different meanings; (5) objection that the verse is abrogated (naskh); (6) objection that the verse is interpreted (ta'wīl) to a different meaning. The author promises to treat each type separately with analysis and examples. This classification is essential for usul al-fiqh and jadal, as it systematizes the possible lines of attack when arguing from revelation.

Key Terms
الاعتراض (al-iʿtirāḍ): An objection raised by the opponent in jadal against the evidence or reasoning of the claimant. [Note: In later works, i'tirad becomes a technical term in jadal for specific objection types.]
الجدل (al-jadal): Dialectic or disputation aiming to convince an opponent using accepted premises, often in religious matters. [Note: In logic (mantiq), jadal is one of the five arts; here it refers to usuli dialectic.]
الدليل (al-dalīl): Evidence, specifically a Qur'anic verse or its indication, used to support a claim. [Note: In logic, dalil often means proof; here it is specifically textual evidence from revelation.]

Key Takeaway
The six types of objections to Qur'anic evidence provide a structured framework for analyzing and responding to debates in usul al-fiqh.

Objection from Inconsistency: Arguer Does Not Accept His Own Evidence
Author explains the first objection type with a Hanafi-Shafi'i example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the inconsistency in an opponent's use of evidence and construct a valid objection or response in a jadal exchange on usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
An objection based on inconsistency is only strong if the arguer cannot defend his use of the principle.

In dialectical disputation (jadal), an objector can challenge the arguer by pointing out that the very evidence or principle he uses is one he does not personally accept. This forces the arguer to either abandon his argument or show that his school actually accepts it. This passage illustrates this with a Hanafi-Shafi'i exchange over dalīl al-khiṭāb (the concept of opposition).

Guided Path
1
Identify the arguer's claim and evidence
Find the specific ruling the Hanafi is trying to prove and the Qur'anic verse he uses as evidence.
The claim: dropping mut'a (gift) for a divorced woman who has been entered. Evidence: Q. 2:236, which conditions mut'a on not having touched.
2
Locate the underlying usuli principle
Determine the methodological principle the arguer relies on. Here, the Hanafi uses the conditional clause to infer a ruling by dalīl al-khiṭāb (concept of opposition).
Dalīl al-khiṭāb means that a condition implies the opposite ruling when the condition is absent.
3
Formulate the objector's challenge
The Shafi'i objects that the Hanafi is using a principle his own school rejects. Show the inconsistency between the principle used and the arguer's accepted usul.
The objection is not about the verse itself but about the validity of the method used to derive the ruling.
4
Analyze the arguer's response
The Hanafi responds by restricting the scope of his school's rejection: Hanafis accept dalīl al-khiṭāb when it is in conditional form. Evaluate whether this response successfully neutralizes the objection.
The response shows that the objector's challenge was not decisive; the arguer can clarify his school's position.

المبحث الأول الاعتراض من جهة أن المستدل لا يقول بنفس الدليل أي أن يستدل المستدل بدليل من القرآن الكريم على حكم مسألة معينة، فيعترض عليه السائل من جهة أن المستدل لا يقول بهذا الدليل، إما من جهة القاعدة الأصولية التي استند إليها، أو من جهة الدليل ذاته بحيث لا يرتضيه المستدل نفسه في الموضع الذي استدل به عليه . المطلب الأول : أن يستدل بطريق من الأصول لا يقول به فقد يستدل المدعي بآية من القرآن ويستند في استدلاله بهذه الآية إلى قاعدة أصولية يخالفها أو ينكرها في أصوله فيواجهه المعترض بأنه لا يقول بهذه القاعدة، وبالتالي لا يستقيم له الاستدلال بالدليل الذي بناه عليها . مثال ذلك : أن يستدل الحنفي بدليل الخطاب (مفهوم المخالفة) (1) أنه لا يقول به أصوليا (2) ، وذلك كاستدلاله في إسقاط المتعة للمدخول بها، بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْكُمْ إِن طَلَّقْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ مَا لَمْ تَمَسُّوهُنَّ أَوْ تَفْرِضُوا لَهُنَّ فَرِيضَةً وَمَتَعُوهُنَّ ... (3) ، فشرط في إيجاب المتعة ألا يكون قد مسها . فيعترض عليه الشافعي بأن هذا استدلال بدليل الخطاب، والحنفية لا يقولون به ولا يعتبرونه طريقا صالحة في استنباط الأحكام. ويمكن للحنفي أن يجيب عن هذا الاعتراض بأنه يقول بهذا الطريق في الاستنباط، لأنه جاء بصيغة الشرط ... إن طَلَّقْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ ...)، و(إن) من حروف الشرط، والحنفية يقولون بدليل الخطاب إذا كان بلفظ الشرط ومثاله كذلك : أن يستدل الحنفي في جواز بیع در همين بدرهم ومد تمر، بقوله (4) (1) سيأتي الحديث عن الاعتراضات المتعلقة بهذا الدليل بصورة مستقلة في الفصل الرابع من الباب . (2) الواضح في أصول الفقه لابن عقيل الحنبلي، ج: 2، ص: 127 (3) سورة البقرة من الآية : 236 · (4) المعونة في الجدل ،للشيرازي، ص: 145 - 146 . هذا
First section: Objection regarding the fact that the arguer does not accept the evidence itself, i.e., the arguer uses evidence from the Noble Qur'an for a ruling on a particular issue, and the questioner objects to him on the basis that the arguer does not accept this evidence, either due to the uṣūlī principle he relied upon, or due to the evidence itself such that the arguer himself does not approve of it in the context where he used it. First subsection: That he argues by a method of uṣūl that he does not accept. For the claimant may argue with a verse from the Qur'an and base his argument on an uṣūlī principle that he contradicts or denies in his own uṣūl, so the objector confronts him with the fact that he does not accept this principle, and consequently his argument based on that evidence is not sound. An example of that: that a Hanafi argues using dālīl al-khiṭāb (muffāhum al-mukhālafa) (1) even though he does not accept it according to uṣūl (2), such as his argument for dropping the mut'a for the woman who has been entered, by the saying of the Exalted: {And there is no blame upon you if you divorce women before having touched them or specified for them an obligation, and provide for them ...} (3), so he made touching a condition for the obligation of mut'a. So the Shafi'i objects to him that this is reasoning by dālīl al-khiṭāb, and the Hanafis do not accept it nor consider it a valid method for deriving rulings. And the Hanafi can answer this objection by saying that he does accept this method in derivation, because it came in the form of a condition: {if you divorce women}, and 'in' is a conditional particle, and the Hanafis accept dālīl al-khiṭāb if it is in conditional wording. And an example of that as well: that the Hanafi argues for the permissibility of selling two dirhams for a dirham and a mudd of dates, by his saying (4) (1) Discussion of objections related to this evidence will come independently in the fourth chapter of the section. (2) Al-Wāḍiḥ fī Uṣūl al-Fiqh by Ibn ʿAqīl al-Ḥanbalī, vol. 2, p. 127. (3) Sūrat al-Baqara, verse 236. (4) Al-Maʿūna fī al-Jadal by al-Shīrāzī, pp. 145-146. End.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage details the first type of objection from the previous classification: the objector argues that the claimant does not accept the evidence he himself uses. This can be either because the claimant relies on an usuli principle he normally rejects, or because he does not hold the evidence as valid in that context. The author then gives a concrete example from Islamic jurisprudence: a Hanafi argues for dropping the mut'a (gift) for a divorced woman who has been entered, using the verse 'if you divorce women before having touched them...' The Shafi'i objects by saying this is an argument from 'dalil al-khitab' (the indicator of the address, i.e., concept of opposition), which the Hanafis generally do not accept as a valid source of law. The author then shows how the Hanafi can defend himself by restricting his school's rejection: the Hanafis actually accept dalil al-khitab when it is in conditional form. The example illustrates that the objection is not automatically fatal; the claimant may respond by showing that the principle does apply in this case. This is a standard jadal exchange: claim, objection, and counter-response. The passage ends with a second example (unfinished) about selling two dirhams for a dirham and a mudd of dates, referencing another source. This lesson teaches students to identify inconsistency in an opponent's use of evidence and to craft appropriate responses.

القاعدة الأصولية
المستدل
المعترض

An objection based on the arguer's inconsistency can be countered by showing that the principle is accepted in the specific form used, as the Hanafi did with conditional dalīl al-khiṭāb.
