Responding to the Objection of Ambiguity (Ijmal)
A Maliki objects to a Hanafi's proof by claiming the verse is mujmal, and the Hanafi responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an opponent's claim of textual ambiguity (ijmal) can be refuted by demonstrating that the term is actually clear (mufassar) based on its known genus.

Core Idea
A term known in its genus is clear, not ambiguous.

In usul al-fiqh debates, an opponent may challenge your proof text by claiming it is ambiguous (mujmal) and therefore cannot serve as evidence. However, if the term in question has a well-known meaning in the language, you can counter by showing it is actually clear (mufassar). This lesson walks through a concrete example from a Hanafi-Maliki exchange over the Quranic verse 'And Allah has permitted trade.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The Maliki objects that the verse 'And Allah has permitted trade' is mujmal (ambiguous) and thus cannot be used as proof. Note the basis of the objection: ambiguity prevents deduction of a ruling.
In usul al-fiqh, a mujmal text requires external clarification; it cannot stand alone as evidence.
2
Spot the First Response Strategy
The Hanafi first defends by stating that there is no explicit text from Abu Hanifa on this point, and the principle (asl) is to treat the verse as general ('amm) rather than ambiguous. This is a procedural response.
This response appeals to the school's methodological principle, not to the nature of the term itself.
3
Analyze the Second Response (Definitional)
The Hanafi then defines mujmal as 'that whose intended meaning is not understood from its wording and requires clarification.' He argues that 'trade' (al-bay') is mufassar because its meaning is known in its genus — i.e., linguistically clear.
Key point: If a term's genus is known, it is not ambiguous. This is a logical refutation based on definition.
4
Evaluate the Dialectical Outcome
By showing that 'trade' is a clear term, the Hanafi invalidates the Maliki's objection. The objection fails because it incorrectly labels a clear term as ambiguous.
The debate demonstrates that not every claim of ambiguity is valid; the objector must prove that the term truly requires external clarification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى: ﴿ وَأَحَلَّ اللَّهُ الْبَيْعَ ... ﴾ ، فيقول له المالكي: هذه الآية لا يصح احتجاجك بها لأنها عندي مجملة والمجمل لا يصح الاحتجاج به . ويمكن للحنفي أن يتخلص من هذا الاعتراض بأن يجيب بأن هذا ليس عندهم عن أبي حنيفة فيه نص، وهو أصل من الأصول، ويعتبر أن الآية عامة، ولا يسلم بأنها مجملة، كما يمكنه أن يجيب أيضًا بأن المجمل هو ما لا يفهم المراد به من لفظه ويفتقر في بيانه إلى غيره والبيع من المفسر الذي يعلم المراد منه، لأن البيع معلوم الجنس، فيكون الاعتراض على ذلك غير صحيح
Allah Most High said: 'And Allah has permitted trade...' Then the Maliki says to him: This verse is not valid as your proof because according to me it is ambiguous (mujmal), and the ambiguous cannot be used as proof. The Hanafi can extricate himself from this objection by answering that according to them (the Hanafis) there is no explicit text from Abu Hanifa on this matter, and it is a fundamental principle (asl), and he considers the verse to be general ('amm) and does not accept that it is ambiguous. He can also answer that the ambiguous is that whose intended meaning is not understood from its wording and requires clarification from elsewhere, whereas 'trade' (al-bay') is from the clear (mufassar) whose intended meaning is known, because 'trade' is known in its genus. So the objection to that is not correct.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange typical of usul al-fiqh debates. A Hanafi uses the Quranic verse 'And Allah has permitted trade' as evidence for a ruling. The Maliki objects by claiming that the verse is mujmal (ambiguous), and an ambiguous text cannot serve as a proof. The Hanafi then gives two possible responses. First, he may assert that there is no explicit text from Abu Hanifa on this point, and that the principle (asl) is to consider the verse as general ('amm) rather than ambiguous. Second, he may argue that the term 'trade' is mufassar (clear), because its meaning is known in the language and does not require clarification. Thus the objection is invalid. This illustrates a common dialectical move: countering an objection of ambiguity by showing that the term in question is actually clear and well-defined in the Islamic legal tradition.

Key Terms
المجمل
العام
المفسر

C) Because Abu Hanifa explicitly said the verse is general.
D) Because the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
A claim of textual ambiguity can be refuted by showing that the term in question is linguistically clear and its meaning is known in its genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Objection: Arguer Does Not Act by Evidence in Its Original Context
Objection that the arguer does not follow the evidence in its own context but uses it elsewhere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 'inconsistency' objection in jadal and evaluate whether it defeats the opponent's argument.

Core Idea
Consistency demands that the evidence you use in one context must be accepted in its own context first.

The most powerful objections in jadal often expose contradictions. In this lesson, we examine an objection against a Hanafi argument for accepting testimony of dhimmis. The objector points out that the Hanafi himself does not accept the Quranic verse in its original context, so using it elsewhere is inconsistent. Understanding this objection sharpens your skill in spotting dialectical weaknesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arguer's claim and the evidence used
Find what the Hanafi claims (dhimmis' testimony among themselves) and the verse he uses (Al-Ma'idah 5:106).
The verse is about bequest during travel.
2
Determine the original context of the evidence
The verse was revealed about testimony against Muslims when traveling. The objector says the verse's original meaning is about testimony against Muslims.
3
Check the arguer's acceptance of the evidence in that original context
Does the Hanafi accept testimony of dhimmis against Muslims? No, he rejects it. So he does not act by the verse in its own context.
4
Assess the objection's force
The objector argues that because the Hanafi does not accept the verse's implication in its original context, his use of it for a different context is inconsistent. The passage says this objection is clear and leaves no room for answer.
5
Evaluate the forced response
Some Hanafis attempted an answer using mantuq and mafhum, but the commentator refutes it by noting that if the original analogy (asl) is invalidated, the branch (far') is too. Understand why this response fails.

المطلب الثاني: أن لا يقول به في الموضع الذي يتناوله أي أن المستدل لا يأخذ بمقتضى الدليل في المعنى الأصلي الذي سيق الدليل لبيانه، لكنه يستعمل هذا الدليل لإفادة معنى آخر، فيعترض عليه بأنه لم يقل بالدليل في موضعه فكيف يقول به في موضع آخر؟! ومثاله : أن يستدل الحنفي في قبول شهادة أهل الذمة بينهم بقوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا شَهَادَةُ بَيْنِكُمْ إِذَا حَضَرَ أَحَدَكُمُ الْمَوْتُ حِينَ الْوَصِيَّةِ اثْنَانِ ذَوَا عَدْلٍ مِنْكُمْ أَوْ آخَرَانِ مِنْ غَيْرِكُمْ ...﴾ ، فدلت الآية على جواز شهادة أهل الذمة ويُعترض على المستدل هنا بأنه لا يقول بقبول شهادة أهل الذمة على المسلمين، وهذه الآية إنما نزلت في هذا المعنى، وبالتالي فهو لا يقول بمقتضاها في موضعها، فكيف يقول به في غير موضعها؟! وعليه لا يصح احتجاجه بها وهذا الاعتراض واضح وصريح ولا يدع مجالا للجواب، ومع ذلك فقد تكلف بعض أتباع أبي حنيفة بالجواب فقالوا بأن هذه الآية دلت على جواز قبول شهادة أهل الذمة على المسلمين بمنطوقها، ودلت على قبول شهادة أهل الذمة بمفهومها، فلما قبلت شهادتهم على المسلمين دل ذلك على أن شهادتهم على الكفار أولى بالقبول، ثم ثبت بالدليل بطلان شهادتهم على المسلمين، فيبقى قبول شهادتهم على أهل الذمة على ما كان عليه ما دام لم يرد دليل بإبطاله، والتكلف في هذا الجواب واضح بل فيه ما يرده لأنه ما دام أصل القياس وهو قبول شهادتهم على المسلمين قد بطل، فإن الفرع المقيس عليه وهو شهادتهم على أهل الذمة يبطل كذلك بالأولى والأحرى
The second demand: that he does not act by it in the context it addresses. Meaning that the arguer does not take the implication of the evidence in the original meaning for which the evidence was brought, but he uses this evidence to convey another meaning. So he is objected to: he did not accept the evidence in its own context, so how can he accept it in another context? Its example: The Hanafi argues for accepting the testimony of dhimmis among themselves by His statement, the Most High: 'O you who have believed, testimony between you, when death approaches one of you, at the time of bequest, [shall be] two persons of justice among you or two others from among you if you are traveling...' The verse indicates the permissibility of testimony of dhimmis. The arguer is objected to here: he does not accept the testimony of dhimmis against Muslims, and this verse was revealed concerning this meaning (testimony against Muslims). Consequently, he does not act by its implication in its context, so how does he act by it outside its context? Therefore, his proof with it is not valid. This objection is clear and explicit and leaves no room for an answer. Nevertheless, some followers of Abu Hanifa forced an answer, saying that this verse indicated the permissibility of accepting testimony of dhimmis against Muslims by its explicit wording (mantuq), and it indicated accepting testimony of dhimmis (against non-Muslims) by its implication (mafhum). So when their testimony against Muslims was accepted, that indicates that their testimony against disbelievers is more worthy of acceptance. Then it was established by evidence the invalidity of their testimony against Muslims, so the acceptance of their testimony against dhimmis remains as it was as long as no evidence has come to invalidate it. But the forced nature of this answer is clear; rather, there is in it what refutes it: as long as the original of the analogy (asl al-qiyas) — i.e., acceptance of their testimony against Muslims — has become invalid, then the branch (far') measured upon it — i.e., their testimony against dhimmis — is also invalidated by priority and greater reason.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage outlines a second type of objection in usul al-jadal: that the arguer does not apply the evidence in its original context, yet uses it in a different context. The objection is that such an approach is inconsistent. The example concerns the Hanafi argument for accepting testimony of dhimmis (non-Muslim subjects) among themselves based on Surah Al-Ma'idah verse 106, which speaks about testimony during bequest when travel is involved. The verse allows two witnesses, and the Hanafi interprets 'or two others from among you' as referring to non-Muslims (dhimmis). The objection is that the verse was revealed in the context of testimony against Muslims, but the Hanafis do not accept testimony of dhimmis against Muslims; thus they do not act by the verse in its original context. Therefore, their use of it to prove testimony among dhimmis is invalid. The text notes that this objection is clear and leaves no room for answer. However, some Hanafi followers attempted a forced answer: they argued that the verse indicates testimony against Muslims by explicit wording, and by implication (mafhum) it indicates testimony against non-Muslims. But then they admit that the testimony against Muslims has been nullified by other evidence, so they argue that the testimony against dhimmis remains valid. The commentator refutes this: once the original (asl) of the analogy (testimony against Muslims) is invalidated, the branch (far') (testimony against dhimmis) is invalidated with greater reason. This illustrates the principle that if the basis of an analogy is rebutted, the analogy collapses.

Key Terms
المستدل
الدليل
المنطوق
المفهوم

Checkpoint
Why is the objection in the second example considered clear and leaving no room for answer?
A) Because the Hanafi has no textual evidence.
B) Because the Hanafi accepts testimony of dhimmis against Muslims.
C) Because the verse was revealed concerning testimony against Muslims, yet the Hanafi does not accept that, making his use of the verse contradictory.
D) Because the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
An objection based on inconsistency—using a proof in one context while rejecting it in its own context—is a powerful refutation that can collapse an argument.

Refuting Qiyas with A Fortiori Reasoning
Author critiques an argument using qiyas on testimony invalidity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct an analogical argument in usul al-fiqh and apply a fortiori reasoning to refute it.

Core Idea
If the root of an analogy is invalid, the branch is even more invalid—awlā.

In this passage, the author examines an argument that attempted to preserve the validity of testimony from disbelievers against the People of the Covenant by distinguishing it from testimony against Muslims. The author exposes the artificiality of this argument and applies a powerful tool from usul al-fiqh and jadal: the principle of a fortiori reasoning (al-awlā wa’l-ahrā). We will dissect this exchange to understand how analogical arguments can be refuted by attacking the root case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original analogy
Locate the asl (testimony against Muslims, hukm: invalid) and the far‘ (testimony against dhimmis, claimed to remain valid). The opponent argued that since the root was invalidated by evidence, the branch remains valid unless similarly nullified.
The original argument is not quoted in full but implied in the author's response.
2
Locate the author's refutation
The author states that because the root of the analogy (acceptance against Muslims) has been invalidated, the branch (acceptance against dhimmis) is likewise invalidated, and indeed more strongly (bi’l-awlā wa’l-ahrā).
This is a direct application of a fortiori reasoning.
3
Understand the a fortiori principle
Al-awlā wa’l-ahrā means that if a ruling applies to a stronger case, it applies even more to a weaker one. Here, if testimony against Muslims (the stronger case, since they are more deserving of acceptance) is invalid, then testimony against dhimmis (the weaker case) is even more clearly invalid.
This principle is common in usul al-fiqh for deriving rulings.
4
Assess the force of the refutation
The author calls the opponent's response 'artificial' (takalluf) because it tries to preserve the branch while the root has been destroyed. The analogy's own logic undermines the opponent's position.
This shows the importance of coherence between asl and far‘ in qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكفار أولى بالقبول، ثم ثبت بالدليل بطلان شهادتهم على المسلمين، فيبقى قبول شهادتهم على أهل الذمة على ما كان عليه ما دام لم يرد دليل بإبطاله، والتكلف في هذا الجواب واضح بل فيه ما يرده لأنه ما دام أصل القياس وهو قبول شهادتهم على المسلمين قد بطل، فإن الفرع المقيس عليه وهو شهادتهم على أهل الذمة يبطل كذلك بالأولى والأحرى
The disbelievers are more deserving of acceptance [of testimony], then it was established by evidence that their testimony against Muslims is invalid. So acceptance of their testimony against the People of the Covenant remains as it was as long as no evidence comes to invalidate it. The artificiality in this answer is clear, rather there is in it what refutes it, because as long as the original analogy, which is the acceptance of their testimony against Muslims, has been invalidated, then the branch analogized upon it, which is their testimony against the People of the Covenant, is likewise invalidated, a fortiori.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical critique of an attempted analogical argument (qiyas) regarding the acceptance of testimony from disbelievers. The argument being critiqued had claimed that since the testimony of disbelievers against Muslims is invalid, their testimony against the People of the Covenant (ahl al-dhimma) should remain valid unless proven otherwise. The author identifies a forced artificiality (takalluf) in this response, refuting it by pointing out that if the original principle (asl) of accepting testimony against Muslims has been nullified by evidence, then the branch (far‘) analogized upon it—accepting testimony against the People of the Covenant—must also be nullified, and indeed more strongly (bi’l-awlā wa’l-ahrā). The reasoning follows the standard structure of qiyas: asl, far‘, hukm, and ‘illa. The author uses the principle of a fortiori reasoning (awlā) to argue that invalidation of the root necessarily entails invalidation of the branch.

Key Terms

فرع
بالأولى والأحرى

Key Takeaway
A fortiori reasoning (al-awlā wa’l-ahrā) shows that when the root case of an analogy is nullified, the branch case is even more clearly nullified.

?

Definition and Example of Objection by al-Qawl bi-Mujib al-Aya
Author defines and illustrates objection by conceding the verse's import while disputing its implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze an objection by 'al-qawl bi-mujib al-aya' in a usul al-fiqh debate, distinguishing the concession of import from the dispute over its applicability.

Core Idea
Concede the verse's wording, but deny it settles the dispute.

In dialectical debates over Quranic evidence, a clever objector may accept the opponent's quoted verse at face value yet argue that it fails to resolve the actual point of disagreement. This maneuver, called 'al-qawl bi-mujib al-aya,' redirects the dispute to the implication of the evidence rather than its literal meaning. In this lesson, we will define this objection and walk through a classic example from usul al-fiqh involving the Hanafi and Maliki schools.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of al-qawl bi-mujib
The objector concedes the literal import (madlul) of the opponent's Quranic evidence but maintains that this conceded import does not resolve the original dispute. In other words, the evidence is presented in a way that does not address the true point of contention.
The key is that the objector does not deny the verse itself, but its applicability to the case at hand.
2
Identify the two necessary components
First, there is the concession: the objector agrees that the verse says what the opponent claims. Second, there is the dispute over implication: the objector argues that the verse's import is not sufficient to decide the ruling in question. The dispute remains because the evidence is not directly on point.
3
Analyze the Maliki's initial inference
The Maliki jurist presents Quran 47:4—'Then either freely or by ransom, until the war lays down its burdens'—as a clear text (nass) proving the permissibility of gratuitous release (mann) and ransom (fida'). He claims the verse is unequivocal on this ruling.
The Maliki treats the verse as a definitive proof.
4
Follow the Hanafi objection step by step
The Hanafi objector does not deny that the verse mentions mann and fida'. Instead, he argues that the verse is not a clear text because the phrase 'until the war lays down its burdens' creates an unknown end condition. This ambiguity (ijmal) means the verse could be interpreted in multiple ways, so it cannot serve as a decisive nass for the permissibility claim.
The objection is that the evidence is ambiguous, not that it is false.
5
Recognize the type of objection
This is an objection by claiming ambiguity (ijmal). The Hanafi concedes the literal wording (the verse permits mann and fida' up to a limit) but disputes that it is a clear text (nass) because the limit is unknown, leaving room for other interpretations. Thus the verse does not conclusively support the Maliki's position.
The dispute shifts from the ruling to the quality of the evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
الاعتراض بالقول بموجب الآية مع المنازعة في مقتضاها و معنى القول بالموجب : أن يسلم المعترض مدلول الخصم مع بقاء النزاع في الحكم، وذلك بجعل المدلول الذي سلمه الخصم ليس هو محل النزاع، أي ورود دعوى نصب الدليل في غير محل النزاع. ويتجه الاعتراض هنا إلى جميع أنواع الأدلة القرآنية من جهة طريق الاستدلال بها وبتعلق الأمر بالاعتراض على الاستدلال بالنص والظاهر والعام والمجمل.
المطلب الأول : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالنص
المراد بالنص، اللفظ الذي رفع في بيانه إلى أبعد غاياته، وقيل: «هو كل لفظ لا يحتمل إلا معنى واحدًا» . والمنازعة في الاستدلال بالنص : أن يتم الاعتراض بمنع كونه نصا في موضع الاستدلال، وذلك إما بدعوى الإجمال، وإما بدعوى الاحتمال .
أ - منع الاستدلال بالنص بدعوى الإجمال :
مثاله : أن يستدل المالكي في جواز المن والفداء بقوله عز وجل : ﴿فَإِمَّا مَنًّا وَإِمَّا فِدَاءً حَتَّى تَضَعَ الحَرْبُ أَوْزَارَهَا ...﴾، فهذا نص في جواز المن والفداء. وهنا يأتي الاعتراض من الحنفي، فيقول : إن هذه الآية ليست بنص في موضع الاستدلال، وإنما هي مجملة في دلالتها على معناها، لأن الآية أباحت المن والفداء إلى غاية مجهولة، إذ ورد فيها قوله تعالى: ﴿ ... حَتَّى تَضَعَ الحرب أَوْزَارَهَا ...﴾، فهذه الغاية مجهولة وتحتمل أن تكون قد وجدت، كما تحتمل أن لا تكون قد وجدت فهي بهذا مجملة وعليه لا يجوز دعوى النص فيها.
Second Section: Objection by saying the verse is in accordance [with the opponent's claim] while disputing its implication. The meaning of 'al-qawl bi'l-mūjib': that the objector concedes the import of the opponent's [argument] while the dispute over the ruling remains, and that is by making the conceded import not the subject of dispute, i.e., the claim of presenting evidence occurs outside the subject of dispute. This objection is directed to all types of Quranic evidence from the perspective of the method of inference by them, and it concerns objection to inference by the clear text (nass), apparent (zāhir), general ('āmm), and ambiguous (mujmal).
First Sub-section: Objection to inference by the clear text (nass). By 'nass' is meant the expression whose clarity is raised to its furthest extent, and it is said: 'It is every expression that bears only one meaning.' The dispute in inference by the clear text is that the objection is made by denying that it is a clear text in the place of inference, either by claiming ambiguity (ijmāl) or by claiming possibility (iḥtimāl).
A: Denial of inference by the clear text on the claim of ambiguity. Example: The Mālikī deduces the permissibility of mann (gratuitous release) and fidā' (ransom) by the words of Allah Most High: 'Then either freely or by ransom, until the war lays down its burdens...' So this is a clear text on the permissibility of mann and fidā'. Here comes the objection from the Ḥanafī, who says: This verse is not a clear text in the place of inference; rather, it is ambiguous in its indication of its meaning, because the verse permitted mann and fidā' up to an unknown limit, since His words 'until the war lays down its burdens' appear in it. So this limit is unknown and it is possible that it has already occurred, just as it is possible that it has not occurred. So by this it is ambiguous, and therefore claiming it as a clear text is not permissible.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and illustrative section on a specific type of dialectical objection: 'al-qawl bi-mūjib al-āya' (objection by conceding the verse's import while disputing its implication). The author defines the term as the objector accepting the literal import (madlūl) of the opponent's evidence but arguing that this import does not resolve the actual dispute, because the evidence is presented in a context that does not address the point of contention. The objector then directs this objection to all types of Quranic evidence: clear text (nass), apparent (zāhir), general ('āmm), and ambiguous (mujmal). The first sub-section focuses on objection to inference by a clear text (nass). The author provides two definitions of nass: (1) an expression whose clarification is pushed to its furthest extent, and (2) an expression that carries only one meaning. The objection comes either by claiming ambiguity (ijmāl) or by claiming possibility (iḥtimāl). A detailed example is given: a Mālikī jurist argues for the permissibility of gratuitous release (mann) and ransom (fidā') of captives based on Quran 47:4, which says 'then either freely or by ransom, until the war lays down its burdens.' The Mālikī claims this is a clear text (nass) for permissibility. The Ḥanafī objector counters that the verse is not a nass in this context because it is ambiguous (mujmal) due to the unknown end condition 'until the war lays down its burdens.' Since this limit is unknown, the verse could be interpreted in multiple ways, so it cannot serve as a definitive nass for the permissibility claim. The objector thus wins by showing that the evidence does not conclusively support the opponent's position.

Key Terms
القول بموجب الآية
النص
المجمل

Key Takeaway
The essence of al-qawl bi-mujib al-aya is to concede the literal meaning of a verse while denying it resolves the dispute, often by pointing out ambiguity or irrelevance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Objection against a Clear Text (Nas) and Its Refutation
Author presents an objection against a clear text argument and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a dialectical objection against a clear text (nas) and explain how the refutation distinguishes between clarity in ruling and probability in other contexts.

Core Idea
A text can be clear in its ruling even if its wording admits multiple interpretations in other aspects.

In usul al-fiqh, a debater might object to an argument based on a clear text (nas) by claiming the wording is ambiguous. Abu Hanifa's argument about freeing a slave in dhihar is a classic example. In this lesson, you will learn how to parse such an objection and its refutation, distinguishing between the clarity of the ruling and the possibility of other interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original argument (mudda'i)
Find Abu Hanifa's position and his evidence from the verse 'freeing a slave'. He claims the verse is a clear text (nas) for the permissibility of freeing any slave, believing or disbelieving, because the wording is absolute.
2
Find the objection (mu'tarid)
The objector claims the verse is not a clear text because the word 'raqaba' (slave) is absolute and applies to both believing and disbelieving slaves equally, so it is ambiguous (mujmal) and can be restricted to the believing slave by other evidence.
3
Analyze the basis of the objection
Why does the objector say the verse is not a clear text? He equates clarity with absence of all ambiguity, assuming that if a wording admits multiple interpretations, it cannot be clear.
4
Extract the refutation (jawab)
The refutation redefines nas: a clear text is explicit in one ruling, even if it admits other interpretations in other contexts. It is not a condition that it has only one meaning; that is rare. Thus the verse remains a clear text regarding the obligation of freeing a slave.
5
Determine who wins by jadal rules
The objector's objection is based on a misunderstanding of the definition of nas. Since the refutation clarifies the correct definition, the original argument stands. The objector fails to undermine the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك : استدلال أبي حنيفة في جواز تحرير الرقبة الكافرة في الظهار، بقوله عز وجل : فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ ... معتبرًا أن الآية نص في جواز كل ما يسمى رقبة مؤمنة كانت أو كافرة فمن اشترط وصف الإيمان في الرقبة فقد أدخل زيادة على النص. ويعترض عليه بأن الآية ليست نصا في موضع الاستدلال، لأن اللفظ فيها مطلق واقع على صفات لم يقيد ببعضها، فقوله تعالى : فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ يحتمل رقبة مؤمنة ويحتمل رقبة كافرة، وإذا احتمل الأمرين بدرجة واحدة، جاز أن يقصر على الرقبة المؤمنة. ويمكن أن يرد هذا الاعتراض بأن النص هو ما كان صريحًا في حكم من الأحكام، وإن كان اللفظ محتملا في غيره، وليس من شرطه أن لا يحتمل إلا معنى واحدًا، لأن هذا يعز وجوده.
And from that is Abu Hanifa's argument regarding the permissibility of freeing a disbelieving slave in dhihar, by His saying, the Mighty and Majestic: '...freeing a slave...' considering the verse to be a clear text (nas) for the permissibility of every slave, believing or disbelieving, so whoever stipulates the attribute of faith in the slave has added an extra condition to the text. And one objects against him by saying that the verse is not a clear text in the place of inference, because the wording therein is absolute, applying to descriptions without qualification by some of them. So His saying, the Exalted: '...freeing a slave...' admits a believing slave and admits a disbelieving slave. And if it admits both possibilities equally, it is permissible to restrict it to the believing slave. And this objection can be refuted by saying that the clear text (nas) is that which is explicit in one ruling, even if the wording is probable in other [contexts]; and it is not a condition that it admits only one meaning, because that is rare in existence.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (jadal) from the science of usul al-fiqh, showing how one objects to an argument based on a clear text (nas) and how that objection is answered. The passage first presents Abu Hanifa's argument: he uses the Quranic verse 'free a slave' (from the verse on dhihar) as a clear text (nas) to support freeing any slave, whether believing or disbelieving, because the verse is absolute. The objector then argues that the verse is not a clear text because the word 'raqaba' (slave) is absolute and unqualified, so it applies to both believing and disbelieving slaves equally; thus it is ambiguous (mujmal) and can be restricted to the believing slave by other evidence. The refutation explains that a clear text (nas) is defined as a wording that is explicit in one ruling (here, the obligation to free a slave) even if it admits multiple interpretations in other respects (e.g., whether the slave must be a believer). The condition for a text to be considered nas is not that it has only one possible meaning, because that is extremely rare; rather, it is sufficient that it clearly establishes a ruling. Thus, the objection is invalid because it confuses the clarity of the ruling with the absence of any other possible interpretation.

Key Terms
النص
الاحتمال
الظهار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the refutation in the passage, what is the correct understanding of a 'clear text' (nas)?
A) A wording that has only one possible meaning.
B) A wording that is explicit in one ruling even if it admits other interpretations in other contexts.
C) A wording that is unqualified (mutlaq).
D) A wording that cannot be restricted by other evidence.

Key Takeaway
A clear text (nas) is defined by its clarity in a specific ruling, not by the absence of any other possible interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar objection against the use of the verse 'So divorce them for their prescribed period' (65:1) as a clear text for the permissibility of divorce at any time, by claiming the wording is ambiguous because it allows different interpretations of 'prescribed period'. Then write the refutation using the principle that nas is explicit in ruling even if ambiguous in other aspects.
?
In a given passage from al-Shirazi's al-Luma' on the definition of nas, identify the mudda'i, mu'tarid, and jawab, and assess who wins according to the rules of jadal.

Definition of Zahir and Its Three Types
Definition and classification of zahir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of zahir and distinguish them based on the source of preponderance.

Core Idea
Zahir is a word with two meanings, one preponderant by convention, custom, or context.

The passage begins by defining 'zahir' as a word that is clear because one of its meanings outweighs the other due to linguistic convention. It then classifies zahir into three types based on what gives the preponderance: linguistic convention, custom, or contextual indication. Understanding this classification is crucial for jadal, because objections often rely on shifting the ground of preponderance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'zahir'. Note that it is defined as a word (lafz) that admits two meanings.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
Genus: word (lafz). Differentia: admits two meanings and is preponderant in one due to 'min hayth al-wad'' (from the aspect of linguistic convention). This definition applies specifically to the first type, but the general concept includes other bases.
The author adds 'min hayth al-wad'' to specify the cause for the first type.
3
Classify the three types
Zahir by wad' (linguistic convention), by 'urf (custom, either legal or linguistic), and by dalala (contextual indication). For each, the source of preponderance differs.
Example: 'salat' is by legal custom, 'asad' for lion is by wad', and a word in a specific verse is by dalala.
4
Connect to later usul terminology
In later works, 'zahir' is often contrasted with 'nass' as being probable. Here, 'zahir by wad'' is clear, but later it becomes a category of probable indication.
This shift is important for understanding the evolution of usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد بالظاهر : اللفظ الذي يحتمل معنيين وهو راجح في أحدهما من حيث الوضع، فلذلك كان متضح الدلالة. والظاهر على ثلاثة أنواع : ظاهر بالوضع، وظاهر بالعرف، وظاهر بالدلالة. أ - المنازعة في الاستدلال بالظاهر من جهة الوضع : ويختص به من المنازعة أمران : أحدهما الحمل على العرف، والثاني أن يحمله المعترض على غير المعنى الذي حمله عليه المستدل في اللغة. 1 - الاعتراض بالحمل على العرف وهو على ضربين: أحدهما أن يحمل على عرف الشرع، والثاني أن يحمل على عرف اللغة. فأما الحمل على عرف الشرع، فمثاله : استدلال ابن القصار وغيره من بعض فقهاء المالكية على أن التسمية ليست بشرط في صحة الذبيحة، بقوله تعالى : وَمَا أَكَلَ السَّبُعُ إِلَّا مَا ذَكَيْتُمْ ...، ووجه الاستدلال أن هذا الذي لم يسمَّ عليه قد ذُكِّيّ، فوجب أن يباح أكله، وقد اعترض عليهم غيرهم من المالكية أيضًا بعدم التسليم بأن من ترك التسمية عامدًا يعتبر مذكيًا، لأن التذكية إذا أطلقت في الشرع فإنما يراد بها الذكاة الشرعية ومتى تعمد الذابح ترك التسمية لم يعتبر مذكيًا شرعًا. والطريق في الجواب على هذا النوع من الاعتراض، بأحد مسلكين : الأول : عدم التسليم بأن لفظة الذكاة لها عرف في الشرع غير ما وضعت له في اللغة وهو مطلق الذبح ومطالبة من يدعي وجود العرف الشرعي بالدليل على ذلك . الثاني : بيان أن الذكاة في الشريعة لا تقتضي التسمية، وإنما تقتضي التطييب.
The meaning of 'zahir' (apparent) is: the wording that admits two meanings and is preponderant in one of them in terms of linguistic convention (wad'), therefore it is clear in its indication (dalala). And the 'zahir' is of three types: apparent by convention (wad'), apparent by custom ('urf), and apparent by indication (dalala). (A) Objection against inference by an apparent text from the aspect of linguistic convention: it specifically involves two matters: one is carrying it to custom ('urf), and the other is that the objector carries it to a meaning other than that which the inferrer carried it to in the language. (1) The objection by carrying to custom, and it is of two types: one is that it be carried to the custom of the Law (shar'), and the second is that it be carried to the custom of the language. As for carrying it to the custom of the Law, its example is: the argument of Ibn al-Qassar and others from among some Maliki jurists that the tasmiya (saying Bismillah) is not a condition for the validity of the slaughtered animal, by His saying, the Exalted: '...and what the beast of prey has eaten except what you have slaughtered...' (Quran 5:3). And the face of the inference is that this [animal] which was not named upon has been slaughtered (tadhkiya), so it is obligatory that its consumption be permitted. And others from among the Malikis objected to them by not conceding that one who intentionally omits the tasmiya is considered as having performed slaughter (mudhakki), because the term 'tadhkiya' when used absolutely in the Law is only intended to mean the lawful (shar'i) method of slaughter; and when the slaughterer intentionally omits the tasmiya, he is not considered a lawful slaughterer in the Law. And the way to answer this type of objection is by one of two approaches: the first is not conceding that the word 'tadhkiya' has a custom in the Law other than what it was placed for in the language, which is absolute slaughter, and demanding from whoever claims the existence of a legal custom to provide evidence for that. The second is to explain that 'tadhkiya' in the Law does not require the tasmiya, but rather requires the purification (tatyib) [of the animal for consumption].
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is from a work on jadal (dialectical method) in usul al-fiqh. It first defines the term 'zahir' (apparent) as a word that has two possible meanings but is preponderant in one of them due to linguistic convention (wad'), making its indication clear. Then it classifies zahir into three types: (1) apparent by wad' (linguistic convention), (2) apparent by 'urf (custom), and (3) apparent by dalala (indication, e.g., contextual clues). The text then turns to the main topic: objections against inference by an apparent text from the aspect of wad'. There are two specific objections: (a) carrying the word to a custom ('urf) instead of its literal wad', and (b) the objector giving the word a different meaning in the language than the one given by the inferrer. The passage elaborates only on the first objection (carrying to 'urf), which itself splits into two: carrying it to the custom of the Law (shar') and carrying it to the custom of language. The example given is famous: Ibn al-Qassar and other Malikis used the Quranic verse '...except what you have slaughtered' to argue that the Bismillah is not a condition for slaughter, because the verse speaks of something that was not named (i.e., an animal killed by a predator but found alive and slaughtered) as 'mudhakka' (slaughtered), so it is permissible to eat it. The objection from other Malikis is that the word 'tadhkiya' in the Law has a specific meaning that includes the intention and the tasmiya, so the verse cannot be taken literally. To rebut this objection, the author offers two approaches: (1) deny that 'tadhkiya' has a separate legal custom different from its linguistic meaning of 'absolute slaughter', and challenge the objector to prove such a custom; (2) argue that even in the Law, 'tadhkiya' does not require tasmiya but only the permissible method of slaughter (i.e., cutting the throat and draining the blood, regardless of whether the name of Allah is mentioned).

Key Terms
الظاهر
العرف
الوضع
الدلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the three types of zahir?
A) Zahir by wad' (linguistic convention)
B) Zahir by 'urf (custom)
C) Zahir by dalala (indication)
D) Zahir by nas (explicit text)

Key Takeaway
Zahir is a word with two meanings, one preponderant due to convention, custom, or context; these three bases define its subtypes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify the type of zahir in the following phrase from a hadith: 'The prayer (al-salāt) is the pillar of religion.' Is 'salāt' preponderant by wad', 'urf (legal custom), or dalala? Explain your reasoning.
?
2. Classify the word 'tadhkiya' in the Quranic verse (5:3) as understood by Ibn al-Qassar (literal) versus the objectors (legal custom). Based on the passage, which type does each side assume?

Objection by Carrying to Custom and Its Rebuttal
First type of objection and its two rebuttals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two subtypes of the 'carrying to custom' objection and reconstruct the two rebuttal approaches from a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Custom can shift meaning, but the respondent can deny the custom or reinterpret it.

After defining zahir, the passage presents the first specific objection: the objector claims that the word should be understood according to custom ('urf) rather than its literal convention. This objection splits into carrying to legal custom or linguistic custom. The example given is a famous Maliki debate on whether tasmiya is required for valid slaughter. The passage then provides two rebuttal strategies: denying the existence of a separate legal custom, or arguing that even under the legal custom, tasmiya is not required. This lesson will guide you through the logical structure of the objection and the two rebuttals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector claims that the word 'tadhkiya' in the Qur'anic verse should be understood according to the custom of the Law (shar'), which includes the requirement of tasmiya. Therefore, the verse does not prove that an animal slaughtered without tasmiya is halal.
This is the first subtype: carrying to legal custom.
2
Identify the respondent's original argument
Ibn al-Qassar argued that the verse calls an animal 'mudhakka' (slaughtered) even though it was not named (since it was a predator's kill), so tasmiya is not a condition.
The respondent relies on the literal meaning (wad') of tadhkiya as absolute slaughter.
3
Analyze the objection type
This is the first subtype: carrying to custom of the Law. The objector asserts that the word has a specific legal custom that overrides the literal convention.
The second subtype (carrying to linguistic custom) would appeal to common language usage.
4
Reconstruct the two rebuttal approaches
First approach: deny that 'tadhkiya' has a separate legal custom and demand proof. Second approach: concede a legal custom but argue it does not require tasmiya, only purification (tatyib).
The first approach shifts the burden of proof; the second engages the custom on its own terms.
5
Evaluate the strategic difference
The first rebuttal is stronger if the objector cannot prove the custom; the second is more concessive but still undermines the objection.
In jadal, the respondent may choose either depending on the evidence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first approach to answer the objection that 'tadhkiya' in the Law has a custom that includes tasmiya?
A) Accept the custom and argue that tasmiya is still not required.
